And bein' busy always keeps me from grievin' too much\r\nabout you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStrange is woman! She would rather have heard some other last remark\r\nthan this.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, very well!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI'll not miss you either.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe smiled at her. \xe2\x80\x9cI doubt if yu' can help missin' me,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked. And\r\nhe was gone at once, galloping on his Monte horse.\r\n\r\nWhich of the two won a victory this day?\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXIII. THE GAME AND THE NATION--ACT FIRST\r\n\r\n\r\nThere can be no doubt of this: All America is divided into two\r\nclasses,--the quality and the equality.\r\n\r\nThe latter will always recognize the former when mistaken for it. Both\r\nwill be with us until our women bear nothing but kings.\r\n\r\nIt was through the Declaration of Independence that we Americans\r\nacknowledged the ETERNAL INEQUALITY of man. For by it we abolished a\r\ncut-and-dried aristocracy. We had seen little men artificially held up\r\nin high places, and great men artificially held down in low places, and\r\nour own justice-loving hearts abhorred this violence to human nature.\r\nTherefore, we decreed that every man should thenceforth have equal\r\nliberty to find his own level. By this very decree we acknowledged and\r\ngave freedom to true aristocracy, saying, \xe2\x80\x9cLet the best man win, whoever\r\nhe is.\xe2\x80\x9d Let the best man win! That is America's word. That is true\r\ndemocracy. And true democracy and true aristocracy are one and the same\r\nthing. If anybody cannot see this, so much the worse for his eyesight.\r\n\r\nThe above reflections occurred to me before reaching Billings, Montana,\r\nsome three weeks after I had unexpectedly met the Virginian at Omaha,\r\nNebraska. I had not known of that trust given to him by Judge Henry,\r\nwhich was taking him East. I was looking to ride with him before long\r\namong the clean hills of Sunk Creek. I supposed he was there. But I came\r\nupon him one morning in Colonel Cyrus Jones's eating palace.\r\n\r\nDid you know the palace? It stood in Omaha, near the trains, and it was\r\nten years old (which is middle-aged in Omaha) when I first saw it. It\r\nwas a shell of wood, painted with golden emblems,--the steamboat, the\r\neagle, the Yosemite,--and a live bear ate gratuities at its entrance.\r\nWeather permitting, it opened upon the world as a stage upon the\r\naudience. You sat in Omaha's whole sight and dined, while Omaha's dust\r\ncame and settled upon the refreshments. It is gone the way of the Indian\r\nand the buffalo, for the West is growing old. You should have seen the\r\npalace and sat there. In front of you passed rainbows of men,--Chinese,\r\nIndian chiefs, Africans, General Miles, younger sons, Austrian nobility,\r\nwide females in pink. Our continent drained prismatically through Omaha\r\nonce.\r\n\r\nSo I was passing that way also, walking for the sake of ventilation from\r\na sleeping-car toward a bath, when the language of Colonel Cyrus Jones\r\ncame out to me. The actual colonel I had never seen before. He stood\r\nat the rear of his palace in gray flowery mustaches and a Confederate\r\nuniform, telling the wishes of his guests to the cook through a hole.\r\nYou always bought meal tickets at once, else you became unwelcome.\r\nGuests here had foibles at times, and a rapid exit was too easy.\r\nTherefore I bought a ticket. It was spring and summer since I had heard\r\nanything like the colonel. The Missouri had not yet flowed into New York\r\ndialect freely, and his vocabulary met me like the breeze of the plains.\r\nSo I went in to be fanned by it, and there sat the Virginian at a table,\r\nalone.\r\n\r\nHis greeting was up to the code of indifference proper on the plains;\r\nbut he presently remarked, \xe2\x80\x9cI'm right glad to see somebody,\xe2\x80\x9d which was a\r\ngood deal to say. \xe2\x80\x9cThem that comes hyeh,\xe2\x80\x9d he observed next, \xe2\x80\x9cdon't eat.\r\nThey feed.\xe2\x80\x9d And he considered the guests with a sombre attention.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cD' yu' reckon they find joyful digestion in this swallo'-an'-get-out\r\ntrough?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you doing here, then?\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, pshaw! When yu' can't have what you choose, yu' just choose what\r\nyou have.\xe2\x80\x9d And he took the bill-of-fare. I began to know that he had\r\nsomething on his mind, so I did not trouble him further.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile he sat studying the bill-of-fare.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEver heard o' them?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired, shoving me the spotted document.\r\n\r\nMost improbable dishes were there,--salmis, canapes, supremes,--all\r\nperfectly spelt and absolutely transparent. It was the old trick of\r\ncopying some metropolitan menu to catch travellers of the third and last\r\ndimension of innocence; and whenever this is done the food is of the\r\nthird and last dimension of awfulness, which the cow-puncher knew as\r\nwell as anybody.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo they keep that up here still,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what about them?\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated. His finger was at a special item,\r\nFROGS' LEGS A LA DELMONICO. \xe2\x80\x9cAre they true anywheres?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked. And I\r\ntold him, certainly. I also explained to him about Delmonico of New York\r\nand about Augustin of Philadelphia.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere's not a little bit o' use in lyin' to me this mawnin',\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\nwith his engaging smile. \xe2\x80\x9cI ain't goin' to awdeh anything's laigs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I'll see how he gets out of it,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, remembering the odd\r\nTexas legend. (The traveller read the bill-of-fare, you know, and called\r\nfor a vol-au-vent. And the proprietor looked at the traveller, and\r\nrunning a pistol into his ear, observed, \xe2\x80\x9cYou'll take hash.\xe2\x80\x9d) I was\r\nthinking of this and wondering what would happen to me. So I took the\r\nstep.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWants frogs' legs, does he?\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Colonel Cyrus Jones. He fixed\r\nhis eye upon me, and it narrowed to a slit. \xe2\x80\x9cToo many brain workers\r\nbreakfasting before yu' came in, professor,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cMissionary ate\r\nthe last leg off me just now. Brown the wheat!\xe2\x80\x9d he commanded, through\r\nthe hole to the cook, for some one had ordered hot cakes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll have fried aiggs,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cCooked both sides.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhite wings!\xe2\x80\x9d sang the colonel through the hole. \xe2\x80\x9cLet 'em fly up and\r\ndown.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCoffee an' no milk,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDraw one in the dark!\xe2\x80\x9d the colonel roared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd beefsteak, rare.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne slaughter in the pan, and let the blood drip!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like a glass of water, please,\xe2\x80\x9d said I. The colonel threw me a\r\nlook of pity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne Missouri and ice for the professor!\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat fello's a right live man,\xe2\x80\x9d commented the Virginian. But he seemed\r\nthoughtful. Presently he inquired, \xe2\x80\x9cYu' say he was a foreigner, an'\r\nlearned fancy cookin' to New Yawk?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat was this cow-puncher's way. Scarcely ever would he let drop a thing\r\nnew to him until he had got from you your whole information about it.\r\nSo I told him the history of Lorenzo Delmonico and his pioneer work, as\r\nmuch as I knew, and the Southerner listened intently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMighty inter-estin',\xe2\x80\x9d he said--\xe2\x80\x9cmighty. He could just take little\r\nold o'rn'ry frawgs, and dandy 'em up to suit the bloods. Mighty\r\ninter-estin'. I expaict, though, his cookin' would give an outraiged\r\nstomach to a plain-raised man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you want to follow it up,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, by way of a sudden experiment,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Molly Wood might have some book about French dishes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the Virginian did not turn a hair. \xe2\x80\x9cI reckon she wouldn't,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nanswered. \xe2\x80\x9cShe was raised in Vermont. They don't bother overly about\r\ntheir eatin' up in Vermont. Hyeh's what Miss Wood recommended the las'\r\ntime I was seein' her,\xe2\x80\x9d the cow-puncher added, bringing Kenilworth from\r\nhis pocket. \xe2\x80\x9cRight fine story. That Queen Elizabeth must have cert'nly\r\nbeen a competent woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was,\xe2\x80\x9d said I. But talk came to an end here. A dusty crew, most\r\nevidently from the plains, now entered and drifted to a table; and each\r\nman of them gave the Virginian about a quarter of a slouchy nod. His\r\ngreeting to them was very serene. Only, Kenilworth went back into his\r\npocket, and he breakfasted in silence. Among those who had greeted him I\r\nnow recognized a face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, that's the man you played cards with at Medicine Bow!\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Trampas. He's got a job at the ranch now.\xe2\x80\x9d The Virginian said no\r\nmore, but went on with his breakfast.\r\n\r\nHis appearance was changed. Aged I would scarcely say, for this\r\nwould seem as if he did not look young. But I think that the boy was\r\naltogether gone from his face--the boy whose freak with Steve had turned\r\nMedicine Bow upside down, whose other freak with the babies had outraged\r\nBear Creek, the boy who had loved to jingle his spurs. But manhood had\r\nonly trained, not broken, his youth. It was all there, only obedient to\r\nthe rein and curb.\r\n\r\nPresently we went together to the railway yard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Judge is doing a right smart o' business this year,\xe2\x80\x9d he began, very\r\ncasually indeed, so that I knew this was important. Besides bells and\r\ncoal smoke, the smell and crowded sounds of cattle rose in the air\r\naround us. \xe2\x80\x9cHyeh's our first gather o' beeves on the ranch,\xe2\x80\x9d continued\r\nthe Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cThe whole lot's shipped through to Chicago in two\r\nsections over the Burlington. The Judge is fighting the Elkhorn road.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n We passed slowly along the two trains,--twenty cars, each car packed\r\nwith huddled, round-eyed, gazing steers. He examined to see if any\r\nanimals were down. \xe2\x80\x9cThey ain't ate or drank anything to speak of,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid, while the terrified brutes stared at us through their slats. \xe2\x80\x9cNot\r\nsince they struck the railroad they've not drank. Yu' might suppose\r\nthey know somehow what they're travellin' to Chicago for.\xe2\x80\x9d And casually,\r\nalways casually, he told me the rest. Judge Henry could not spare his\r\nforeman away from the second gather of beeves. Therefore these two\r\nten-car trains with their double crew of cow-boys had been given to the\r\nVirginian's charge. After Chicago, he was to return by St. Paul over\r\nthe Northern Pacific; for the Judge had wished him to see certain of the\r\nroad's directors and explain to them persuasively how good a thing it\r\nwould be for them to allow especially cheap rates to the Sunk Creek\r\noutfit henceforth. This was all the Virginian told me; and it contained\r\nthe whole matter, to be sure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you're acting foreman,\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, somebody has to have the say, I reckon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd of course you hated the promotion?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't know about promotion,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied. \xe2\x80\x9cThe boys have been used\r\nto seein' me one of themselves. Why don't you come along with us far as\r\nPlattsmouth?\xe2\x80\x9d Thus he shifted the subject from himself, and called to my\r\nnotice the locomotives backing up to his cars, and reminded me that from\r\nPlattsmouth I had the choice of two trains returning. But he could not\r\nhide or belittle this confidence of his employer in him. It was the care\r\nof several thousand perishable dollars and the control of men. It was a\r\ncompliment. There were more steers than men to be responsible for; but\r\nnone of the steers had been suddenly picked from the herd and set above\r\nhis fellows. Moreover, Chicago finished up the steers; but the new-made\r\ndeputy foreman had then to lead his six highly unoccupied brethren away\r\nfrom towns, and back in peace to the ranch, or disappoint the Judge, who\r\nneeded their services. These things sometimes go wrong in a land where\r\nthey say you are all born equal; and that quarter of a nod in Colonel\r\nCyrus Jones's eating palace held more equality than any whole nod you\r\ncould see. But the Virginian did not see it, there being a time for all\r\nthings.\r\n\r\nWe trundled down the flopping, heavy-eddied Missouri to Plattsmouth,\r\nand there they backed us on to a siding, the Christian Endeavor being\r\nexpected to pass that way. And while the equality absorbed themselves in\r\na deep but harmless game of poker by the side of the railway line,\r\nthe Virginian and I sat on the top of a car, contemplating the sandy\r\nshallows of the Platte.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think you'd take a hand,\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoker? With them kittens?\xe2\x80\x9d One flash of the inner man lightened in his\r\neyes and died away, and he finished with his gentle drawl, \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I play,\r\nI want it to be interestin'.\xe2\x80\x9d He took out Sir Walter's Kenilworth once\r\nmore, and turned the volume over and over slowly, without opening it.\r\nYou cannot tell if in spirit he wandered on Bear Creek with the girl\r\nwhose book it was. The spirit will go one road, and the thought another,\r\nand the body its own way sometimes. \xe2\x80\x9cQueen Elizabeth would have played a\r\nmighty pow'ful game,\xe2\x80\x9d was his next remark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoker?\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, seh. Do you expaict Europe has got any queen equal to her at\r\npresent?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI doubted it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVictoria'd get pretty nigh slain sliding chips out agaynst Elizabeth.\r\nOnly mos' prob'ly Victoria she'd insist on a half-cent limit. You have\r\nread this hyeh Kenilworth? Well, deal Elizabeth ace high, an' she could\r\nscare Robert Dudley with a full house plumb out o' the bettin'.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI said that I believed she unquestionably could.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, \xe2\x80\x9cif Essex's play got next her too near, I\r\nreckon she'd have stacked the cyards. Say, d' yu' remember Shakespeare's\r\nfat man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFalstaff? Oh, yes, indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAin't that grand? Why, he makes men talk the way they do in life.\r\nI reckon he couldn't get printed to-day. It's a right down shame\r\nShakespeare couldn't know about poker. He'd have had Falstaff playing\r\nall day at that Tearsheet outfit. And the Prince would have beat him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Prince had the brains,\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBrains?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, didn't he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI neveh thought to notice. Like as not he did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Falstaff didn't, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, seh! Falstaff could have played whist.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you know what you're talking about; I don't,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, for he\r\nwas drawling again.\r\n\r\nThe cow-puncher's eye rested a moment amiably upon me. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can play\r\nwhist with your brains,\xe2\x80\x9d he mused,--\xe2\x80\x9cbrains and cyards. Now cyards are\r\nonly one o' the manifestations of poker in this hyeh world. One o' the\r\nshapes yu fool with it in when the day's work is oveh. If a man is built\r\nlike that Prince boy was built (and it's away down deep beyond brains),\r\nhe'll play winnin' poker with whatever hand he's holdin' when the\r\ntrouble begins. Maybe it will be a mean, triflin' army, or an empty\r\nsix-shooter, or a lame hawss, or maybe just nothin' but his natural\r\ncountenance. 'Most any old thing will do for a fello' like that Prince\r\nboy to play poker with.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I'd be grateful for your definition of poker,\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\r\nAgain the Virginian looked me over amiably. \xe2\x80\x9cYou put up a mighty pretty\r\ngame o' whist yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked. \xe2\x80\x9cDon't that give you the contented\r\nspirit?\xe2\x80\x9d And before I had any reply to this, the Christian Endeavor\r\nbegan to come over the bridge. Three instalments crossed the Missouri\r\nfrom Pacific Junction, bound for Pike's Peak, every car swathed in\r\nbright bunting, and at each window a Christian with a handkerchief,\r\njoyously shrieking. Then the cattle trains got the open signal, and I\r\njumped off. \xe2\x80\x9cTell the Judge the steers was all right this far,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nVirginian.\r\n\r\nThat was the last of the deputy foreman for a while.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXIV. BETWEEN THE ACTS\r\n\r\n\r\nMy road to Sunk Creek lay in no straight line. By rail I diverged\r\nnorthwest to Fort Meade, and thence, after some stay with the kind\r\nmilitary people, I made my way on a horse. Up here in the Black Hills it\r\nsluiced rain most intolerably. The horse and I enjoyed the country and\r\nourselves but little; and when finally I changed from the saddle into a\r\nstagecoach, I caught a thankful expression upon the animal's face, and\r\nreturned the same.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSix legs inside this jerky to-night?\xe2\x80\x9d said somebody, as I climbed\r\nthe wheel. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, we'll give thanks for not havin' eight,\xe2\x80\x9d he added\r\ncheerfully. \xe2\x80\x9cClamp your mind on to that, Shorty.\xe2\x80\x9d And he slapped the\r\nshoulder of his neighbor. Naturally I took these two for old companions.\r\nBut we were all total strangers. They told me of the new gold excitement\r\nat Rawhide, and supposed it would bring up the Northern Pacific; and\r\nwhen I explained the millions owed to this road's German bondholders,\r\nthey were of opinion that a German would strike it richer at Rawhide. We\r\nspoke of all sorts of things, and in our silence I gloated on the autumn\r\nholiday promised me by Judge Henry. His last letter had said that an\r\noutfit would be starting for his ranch from Billings on the seventh, and\r\nhe would have a horse for me. This was the fifth. So we six legs in the\r\njerky travelled harmoniously on over the rain-gutted road, getting no\r\ndeeper knowledge of each other than what our outsides might imply.\r\n\r\nNot that we concealed anything. The man who had slapped Shorty\r\nintroduced himself early. \xe2\x80\x9cScipio le Moyne, from Gallipolice, Ohio,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cThe eldest of us always gets called Scipio. It's French. But\r\nus folks have been white for a hundred years.\xe2\x80\x9d He was limber and\r\nlight-muscled, and fell skilfully about, evading bruises when the\r\njerky reeled or rose on end. He had a strange, long, jocular nose, very\r\nwary-looking, and a bleached blue eye. Cattle was his business, as a\r\nrule, but of late he had been \xe2\x80\x9clooking around some,\xe2\x80\x9d and Rawhide seemed\r\nmuch on his brain. Shorty struck me as \xe2\x80\x9clooking around\xe2\x80\x9d also. He was\r\nquite short, indeed, and the jerky hurt him almost every time. He was\r\nlight-haired and mild. Think of a yellow dog that is lost, and fancies\r\neach newcomer in sight is going to turn out his master, and you will\r\nhave Shorty.\r\n\r\nIt was the Northern Pacific that surprised us into intimacy. We were\r\nnearing Medora. We had made a last arrangement of our legs. I lay\r\nstretched in silence, placid in the knowledge it was soon to end. So\r\nI drowsed. I felt something sudden, and, waking, saw Scipio passing\r\nthrough the air. As Shorty next shot from the jerky, I beheld smoke and\r\nthe locomotive. The Northern Pacific had changed its schedule. A valise\r\nis a poor companion for catching a train with. There was rutted sand\r\nand lumpy, knee-high grease wood in our short cut. A piece of stray wire\r\nsprang from some hole and hung caracoling about my ankle. Tin cans spun\r\nfrom my stride. But we made a conspicuous race. Two of us waved hats,\r\nand there was no moment that some one of us was not screeching. It meant\r\ntwenty-four hours to us.\r\n\r\nPerhaps we failed to catch the train's attention, though the theory\r\nseems monstrous. As it moved off in our faces, smooth and easy and\r\ninsulting, Scipio dropped instantly to a walk, and we two others\r\noutstripped him and came desperately to the empty track. There went the\r\ntrain. Even still its puffs were the separated puffs of starting, that\r\nbitten-off, snorty kind, and sweat and our true natures broke freely\r\nforth.\r\n\r\nI kicked my valise, and then sat on it, dumb.\r\n\r\nShorty yielded himself up aloud. All his humble secrets came out of\r\nhim. He walked aimlessly round, lamenting. He had lost his job, and he\r\nmentioned the ranch. He had played cards, and he mentioned the man. He\r\nhad sold his horse and saddle to catch a friend on this train, and he\r\nmentioned what the friend had been going to do for him. He told a string\r\nof griefs and names to the air, as if the air knew.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile Scipio arrived with extreme leisure at the rails. He stuck\r\nhis hands into his pockets and his head out at the very small train.\r\nHis bleached blue eyes shut to slits as he watched the rear car in its\r\nsmoke-blur ooze away westward among the mounded bluffs. \xe2\x80\x9cLucky it's out\r\nof range,\xe2\x80\x9d I thought. But now Scipio spoke to it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you seem to think you've left me behind,\xe2\x80\x9d he began easily, in\r\nfawning tones. \xe2\x80\x9cYou're too much of a kid to have such thoughts. Age\r\nsome.\xe2\x80\x9d His next remark grew less wheedling. \xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn't be a bit proud\r\nto meet yu'. Why, if I was seen travellin' with yu', I'd have to explain\r\nit to my friends! Think you've got me left, do yu'? Just because yu'\r\nride through this country on a rail, do yu' claim yu' can find your way\r\naround? I could take yu' out ten yards in the brush and lose yu' in\r\nten seconds, you spangle-roofed hobo! Leave ME behind? you recent\r\nblanket-mortgage yearlin'! You plush-lined, nickel-plated, whistlin'\r\nwash room, d' yu' figure I can't go east just as soon as west? Or I'll\r\nstay right here if it suits me, yu' dude-inhabited hot-box! Why, yu'\r\ncoon-bossed face-towel--\xe2\x80\x9d But from here he rose in flights of novelty\r\nthat appalled and held me spellbound, and which are not for me to say\r\nto you. Then he came down easily again, and finished with expressions of\r\nsympathy for it because it could never have known a mother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you expaict it could show a male parent offhand?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired a slow\r\nvoice behind us. I jumped round, and there was the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMale parent!\xe2\x80\x9d scoffed the prompt Scipio. \xe2\x80\x9cAin't you heard about THEM\r\nyet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThem? Was there two?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo? The blamed thing was sired by a whole doggone Dutch syndicate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, the piebald son of a gun!\xe2\x80\x9d responded the Virginian, sweetly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ngot them steers through all right,\xe2\x80\x9d he added to me. \xe2\x80\x9cSorry to see yu'\r\nget so out o' breath afteh the train. Is your valise sufferin' any?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho's he?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Scipio, curiously, turning to me.\r\n\r\nThe Southerner sat with a newspaper on the rear platform of a caboose.\r\nThe caboose stood hitched behind a mile or so of freight train, and\r\nthe train was headed west. So here was the deputy foreman, his steers\r\ndelivered in Chicago, his men (I could hear them) safe in the caboose,\r\nhis paper in his lap, and his legs dangling at ease over the railing. He\r\nwore the look of a man for whom things are going smooth. And for me the\r\nway to Billings was smooth now, also.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho's he?\xe2\x80\x9d Scipio repeated.\r\n\r\nBut from inside the caboose loud laughter and noise broke on us. Some\r\none was reciting \xe2\x80\x9cAnd it's my night to howl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe'll all howl when we get to Rawhide,\xe2\x80\x9d said some other one; and they\r\nhowled now.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese hyeh steam cyars,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian to Scipio, \xe2\x80\x9cmake a man's\r\nlanguage mighty nigh as speedy as his travel.\xe2\x80\x9d Of Shorty he took no\r\nnotice whatever--no more than of the manifestations in the caboose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo yu' heard me speakin' to the express,\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I guess,\r\nsometimes I--See here,\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, for the Virginian was gravely\r\nconsidering him, \xe2\x80\x9cI may have talked some, but I walked a whole lot. You\r\ndidn't catch ME squandering no speed. Soon as--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI noticed,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, \xe2\x80\x9cthinkin' came quicker to yu' than\r\nrunnin'.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was glad I was not Shorty, to have my measure taken merely by my\r\nway of missing a train. And of course I was sorry that I had kicked my\r\nvalise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I could tell yu'd been enjoyin' us!\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio. \xe2\x80\x9cObservin'\r\nsomebody else's scrape always kind o' rests me too. Maybe you're a\r\nphilosopher, but maybe there's a pair of us drawd in this deal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nApproval now grew plain upon the face of the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cBy your laigs,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said he, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are used to the saddle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'd be called used to it, I expect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy your hands,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Southerner, again, \xe2\x80\x9cyou ain't roped many\r\nsteers lately. Been cookin' or something?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Scipio, \xe2\x80\x9ctell my future some now. Draw a conclusion from\r\nmy mouth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm right distressed,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the gentle Southerner, \xe2\x80\x9cwe've not a\r\ndrop in the outfit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, drink with me uptown!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Scipio. \xe2\x80\x9cI'm pleased to death with\r\nyu'.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Virginian glanced where the saloons stood just behind the station,\r\nand shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it ain't a bit far to whiskey from here!\xe2\x80\x9d urged the other,\r\nplaintively. \xe2\x80\x9cStep down, now. Scipio le Moyne's my name. Yes, you're\r\nlookin' for my brass ear-rings. But there ain't no ear-rings on me. I've\r\nbeen white for a hundred years. Step down. I've a forty-dollar thirst.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou're certainly white,\xe2\x80\x9d began the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cBut--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere the caboose resumed:\r\n\r\n     \xe2\x80\x9cI'm wild, and woolly, and full of peas;\r\n      I'm hard to curry above the knees;\r\n      I'm a she-wolf from Bitter Creek, and\r\n      It's my night to ho-o-wl--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd as they howled and stamped, the wheels of the caboose began to turn\r\ngently and to murmur.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian rose suddenly. \xe2\x80\x9cWill yu' save that thirst and take a\r\nforty-dollar job?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMissin' trains, profanity, or what?\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll tell yu' soon as I'm sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this Scipio looked hard at the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you're talkin'\r\nbusiness!\xe2\x80\x9d said he, and leaped on the caboose, where I was already. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nWAS thinkin' of Rawhide,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I ain't any more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, good luck!\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty, on the track behind us.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, say!\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio, \xe2\x80\x9che wanted to go on that train, just like me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet on,\xe2\x80\x9d called the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cBut as to getting a job, he ain't just\r\nlike you.\xe2\x80\x9d So Shorty came, like a lost dog when you whistle to him.\r\n\r\nOur wheels clucked over the main-line switch. A train-hand threw it shut\r\nafter us, jumped aboard, and returned forward over the roofs. Inside the\r\ncaboose they had reached the third howling of the she-wolf.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFriends of yourn?\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy outfit,\xe2\x80\x9d drawled the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo yu' always travel outside?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Scipio.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's lonesome in there,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the deputy foreman. And here one of\r\nthem came out, slamming the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHell!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, at sight of the distant town. Then, truculently, to the\r\nVirginian, \xe2\x80\x9cI told you I was going to get a bottle here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave your bottle, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said the deputy foreman, and kicked him off\r\ninto Dakota. (It was not North Dakota yet; they had not divided it.)\r\nThe Virginian had aimed his pistol at about the same time with his\r\nboot. Therefore the man sat in Dakota quietly, watching us go away into\r\nMontana, and offering no objections. Just before he became too small to\r\nmake out, we saw him rise and remove himself back toward the saloons.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXV. THE GAME AND THE NATION--ACT SECOND\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is the only step I have had to take this whole trip,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nVirginian. He holstered his pistol with a jerk. \xe2\x80\x9cI have been fearing\r\nhe would force it on me.\xe2\x80\x9d And he looked at empty, receding Dakota with\r\ndisgust. \xe2\x80\x9cSo nyeh back home!\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKnown your friend long?\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Scipio to me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFairly,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered.\r\n\r\nScipio's bleached eyes brightened with admiration as he considered the\r\nSoutherner's back. \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d he stated judicially, \xe2\x80\x9cstart awful early when\r\nyu' go to fool with him, or he'll make you feel unpunctual.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI expaict I've had them almost all of three thousand miles,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nVirginian, tilting his head toward the noise in the caboose. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I've\r\nstrove to deliver them back as I received them. The whole lot. And I\r\nwould have. But he has spoiled my hopes.\xe2\x80\x9d The deputy foreman looked\r\nagain at Dakota. \xe2\x80\x9cIt's a disappointment,\xe2\x80\x9d he added. \xe2\x80\x9cYou may know what I\r\nmean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI had known a little, but not to the very deep, of the man's pride and\r\npurpose in this trust. Scipio gave him sympathy. \xe2\x80\x9cThere must be quite a\r\nbalance of 'em left with yu' yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio, cheeringly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had the boys plumb contented,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued the deputy foreman, hurt\r\ninto open talk of himself. \xe2\x80\x9cAway along as far as Saynt Paul I had them\r\nreconciled to my authority. Then this news about gold had to strike us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd they're a-dreamin' nuggets and Parisian bowleyvards,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested\r\nScipio.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian smiled gratefully at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFortune is shinin' bright and blindin' to their delicate young eyes,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n he said, regaining his usual self.\r\n\r\nWe all listened a moment to the rejoicings within.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEnergetic, ain't they?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Southerner. \xe2\x80\x9cBut none of 'em was\r\nwhelped savage enough to sing himself bloodthirsty. And though they're\r\nstrainin' mighty earnest not to be tame, they're goin' back to Sunk\r\nCreek with me accordin' to the Judge's awders. Never a calf of them will\r\ndesert to Rawhide, for all their dangerousness; nor I ain't goin' to\r\nhave any fuss over it. Only one is left now that don't sing. Maybe I\r\nwill have to make some arrangements about him. The man I have parted\r\nwith,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with another glance at Dakota, \xe2\x80\x9cwas our cook, and I will\r\nask yu' to replace him, Colonel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nScipio gaped wide. \xe2\x80\x9cColonel! Say!\xe2\x80\x9d He stared at the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cDid I\r\nmeet yu' at the palace?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot exackly meet,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Southerner. \xe2\x80\x9cI was present one mawnin'\r\nlas' month when this gentleman awdehed frawgs' laigs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSakes and saints, but that was a mean position!\xe2\x80\x9d burst out Scipio. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nhad to tell all comers anything all day. Stand up and jump language hot\r\noff my brain at 'em. And the pay don't near compensate for the drain on\r\nthe system. I don't care how good a man is, you let him keep a-tappin'\r\nhis presence of mind right along, without takin' a lay-off, and you'll\r\nhave him sick. Yes, sir. You'll hit his nerves. So I told them they\r\ncould hire some fresh man, for I was goin' back to punch cattle or fight\r\nIndians, or take a rest somehow, for I didn't propose to get jaded,\r\nand me only twenty-five years old. There ain't no regular Colonel\r\nCyrus Jones any more, yu' know. He met a Cheyenne telegraph pole in\r\nseventy-four, and was buried. But his palace was doin' big business, and\r\nhe had been a kind of attraction, and so they always keep a live bear\r\noutside, and some poor fello', fixed up like the Colonel used to be,\r\ninside. And it's a turruble mean position. Course I'll cook for yu'.\r\nYu've a dandy memory for faces!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wasn't right convinced till I kicked him off and you gave that shut\r\nto your eyes again,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian.\r\n\r\nOnce more the door opened. A man with slim black eyebrows, slim black\r\nmustache, and a black shirt tied with a white handkerchief was looking\r\nsteadily from one to the other of us.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood day!\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked generally and without enthusiasm; and to the\r\nVirginian, \xe2\x80\x9cWhere's Schoffner?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI expaict he'll have got his bottle by now, Trampas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTrampas looked from one to the other of us again. \xe2\x80\x9cDidn't he say he was\r\ncoming back?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe reminded me he was going for a bottle, and afteh that he didn't wait\r\nto say a thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTrampas looked at the platform and the railing and the steps. \xe2\x80\x9cHe told\r\nme he was coming back,\xe2\x80\x9d he insisted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't reckon he has come, not without he clumb up ahaid somewhere.\r\nAn' I mus' say, when he got off he didn't look like a man does when he\r\nhas the intention o' returnin'.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this Scipio coughed, and pared his nails attentively. We had already\r\nbeen avoiding each other's eye. Shorty did not count. Since he got\r\naboard, his meek seat had been the bottom step.\r\n\r\nThe thoughts of Trampas seemed to be in difficulty. \xe2\x80\x9cHow long's this\r\ntrain been started?\xe2\x80\x9d he demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis hyeh train?\xe2\x80\x9d The Virginian consulted his watch. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it's been\r\nfanning it a right smart little while,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, laying no stress upon\r\nhis indolent syllables.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuh!\xe2\x80\x9d went Trampas. He gave the rest of us a final unlovely scrutiny.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to have become a passenger train,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. And he returned\r\nabruptly inside the caboose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs he the member who don't sing?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Scipio.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's the specimen,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Southerner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe don't seem musical in the face,\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPshaw!\xe2\x80\x9d returned the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you surely ain't the man to mind\r\nugly mugs when they're hollow!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe noise inside had dropped quickly to stillness. You could scarcely\r\ncatch the sound of talk. Our caboose was clicking comfortably westward,\r\nrail after rail, mile upon mile, while night was beginning to rise from\r\nearth into the clouded sky.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if they have sent a search party forward to hunt Schoffner?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cI think I'll maybe join their meeting.\xe2\x80\x9d He opened\r\nthe door upon them. \xe2\x80\x9cKind o' dark hyeh, ain't it?\xe2\x80\x9d said he. And lighting\r\nthe lantern, he shut us out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do yu' think?\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio to me. \xe2\x80\x9cWill he take them to Sunk\r\nCreek?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe evidently thinks he will,\xe2\x80\x9d said I. \xe2\x80\x9cHe says he will, and he has the\r\ncourage of his convictions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat ain't near enough courage to have!\xe2\x80\x9d Scipio exclaimed.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere's times in life when a man has got to have courage WITHOUT\r\nconvictions--WITHOUT them--or he is no good. Now your friend is that\r\ndeep constitooted that you don't know and I don't know what he's\r\nthinkin' about all this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf there's to be any gun-play,\xe2\x80\x9d put in the excellent Shorty, \xe2\x80\x9cI'll\r\nstand in with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, go to bed with your gun-play!\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Scipio, entirely\r\ngood-humored. \xe2\x80\x9cIs the Judge paying for a carload of dead punchers to\r\ngather his beef for him? And this ain't a proposition worth a man's\r\ngettin' hurt for himself, anyway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's so,\xe2\x80\x9d Shorty assented.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d speculated Scipio, as the night drew deeper round us and the\r\ncaboose click-clucked and click-clucked over the rail joints; \xe2\x80\x9che's\r\nwaitin' for somebody else to open this pot. I'll bet he don't know but\r\none thing now, and that's that nobody else shall know he don't know\r\nanything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nScipio had delivered himself. He lighted a cigarette, and no more wisdom\r\ncame from him. The night was established. The rolling bad-lands sank\r\naway in it. A train-hand had arrived over the roof, and hanging the red\r\nlights out behind, left us again without remark or symptom of curiosity.\r\nThe train-hands seemed interested in their own society and lived in\r\ntheir own caboose. A chill wind with wet in it came blowing from the\r\ninvisible draws, and brought the feel of the distant mountains.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's Montana!\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio, snuffing. \xe2\x80\x9cI am glad to have it inside my\r\nlungs again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAin't yu' getting cool out there?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian's voice. \xe2\x80\x9cPlenty\r\nroom inside.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPerhaps he had expected us to follow him; or perhaps he had meant us\r\nto delay long enough not to seem like a reenforcement. \xe2\x80\x9cThese gentlemen\r\nmissed the express at Medora,\xe2\x80\x9d he observed to his men, simply.\r\n\r\nWhat they took us for upon our entrance I cannot say, or what they\r\nbelieved. The atmosphere of the caboose was charged with voiceless\r\ncurrents of thought. By way of a friendly beginning to the three hundred\r\nmiles of caboose we were now to share so intimately, I recalled myself\r\nto them. I trusted no more of the Christian Endeavor had delayed them.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am so lucky to have caught you again,\xe2\x80\x9d I finished. \xe2\x80\x9cI was afraid my\r\nlast chance of reaching the Judge's had gone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThus I said a number of things designed to be agreeable, but they met my\r\nsmall talk with the smallest talk you can have. \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d for instance, and\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPretty well, I guess,\xe2\x80\x9d and grave strikings of matches and thoughtful\r\nlooks at the floor. I suppose we had made twenty miles to the\r\nimperturbable clicking of the caboose when one at length asked his\r\nneighbor had he ever seen New York.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said the other. \xe2\x80\x9cFlooded with dudes, ain't it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSwimmin',\xe2\x80\x9d said the first.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLeakin', too,\xe2\x80\x9d said a third.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my gracious!\xe2\x80\x9d said a fourth, and beat his knee in private\r\ndelight. None of them ever looked at me. For some reason I felt\r\nexceedingly ill at ease.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood clothes in New York,\xe2\x80\x9d said the third.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRich food,\xe2\x80\x9d said the first.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFresh eggs, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said the third.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my gracious!\xe2\x80\x9d said the fourth, beating his knee.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d observed the Virginian, unexpectedly; \xe2\x80\x9cthey tell me that\r\naiggs there ain't liable to be so rotten as yu'll strike 'em in this\r\ncountry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNone of them had a reply for this, and New York was abandoned. For some\r\nreason I felt much better.\r\n\r\nIt was a new line they adopted next, led off by Trampas.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoing to the excitement?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired, selecting Shorty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcitement?\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty, looking up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoing to Rawhide?\xe2\x80\x9d Trampas repeated. And all watched Shorty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I'm all adrift missin' that express,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe I can give you employment,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cI am taking\r\nan outfit across the basin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou'll find most folks going to Rawhide, if you're looking for\r\ncompany,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued Trampas, fishing for a recruit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow about Rawhide, anyway?\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio, skillfully deflecting this\r\nmissionary work. \xe2\x80\x9cAre they taking much mineral out? Have yu' seen any of\r\nthe rock?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRock?\xe2\x80\x9d broke in the enthusiast who had beaten his knee. \xe2\x80\x9cThere!\xe2\x80\x9d And he\r\nbrought some from his pocket.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou're always showing your rock,\xe2\x80\x9d said Trampas, sulkily; for Scipio now\r\nheld the conversation, and Shorty returned safely to his dozing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cH'm!\xe2\x80\x9d went Scipio at the rock. He turned it back and forth in his hand,\r\nlooking it over; he chucked and caught it slightingly in the air, and\r\nhanded it back. \xe2\x80\x9cPorphyry, I see.\xe2\x80\x9d That was his only word about it. He\r\nsaid it cheerily. He left no room for discussion. You could not damn\r\na thing worse. \xe2\x80\x9cEver been in Santa Rita?\xe2\x80\x9d pursued Scipio, while the\r\nenthusiast slowly pushed his rock back into his pocket. \xe2\x80\x9cThat's down in\r\nNew Mexico. Ever been to Globe, Arizona?\xe2\x80\x9d And Scipio talked away about\r\nthe mines he had known. There was no getting at Shorty any more that\r\nevening. Trampas was foiled of his fish, or of learning how the fish's\r\nheart lay. And by morning Shorty had been carefully instructed to change\r\nhis mind about once an hour. This is apt to discourage all but very\r\nsuperior missionaries. And I too escaped for the rest of this night. At\r\nGlendive we had a dim supper, and I bought some blankets; and after that\r\nit was late, and sleep occupied the attention of us all.\r\n\r\nWe lay along the shelves of the caboose, a peaceful sight I should\r\nthink, in that smoothly trundling cradle. I slept almost immediately, so\r\ntired that not even our stops or anything else waked me, save once, when\r\nthe air I was breathing grew suddenly pure, and I roused. Sitting in\r\nthe door was the lonely figure of the Virginian. He leaned in silent\r\ncontemplation of the occasional moon, and beneath it the Yellowstone's\r\nswift ripples. On the caboose shelves the others slept sound and still,\r\neach stretched or coiled as he had first put himself. They were not\r\nuntrustworthy to look at, it seemed to me--except Trampas. You would\r\nhave said the rest of that young humanity was average rough male blood,\r\nmerely needing to be told the proper things at the right time; and one\r\nbig bunchy stocking of the enthusiast stuck out of his blanket, solemn\r\nand innocent, and I laughed at it. There was a light sound by the door,\r\nand I found the Virginian's eye on me. Finding who it was, he nodded\r\nand motioned with his hand to go to sleep. And this I did with him in\r\nmy sight, still leaning in the open door, through which came the\r\ninterrupted moon and the swimming reaches of the Yellowstone.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXVI. THE GAME AND THE NATION--LAST ACT\r\n\r\n\r\nIt has happened to you, has it not, to wake in the morning and wonder\r\nfor a while where on earth you are? Thus I came half to life in the\r\ncaboose, hearing voices, but not the actual words at first.\r\n\r\nBut presently, \xe2\x80\x9cHathaway!\xe2\x80\x9d said some one more clearly. \xe2\x80\x9cPortland 1291!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis made no special stir in my intelligence, and I drowsed off again\r\nto the pleasant rhythm of the wheels. The little shock of stopping next\r\nbrought me to, somewhat, with the voices still round me; and when we\r\nwere again in motion, I heard: \xe2\x80\x9cRosebud! Portland 1279!\xe2\x80\x9d These figures\r\njarred me awake, and I said, \xe2\x80\x9cIt was 1291 before,\xe2\x80\x9d and sat up in my\r\nblankets.\r\n\r\nThe greeting they vouchsafed and the sight of them clustering\r\nexpressionless in the caboose brought last evening's uncomfortable\r\nmemory back to me. Our next stop revealed how things were going to-day.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cForsythe,\xe2\x80\x9d one of them read on the station. \xe2\x80\x9cPortland 1266.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were counting the lessening distance westward. This was the\r\nundercurrent of war. It broke on me as I procured fresh water at\r\nForsythe and made some toilet in their stolid presence. We were drawing\r\nnearer the Rawhide station--the point, I mean, where you left the\r\nrailway for the new mines. Now Rawhide station lay this side of\r\nBillings. The broad path of desertion would open ready for their feet\r\nwhen the narrow path to duty and Sunk Creek was still some fifty miles\r\nmore to wait. Here was Trampas's great strength; he need make no move\r\nmeanwhile, but lie low for the immediate temptation to front and waylay\r\nthem and win his battle over the deputy foreman. But the Virginian\r\nseemed to find nothing save enjoyment in this sunny September morning,\r\nand ate his breakfast at Forsythe serenely.\r\n\r\nThat meal done and that station gone, our caboose took up again its easy\r\ntrundle by the banks of the Yellowstone. The mutineers sat for a while\r\ndigesting in idleness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat's your scar?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired one at length inspecting casually the neck\r\nof his neighbor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFoolishness,\xe2\x80\x9d the other answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYourn?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don't know but I prefer to have myself to thank for a thing,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said the first.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was displaying myself,\xe2\x80\x9d continued the second. \xe2\x80\x9cOne day last summer it\r\nwas. We come on a big snake by Torrey Creek corral. The boys got betting\r\npretty lively that I dassent make my word good as to dealing with him,\r\nso I loped my cayuse full tilt by Mr. Snake, and swung down and catched\r\nhim up by the tail from the ground, and cracked him same as a whip, and\r\nsnapped his head off. You've saw it done?\xe2\x80\x9d he said to the audience.\r\n\r\nThe audience nodded wearily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the loose head flew agin me, and the fangs caught. I was pretty\r\nsick for a while.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt don't pay to be clumsy,\xe2\x80\x9d said the first man. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you'd snapped the\r\nsnake away from yu' instead of toward yu', its head would have whirled\r\noff into the brush, same as they do with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow like a knife-cut your scar looks!\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't it?\xe2\x80\x9d said the snake-snapper. \xe2\x80\x9cThere's many that gets fooled by\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn antelope knows a snake is his enemy,\xe2\x80\x9d said another to me. \xe2\x80\x9cEver seen\r\na buck circling round and round a rattler?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have always wanted to see that,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, heartily. For this I knew to\r\nbe a respectable piece of truth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's worth seeing,\xe2\x80\x9d the man went on. \xe2\x80\x9cAfter the buck gets close in, he\r\ngives an almighty jump up in the air, and down comes his four hoofs in\r\na bunch right on top of Mr. Snake. Cuts him all to hash. Now you tell me\r\nhow the buck knows that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOf course I could not tell him. And again we sat in silence for a\r\nwhile--friendlier silence, I thought.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA skunk'll kill yu' worse than a snake bite,\xe2\x80\x9d said another, presently.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don't mean that way,\xe2\x80\x9d he added. For I had smiled. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is a\r\nbrown skunk down in Arkansaw. Kind of prairie-dog brown. Littler than\r\nour variety, he is. And he is mad the whole year round, same as a dog\r\ngets. Only the dog has a spell and dies but this here Arkansaw skunk\r\nis mad right along, and it don't seem to interfere with his business in\r\nother respects. Well, suppose you're camping out, and suppose it's a hot\r\nnight, or you're in a hurry, and you've made camp late, or anyway you\r\nhaven't got inside any tent, but you have just bedded down in the open.\r\nSkunk comes travelling along and walks on your blankets. You're warm. He\r\nlikes that, same as a cat does. And he tramps with pleasure and comfort,\r\nsame as a cat. And you move. You get bit, that's all. And you die of\r\nhydrophobia. Ask anybody.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMost extraordinary!\xe2\x80\x9d said I. \xe2\x80\x9cBut did you ever see a person die from\r\nthis?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir. Never happened to. My cousin at Bald Knob did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDied?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir. Saw a man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how do you know they're not sick skunks?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir! They're well skunks. Well as anything. You'll not meet skunks\r\nin any state of the Union more robust than them in Arkansaw. And thick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's awful true,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed another. \xe2\x80\x9cI have buried hundreds of dollars'\r\nworth of clothes in Arkansaw.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn't yu' travel in a sponge bag?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Scipio. And this\r\nbrought a slight silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeakin' of bites,\xe2\x80\x9d spoke up a new man, \xe2\x80\x9chow's that?\xe2\x80\x9d He held up his\r\nthumb.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy!\xe2\x80\x9d breathed Scipio. \xe2\x80\x9cMust have been a lion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man wore a wounded look. \xe2\x80\x9cI was huntin' owl eggs for a botanist from\r\nBoston,\xe2\x80\x9d he explained to me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChiropodist, weren't he?\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio. \xe2\x80\x9cOr maybe a sonnabulator?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, honest,\xe2\x80\x9d protested the man with the thumb; so that I was sorry for\r\nhim, and begged him to go on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll listen to you,\xe2\x80\x9d I assured him. And I wondered why this politeness\r\nof mine should throw one or two of them into stifled mirth. Scipio, on\r\nthe other hand, gave me a disgusted look and sat back sullenly for a\r\nmoment, and then took himself out on the platform, where the Virginian\r\nwas lounging.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe young feller wore knee-pants and ever so thick spectacles with a\r\nhalf-moon cut in 'em,\xe2\x80\x9d resumed the narrator, \xe2\x80\x9cand he carried a tin box\r\nstrung to a strap I took for his lunch till it flew open on him and a\r\nhorn toad hustled out. Then I was sure he was a botanist--or whatever\r\nyu' say they're called. Well, he would have owl eggs--them little\r\nprairie-owl that some claim can turn their head clean around and\r\nkeep a-watchin' yu', only that's nonsense. We was ridin' through that\r\nprairie-dog town, used to be on the flat just after yu' crossed the\r\nsouth fork of Powder River on the Buffalo trail, and I said I'd dig an\r\nowl nest out for him if he was willing to camp till I'd dug it. I wanted\r\nto know about them owls some myself--if they did live with the dogs and\r\nsnakes, yu' know,\xe2\x80\x9d he broke off, appealing to me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d I told him eagerly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo while the botanist went glarin' around the town with his glasses to\r\nsee if he could spot a prairie-dog and an owl usin' the same hole, I was\r\ndiggin' in a hole I'd seen an owl run down. And that's what I got.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nheld up his thumb again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe snake!\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir. Mr. Rattler was keepin' house that day. Took me right there.\r\nI hauled him out of the hole hangin' to me. Eight rattles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEight!\xe2\x80\x9d said I. \xe2\x80\x9cA big one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir. Thought I was dead. But the woman--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe woman?\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, woman. Didn't I tell yu' the botanist had his wife along? Well, he\r\ndid. And she acted better than the man, for he was losin' his head,\r\nand shoutin' he had no whiskey, and he didn't guess his knife was sharp\r\nenough to amputate my thumb, and none of us chewed, and the doctor\r\nwas twenty miles away, and if he had only remembered to bring his\r\nammonia--well, he was screeching out 'most everything he knew in the\r\nworld, and without arranging it any, neither. But she just clawed his\r\npocket and burrowed and kep' yelling, 'Give him the stone, Augustus!'\r\nAnd she whipped out one of them Injun medicine-stones,--first one I ever\r\nseen,--and she clapped it on to my thumb, and it started in right away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did it do?\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSucked. Like blotting-paper does. Soft and funny it was, and gray. They\r\nget 'em from elks' stomachs, yu' know. And when it had sucked the poison\r\nout of the wound, off it falls of my thumb by itself! And I thanked the\r\nwoman for saving my life that capable and keeping her head that cool.\r\nI never knowed how excited she had been till afterward. She was awful\r\nshocked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose she started to talk when the danger was over,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, with\r\ndeep silence around me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; she didn't say nothing to me. But when her next child was born, it\r\nhad eight rattles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDin now rose wild in the caboose. They rocked together. The enthusiast\r\nbeat his knee tumultuously. And I joined them. Who could help it? It\r\nhad been so well conducted from the imperceptible beginning. Fact and\r\nfalsehood blended with such perfect art. And this last, an effect so\r\nnew made with such world-old material! I cared nothing that I was\r\nthe victim, and I joined them; but ceased, feeling suddenly somehow\r\nestranged or chilled. It was in their laughter. The loudness was too\r\nloud. And I caught the eyes of Trampas fixed upon the Virginian with\r\nexultant malevolence. Scipio's disgusted glance was upon me from the\r\ndoor.\r\n\r\nDazed by these signs, I went out on the platform to get away from the\r\nnoise. There the Virginian said to me: \xe2\x80\x9cCheer up! You'll not be so easy\r\nfor 'em that-a-way next season.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe said no more; and with his legs dangled over the railing, appeared to\r\nresume his newspaper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat's the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d said I to Scipio.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don't mind if he don't,\xe2\x80\x9d Scipio answered. \xe2\x80\x9cCouldn't yu' see? I\r\ntried to head 'em off from yu' all I knew, but yu' just ran in among 'em\r\nyourself. Couldn't yu' see? Kep' hinderin' and spoilin' me with askin'\r\nthose urgent questions of yourn--why, I had to let yu' go your way! Why,\r\nthat wasn't the ordinary play with the ordinary tenderfoot they treated\r\nyou to! You ain't a common tenderfoot this trip. You're the foreman's\r\nfriend. They've hit him through you. That's the way they count it. It's\r\nmade them encouraged. Can't yu' see?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nScipio stated it plainly. And as we ran by the next station, \xe2\x80\x9cHoward!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n they harshly yelled. \xe2\x80\x9cPortland 1256!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe had been passing gangs of workmen on the track. And at that last yell\r\nthe Virginian rose. \xe2\x80\x9cI reckon I'll join the meeting again,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis filling and repairing looks like the washout might have been\r\ntrue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWashout?\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBig Horn bridge, they say--four days ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I wish it came this side Rawhide station.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo yu'?\xe2\x80\x9d drawled the Virginian. And smiling at Scipio, he lounged in\r\nthrough the open door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe beats me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio, shaking his head. \xe2\x80\x9cHis trail is turruble\r\nhard to anticipate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe listened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWork bein' done on the road, I see,\xe2\x80\x9d the Virginian was saying, very\r\nfriendly and conversational.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe see it too,\xe2\x80\x9d said the voice of Trampas.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeem to be easin' their grades some.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRoads do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCheaper to build 'em the way they want 'em at the start, a man would\r\nthink,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested the Virginian, most friendly. \xe2\x80\x9cThere go some more\r\nI-talians.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey're Chinese,\xe2\x80\x9d said Trampas.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's so,\xe2\x80\x9d acknowledged the Virginian, with a laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat's he monkeyin' at now?\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Scipio.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWithout cheap foreigners they couldn't afford all this hyeh new\r\ngradin',\xe2\x80\x9d the Southerner continued.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGrading! Can't you tell when a flood's been eating the banks?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, sweet as honey. \xe2\x80\x9cBut 'ain't yu' heard\r\nof the improvements west of Big Timber, all the way to Missoula, this\r\nseason? I'm talkin' about them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! Talking about them. Yes, I've heard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood money-savin' scheme, ain't it?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cLettin' a\r\nfreight run down one hill an' up the next as far as she'll go without\r\nsteam, an' shavin' the hill down to that point.\xe2\x80\x9d Now this was an honest\r\nengineering fact. \xe2\x80\x9cBetter'n settin' dudes squintin' through telescopes\r\nand cypherin' over one per cent reductions,\xe2\x80\x9d the Southerner commented.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's common sense,\xe2\x80\x9d assented Trampas. \xe2\x80\x9cHave you heard the new scheme\r\nabout the water-tanks?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ain't right certain,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Southerner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must watch this,\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio, \xe2\x80\x9cor I shall bust.\xe2\x80\x9d He went in, and so\r\ndid I.\r\n\r\nThey were all sitting over this discussion of the Northern Pacific's\r\nrecent policy as to betterments, as though they were the board of\r\ndirectors. Pins could have dropped. Only nobody would have cared to hear\r\na pin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey used to put all their tanks at the bottom of their grades,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nTrampas.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, yu' get the water easier at the bottom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can pump it to the top, though,\xe2\x80\x9d said Trampas, growing superior.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd it's cheaper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat gets me,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, interested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrains after watering can start down hill now and get the benefit of\r\nthe gravity. It'll cut down operating expenses a heap.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's cert'nly common sense!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the Virginian, absorbed. \xe2\x80\x9cBut\r\nain't it kind o' tardy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLive and learn. So they gained speed, too. High speed on half the coal\r\nthis season, until the accident.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAccident!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, instantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYellowstone Limited. Man fired at engine driver. Train was flying past\r\nthat quick the bullet broke every window and killed a passenger on the\r\nback platform. You've been running too much with aristocrats,\xe2\x80\x9d finished\r\nTrampas, and turned on his heel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHaw, hew!\xe2\x80\x9d began the enthusiast, but his neighbor gripped him to\r\nsilence. This was a triumph too serious for noise. Not a mutineer moved;\r\nand I felt cold.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrampas,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, \xe2\x80\x9cI thought yu'd be afeared to try it on\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTrampas whirled round. His hand was at his belt. \xe2\x80\x9cAfraid!\xe2\x80\x9d he sneered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShorty!\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio, sternly, and leaping upon that youth, took his\r\nhalf-drawn pistol from him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm obliged to yu',\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian to Scipio. Trampas's hand left\r\nhis belt. He threw a slight, easy look at his men, and keeping his back\r\nto the Virginian, walked out on the platform and sat on the chair where\r\nthe Virginian had sat so much.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't you comprehend,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian to Shorty, amiably, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\r\nthis hyeh question has been discussed peaceable by civilized citizens?\r\nNow you sit down and be good, and Mr. Le Moyne will return your gun when\r\nwe're across that broken bridge, if they have got it fixed for heavy\r\ntrains yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis train will be lighter when it gets to that bridge,\xe2\x80\x9d spoke Trampas,\r\nout on his chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, that's true, too!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cMaybe none of us are\r\ncrossin' that Big Horn bridge now, except me. Funny if yu' should end by\r\npersuadin' me to quit and go to Rawhide myself! But I reckon I'll not. I\r\nreckon I'll worry along to Sunk Creek, somehow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't forget I'm cookin' for yu',\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio, gruffy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm obliged to yu',\xe2\x80\x9d said the Southerner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were speaking of a job for me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm right obliged. But yu' see--I ain't exackly foreman the way this\r\ncomes out, and my promises might not bind Judge Henry to pay salaries.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA push came through the train from forward. We were slowing for the\r\nRawhide station, and all began to be busy and to talk. \xe2\x80\x9cGoing up to the\r\nmines to-day?\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cOh, let's grub first.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cGuess it's too late, anyway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n And so forth; while they rolled and roped their bedding, and put on\r\ntheir coats with a good deal of elbow motion, and otherwise showed\r\noff. It was wasted. The Virginian did not know what was going on in the\r\ncaboose. He was leaning and looking out ahead, and Scipio's puzzled\r\neye never left him. And as we halted for the water-tank, the Southerner\r\nexclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cThey 'ain't got away yet!\xe2\x80\x9d as if it were good news to him.\r\n\r\nHe meant the delayed trains. Four stalled expresses were in front of us,\r\nbesides several freights. And two hours more at least before the bridge\r\nwould be ready.\r\n\r\nTravellers stood and sat about forlorn, near the cars, out in the\r\nsage-brush, anywhere. People in hats and spurs watched them, and Indian\r\nchiefs offered them painted bows and arrows and shiny horns.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon them passengers would prefer a laig o' mutton,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nVirginian to a man loafing near the caboose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBet your life!\xe2\x80\x9d said the man. \xe2\x80\x9cFirst lot has been stuck here four\r\ndays.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlumb starved, ain't they?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBet your life! They've eat up their dining cars and they've eat up this\r\ntown.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, looking at the town, \xe2\x80\x9cI expaict the\r\ndining-cyars contained more nourishment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, you're about right there!\xe2\x80\x9d said the man. He walked beside the\r\ncaboose as we puffed slowly forward from the water-tank to our siding.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFine business here if we'd only been ready,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd the\r\nCrow agent has let his Indians come over from the reservation. There has\r\nbeen a little beef brought in, and game, and fish. And big money in it,\r\nbet your life! Them Eastern passengers has just been robbed. I wisht I\r\nhad somethin' to sell!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnything starting for Rawhide this afternoon?\xe2\x80\x9d said Trampas, out of the\r\ncaboose door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot until morning,\xe2\x80\x9d said the man. \xe2\x80\x9cYou going to the mines?\xe2\x80\x9d he resumed\r\nto the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the Southerner, slowly and casually, and addressing\r\nhimself strictly to the man, while Trampas, on his side, paid obvious\r\ninattention, \xe2\x80\x9cthis hyeh delay, yu' see, may unsettle our plans some.\r\nBut it'll be one of two ways,--we're all goin' to Rawhide, or we're all\r\ngoin' to Billings. We're all one party, yu' see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTrampas laughed audibly inside the door as he rejoined his men. \xe2\x80\x9cLet him\r\nkeep up appearances,\xe2\x80\x9d I heard him tell them. \xe2\x80\x9cIt don't hurt us what he\r\nsays to strangers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I'm goin' to eat hearty either way,\xe2\x80\x9d continued the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nI ain' goin' to be robbed. I've been kind o' promisin' myself a treat if\r\nwe stopped hyeh.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTown's eat clean out,\xe2\x80\x9d said the man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo yu' tell me. But all you folks has forgot one source of revenue that\r\nyu' have right close by, mighty handy. If you have got a gunny sack,\r\nI'll show you how to make some money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBet your life!\xe2\x80\x9d said the man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Le Moyne,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, \xe2\x80\x9cthe outfit's cookin' stuff is\r\naboard, and if you'll get the fire ready, we'll try how frawgs' laigs go\r\nfried.\xe2\x80\x9d He walked off at once, the man following like a dog. Inside the\r\ncaboose rose a gust of laughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrogs!\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Scipio. And then turning a blank face to me, \xe2\x80\x9cFrogs?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cColonel Cyrus Jones had them on his bill of fare,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9c'FROGS'\r\nLEGS A LA DELMONICO.'\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShoo! I didn't get up that thing. They had it when I came. Never looked\r\nat it. Frogs?\xe2\x80\x9d He went down the steps very slowly, with a long frown.\r\nReaching the ground, he shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cThat man's trail is surely\r\nhard to anticipate,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I must hurry up that fire. For his\r\nappearance has given me encouragement,\xe2\x80\x9d Scipio concluded, and became\r\nbrisk. Shorty helped him, and I brought wood. Trampas and the other\r\npeople strolled off to the station, a compact band.\r\n\r\nOur little fire was built beside the caboose, so the cooking things\r\nmight be easily reached and put back. You would scarcely think such\r\noperations held any interest, even for the hungry, when there seemed\r\nto be nothing to cook. A few sticks blazing tamely in the dust, a\r\nfrying-pan, half a tin bucket of lard, some water, and barren plates and\r\nknives and forks, and three silent men attending to them--that was all.\r\nBut the travellers came to see. These waifs drew near us, and stood, a\r\nsad, lone, shifting fringe of audience; four to begin with; and then two\r\nwandered away; and presently one of these came back, finding it worse\r\nelsewhere. \xe2\x80\x9cSupper, boys?\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cBreakfast,\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio, crossly.\r\nAnd no more of them addressed us. I heard them joylessly mention Wall\r\nStreet to each other, and Saratoga; I even heard the name Bryn Mawr,\r\nwhich is near Philadelphia. But these fragments of home dropped in the\r\nwilderness here in Montana beside a freight caboose were of no interest\r\nto me now.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLooks like frogs down there, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio. \xe2\x80\x9cSee them marshy sloos\r\nfull of weeds?\xe2\x80\x9d We took a little turn and had a sight of the Virginian\r\nquite active among the ponds. \xe2\x80\x9cHush! I'm getting some thoughts,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n continued Scipio. \xe2\x80\x9cHe wasn't sorry enough. Don't interrupt me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm not,\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. But I'd 'most caught a-hold.\xe2\x80\x9d And Scipio muttered to himself again,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe wasn't sorry enough.\xe2\x80\x9d Presently he swore loud and brilliantly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell yu'!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat did he say to Trampas after that play they\r\nexchanged over railroad improvements and Trampas put the josh on him?\r\nDidn't he say, 'Trampas, I thought you'd be afraid to do it?' Well, sir,\r\nTrampas had better have been afraid. And that's what he meant. There's\r\nwhere he was bringin' it to. Trampas made an awful bad play then. You\r\nwait. Glory, but he's a knowin' man! Course he wasn't sorry. I guess he\r\nhad the hardest kind of work to look as sorry as he did. You wait.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait? What for? Go on, man! What for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't know! I don't know! Whatever hand he's been holdin' up, this is\r\nthe show-down. He's played for a show-down here before the caboose gets\r\noff the bridge. Come back to the fire, or Shorty'll be leavin' it\r\ngo out. Grow happy some, Shorty!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried on arriving, and his hand\r\ncracked on Shorty's shoulder. \xe2\x80\x9cSupper's in sight, Shorty. Food for\r\nreflection.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone for the stomach?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the passenger who had spoken once before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe're figuring on that too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio. His crossness had melted\r\nentirely away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, they're cow-boys!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed another passenger; and he moved\r\nnearer.\r\n\r\nFrom the station Trampas now came back, his herd following him less\r\ncompactly. They had found famine, and no hope of supplies until the\r\nnext train from the East. This was no fault of Trampas's; but they were\r\nfollowing him less compactly. They carried one piece of cheese, the\r\nsize of a fist, the weight of a brick, the hue of a corpse. And the\r\npassengers, seeing it, exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cThere's Old Faithful again!\xe2\x80\x9d and took\r\noff their hats.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou gentlemen met that cheese before, then?\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio, delighted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's been offered me three times a day for four days,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\npassenger. \xe2\x80\x9cDid he want a dollar or a dollar and a half?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo dollars!\xe2\x80\x9d blurted out the enthusiast. And all of us save Trampas\r\nfell into fits of imbecile laughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere comes our grub, anyway,\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio, looking off toward the\r\nmarshes. And his hilarity sobered away in a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, the train will be in soon,\xe2\x80\x9d stated Trampas. \xe2\x80\x9cI guess we'll get a\r\ndecent supper without frogs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll interest settled now upon the Virginian. He was coming with his man\r\nand his gunny sack, and the gunny sack hung from his shoulder heavily,\r\nas a full sack should. He took no notice of the gathering, but sat down\r\nand partly emptied the sack. \xe2\x80\x9cThere,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, very businesslike, to his\r\nassistant, \xe2\x80\x9cthat's all we'll want. I think you'll find a ready market\r\nfor the balance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my gracious!\xe2\x80\x9d said the enthusiast. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat fool eats a frog?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I'm fool enough for a tadpole!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the passenger. And they began\r\nto take out their pocket-books.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can cook yours right hyeh, gentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, with\r\nhis slow Southern courtesy. \xe2\x80\x9cThe dining-cyars don't look like they were\r\nfired up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much will you sell a couple for?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired the enthusiast.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian looked at him with friendly surprise. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, help yourself!\r\nWe're all together yet awhile. Help yourselves,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated, to Trampas\r\nand his followers. These hung back a moment, then, with a slinking\r\nmotion, set the cheese upon the earth and came forward nearer the fire\r\nto receive some supper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt won't scarcely be Delmonico style,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian to the\r\npassengers, \xe2\x80\x9cnor yet Saynt Augustine.\xe2\x80\x9d He meant the great Augustin, the\r\ntraditional chef of Philadelphia, whose history I had sketched for him\r\nat Colonel Cyrus Jones's eating palace.\r\n\r\nScipio now officiated. His frying-pan was busy, and prosperous odors\r\nrose from it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRun for a bucket of fresh water, Shorty,\xe2\x80\x9d the Virginian continued,\r\nbeginning his meal. \xe2\x80\x9cColonel, yu' cook pretty near good. If yu' had sold\r\n'em as advertised, yu'd have cert'nly made a name.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSeveral were now eating with satisfaction, but not Scipio. It was all\r\nthat he could do to cook straight. The whole man seemed to glisten.\r\nHis eye was shut to a slit once more, while the innocent passengers\r\nthankfully swallowed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, you see, you have made some money,\xe2\x80\x9d began the Virginian to the\r\nnative who had helped him get the frogs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBet your life!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the man. \xe2\x80\x9cDivvy, won't you?\xe2\x80\x9d And he held out\r\nhalf his gains.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKeep 'em,\xe2\x80\x9d returned the Southerner. \xe2\x80\x9cI reckon we're square. But I\r\nexpaict they'll not equal Delmonico's, seh?\xe2\x80\x9d he said to a passenger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't trust the judgment of a man as hungry as I am!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the\r\ntraveller, with a laugh. And he turned to his fellow-travellers. \xe2\x80\x9cDid\r\nyou ever enjoy supper at Delmonico's more than this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever!\xe2\x80\x9d they sighed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, look here,\xe2\x80\x9d said the traveller, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat fools the people of this\r\ntown are! Here we've been all these starving days, and you come and get\r\nahead of them!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's right easy explained,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cI've been where\r\nthere was big money in frawgs, and they 'ain't been. They're all cattle\r\nhyeh. Talk cattle, think cattle, and they're bankrupt in consequence.\r\nFallen through. Ain't that so?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired of the native.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's about the way,\xe2\x80\x9d said the man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's mighty hard to do what your neighbors ain't doin',\xe2\x80\x9d pursued the\r\nVirginian. \xe2\x80\x9cMontana is all cattle, an' these folks must be cattle,\r\nan' never notice the country right hyeh is too small for a range, an'\r\nswampy, anyway, an' just waitin' to be a frawg ranch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this, all wore a face of careful reserve.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm not claimin' to be smarter than you folks hyeh,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nVirginian, deprecatingly, to his assistant. \xe2\x80\x9cBut travellin' learns a\r\nman many customs. You wouldn't do the business they done at Tulare,\r\nCalifornia, north side o' the lake. They cert'nly utilized them hopeless\r\nswamps splendid. Of course they put up big capital and went into it\r\nscientific, gettin' advice from the government Fish Commission, an' such\r\nlike knowledge. Yu' see, they had big markets for their frawgs,--San\r\nFrancisco, Los Angeles, and clear to New York afteh the Southern Pacific\r\nwas through. But up hyeh yu' could sell to passengers every day like yu'\r\ndone this one day. They would get to know yu' along the line. Competing\r\nswamps are scarce. The dining-cyars would take your frawgs, and yu'\r\nwould have the Yellowstone Park for four months in the year. Them hotels\r\nare anxious to please, an' they would buy off yu' what their Eastern\r\npatrons esteem as fine-eatin'. And you folks would be sellin' something\r\ninstead o' nothin'.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's a practical idea,\xe2\x80\x9d said a traveller. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd little cost.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd little cost,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould Eastern people eat frogs?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired the man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook at us!\xe2\x80\x9d said the traveller.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDelmonico doesn't give yu' such a treat!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot exactly!\xe2\x80\x9d the traveller exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much would be paid for frogs?\xe2\x80\x9d said Trampas to him. And I saw\r\nScipio bend closer to his cooking.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don't know,\xe2\x80\x9d said the traveller. \xe2\x80\x9cWe've paid pretty well, you\r\nsee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou're late for Tulare, Trampas,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was not thinking of Tulare,\xe2\x80\x9d Trampas retorted. Scipio's nose was in\r\nthe frying-pan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMos' comical spot you ever struck!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, looking round\r\nupon the whole company. He allowed himself a broad smile of retrospect.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo hear 'em talk frawgs at Tulare! Same as other folks talks hawsses or\r\nsteers or whatever they're raising to sell. Yu'd fall into it yourselves\r\nif yu' started the business. Anything a man's bread and butter depends\r\non, he's going to be earnest about. Don't care if it is a frawg.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's so,\xe2\x80\x9d said the native. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd it paid good?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe only money in the county was right there,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the Virginian.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was a dead county, and only frawgs was movin'. But that business was\r\na-fannin' to beat four of a kind. It made yu' feel strange at first, as\r\nI said. For all the men had been cattle-men at one time or another.\r\nTill yu' got accustomed, it would give 'most anybody a shock to hear 'em\r\nspeak about herdin' the bulls in a pasture by themselves.\xe2\x80\x9d The Virginian\r\nallowed himself another smile, but became serious again. \xe2\x80\x9cThat was their\r\npolicy,\xe2\x80\x9d he explained. \xe2\x80\x9cExcept at certain times o' year they kept the\r\nbulls separate. The Fish Commission told 'em they'd better, and it\r\ncert'nly worked mighty well. It or something did--for, gentlemen, hush!\r\nbut there was millions.
