Trampas lay in his bed, not asleep, and\r\nsullen as ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe ain't got religion this trip,\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio to me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid his new foreman get it?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuh! It would spoil him. You keep around that's all. Keep around.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nScipio was not to be probed; and I went, still baffled, to my repose.\r\n\r\nNo light burned in the cabin as I approached its door.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian's room was quiet and dark; and that Dr. MacBride slumbered\r\nwas plainly audible to me, even before I entered. Go fishing with him!\r\nI thought, as I undressed. And I selfishly decided that the Judge might\r\nhave this privilege entirely to himself. Sleep came to me fairly\r\nsoon, in spite of the Doctor. I was wakened from it by my bed's being\r\njolted--not a pleasant thing that night. I must have started. And it\r\nwas the quiet voice of the Virginian that told me he was sorry to have\r\naccidentally disturbed me. This disturbed me a good deal more. But\r\nhis steps did not go to the bunk house, as my sensational mind had\r\nsuggested. He was not wearing much, and in the dimness he seemed taller\r\nthan common. I next made out that he was bending over Dr. MacBride. The\r\ndivine at last sprang upright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am armed,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cTake care. Who are you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can lay down your gun, seh. I feel like my spirit was going to bear\r\nwitness. I feel like I might get an enlightening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was using some of the missionary's own language. The baffling I had\r\nbeen treated to by Scipio melted to nothing in this. Did living men\r\npetrify, I should have changed to mineral between the sheets. The Doctor\r\ngot out of bed, lighted his lamp, and found a book; and the two retired\r\ninto the Virginian's room, where I could hear the exhortations as I\r\nlay amazed. In time the Doctor returned, blew out his lamp, and settled\r\nhimself. I had been very much awake, but was nearly gone to sleep again,\r\nwhen the door creaked and the Virginian stood by the Doctor's side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you awake, seh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat? What's that? What is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me, seh. The enemy is winning on me. I'm feeling less inward\r\nopposition to sin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lamp was lighted, and I listened to some further exhortations.\r\nThey must have taken half an hour. When the Doctor was in bed again, I\r\nthought that I heard him sigh. This upset my composure in the dark;\r\nbut I lay face downward in the pillow, and the Doctor was soon again\r\nsnoring. I envied him for a while his faculty of easy sleep. But I must\r\nhave dropped off myself; for it was the lamp in my eyes that now waked\r\nme as he came back for the third time from the Virginian's room. Before\r\nblowing the light out he looked at his watch, and thereupon I inquired\r\nthe hour of him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\nI could not sleep any more now, and I lay watching the darkness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm afeared to be alone!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian's voice presently in the\r\nnext room. \xe2\x80\x9cI'm afeared.\xe2\x80\x9d There was a short pause, and then he shouted\r\nvery loud, \xe2\x80\x9cI'm losin' my desire afteh the sincere milk of the Word!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat? What's that? What?\xe2\x80\x9d The Doctor's cot gave a great crack as he\r\nstarted up listening, and I put my face deep in the pillow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm afeared! I'm afeared! Sin has quit being bitter in my belly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCourage, my good man.\xe2\x80\x9d The Doctor was out of bed with his lamp again,\r\nand the door shut behind him. Between them they made it long this time.\r\nI saw the window become gray; then the corners of the furniture grow\r\nvisible; and outside, the dry chorus of the blackbirds began to fill the\r\ndawn. To these the sounds of chickens and impatient hoofs in the stable\r\nwere added, and some cow wandered by loudly calling for her calf. Next,\r\nsome one whistling passed near and grew distant. But although the cold\r\nhue that I lay staring at through the window warmed and changed, the\r\nDoctor continued working hard over his patient in the next room. Only a\r\nword here and there was distinct; but it was plain from the Virginian's\r\nfewer remarks that the sin in his belly was alarming him less. Yes, they\r\nmade this time long. But it proved, indeed, the last one. And though\r\nsome sort of catastrophe was bound to fall upon us, it was myself who\r\nprecipitated the thing that did happen.\r\n\r\nDay was wholly come. I looked at my own watch, and it was six. I had\r\nbeen about seven hours in my bed, and the Doctor had been about seven\r\nhours out of his. The door opened, and he came in with his book and\r\nlamp. He seemed to be shivering a little, and I saw him cast a longing\r\neye at his couch. But the Virginian followed him even as he blew out\r\nthe now quite superfluous light. They made a noticeable couple in their\r\nunderclothes: the Virginian with his lean racehorse shanks running to\r\na point at his ankle, and the Doctor with his stomach and his fat\r\nsedentary calves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou'll be going to breakfast and the ladies, seh, pretty soon,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe Virginian, with a chastened voice. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I'll worry through the day\r\nsomehow without yu'. And to-night you can turn your wolf loose on me\r\nagain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOnce more it was no use. My face was deep in the pillow, but I made\r\nsounds as of a hen who has laid an egg. It broke on the Doctor with a\r\ntotal instantaneous smash, quite like an egg.\r\n\r\nHe tried to speak calmly. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is a disgrace. An infamous disgrace.\r\nNever in my life have I--\xe2\x80\x9d Words forsook him, and his face grew redder.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever in my life--\xe2\x80\x9d He stopped again, because, at the sight of him\r\nbeing dignified in his red drawers, I was making the noise of a dozen\r\nhens. It was suddenly too much for the Virginian. He hastened into his\r\nroom, and there sank on the floor with his head in his hands. The Doctor\r\nimmediately slammed the door upon him, and this rendered me easily fit\r\nfor a lunatic asylum. I cried into my pillow, and wondered if the Doctor\r\nwould come and kill me. But he took no notice of me whatever. I could\r\nhear the Virginian's convulsions through the door, and also the Doctor\r\nfuriously making his toilet within three feet of my head; and I lay\r\nquite still with my face the other way, for I was really afraid to look\r\nat him. When I heard him walk to the door in his boots, I ventured to\r\npeep; and there he was, going out with his bag in his hand. As I still\r\ncontinued to lie, weak and sore, and with a mind that had ceased all\r\noperation, the Virginian's door opened. He was clean and dressed and\r\ndecent, but the devil still sported in his eye. I have never seen a\r\ncreature more irresistibly handsome.\r\n\r\nThen my mind worked again. \xe2\x80\x9cYou've gone and done it,\xe2\x80\x9d said I. \xe2\x80\x9cHe's\r\npacked his valise. He'll not sleep here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Virginian looked quickly out of the door. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, he's leavin' us!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nexclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cDrivin' away right now in his little old buggy!\xe2\x80\x9d He turned\r\nto me, and our eyes met solemnly over this large fact. I thought that\r\nI perceived the faintest tincture of dismay in the features of Judge\r\nHenry's new, responsible, trusty foreman. This was the first act of his\r\nadministration. Once again he looked out at the departing missionary.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d he vindictively stated, \xe2\x80\x9cI cert'nly ain't goin' to run afteh\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d And he looked at me again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you suppose the Judge knows?\xe2\x80\x9d I inquired.\r\n\r\nHe shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cThe windo' shades is all down still oveh yondeh.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n He paused. \xe2\x80\x9cI don't care,\xe2\x80\x9d he stated, quite as if he had been ten years\r\nold. Then he grinned guiltily. \xe2\x80\x9cI was mighty respectful to him all\r\nnight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, respectful! Especially when you invited him to turn his wolf\r\nloose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Virginian gave a joyous gulp. He now came and sat down on the edge\r\nof my bed. \xe2\x80\x9cI spoke awful good English to him most of the time,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nhe. \xe2\x80\x9cI can, yu' know, when I cinch my attention tight on to it. Yes,\r\nI cert'nly spoke a lot o' good English. I didn't understand some of it\r\nmyself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was now growing frankly pleased with his exploit. He had builded so\r\nmuch better than he knew. He got up and looked out across the crystal\r\nworld of light. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Doctor is at one-mile crossing,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cHe'll\r\nget breakfast at the N-lazy-Y.\xe2\x80\x9d Then he returned and sat again on my\r\nbed, and began to give me his real heart. \xe2\x80\x9cI never set up for being\r\nbetter than others. Not even to myself. My thoughts ain't apt to travel\r\naround making comparisons. And I shouldn't wonder if my memory took\r\nas much notice of the meannesses I have done as of--as of the other\r\nactions. But to have to sit like a dumb lamb and let a stranger tell yu'\r\nfor an hour that yu're a hawg and a swine, just after you have acted in\r\na way which them that know the facts would call pretty near white--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrampas!\xe2\x80\x9d I could not help exclaiming.\r\n\r\nFor there are moments of insight when a guess amounts to knowledge.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas Scipio told--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Not a word. He wouldn't tell me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, yu' see, I arrived home hyeh this evenin' with several thoughts\r\nworkin' and stirrin' inside me. And not one o' them thoughts was what\r\nyu'd call Christian. I ain't the least little bit ashamed of 'em. I'm a\r\nhuman. But after the Judge--well, yu' heard him. And so when I went away\r\nfrom that talk and saw how positions was changed--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA step outside stopped him short. Nothing more could be read in his\r\nface, for there was Trampas himself in the open door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood morning,\xe2\x80\x9d said Trampas, not looking at us. He spoke with the same\r\ncool sullenness of yesterday.\r\n\r\nWe returned his greeting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe I'm late in congratulating you on your promotion,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian consulted his watch. \xe2\x80\x9cIt's only half afteh six,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nreturned.\r\n\r\nTrampas's sullenness deepened. \xe2\x80\x9cAny man is to be congratulated on\r\ngetting a rise, I expect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis time the Virginian let him have it. \xe2\x80\x9cCert'nly. And I ain't\r\nforgetting how much I owe mine to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTrampas would have liked to let himself go. \xe2\x80\x9cI've not come here for any\r\nforgiveness,\xe2\x80\x9d he sneered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen did yu' feel yu' needed any?\xe2\x80\x9d The Virginian was impregnable.\r\n\r\nTrampas seemed to feel how little he was gaining this way. He came out\r\nstraight now. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I haven't any Judge behind me, I know. I heard you'd\r\nbe paying the boys this morning, and I've come for my time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou're thinking of leaving us?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the new foreman. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat's your\r\ndissatisfaction?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I'm not needing anybody back of me. I'll get along by myself.\xe2\x80\x9d It\r\nwas thus he revealed his expectation of being dismissed by his enemy.\r\n\r\nThis would have knocked any meditated generosity out of my heart. But\r\nI was not the Virginian. He shifted his legs, leaned back a little, and\r\nlaughed. \xe2\x80\x9cGo back to your job, Trampas, if that's all your complaint.\r\nYou're right about me being in luck. But maybe there's two of us in\r\nluck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was this that Scipio had preferred me to see with my own eyes. The\r\nfight was between man and man no longer. The case could not be one of\r\nforgiveness; but the Virginian would not use his official position to\r\ncrush his subordinate.\r\n\r\nTrampas departed with something muttered that I did not hear, and the\r\nVirginian closed intimate conversation by saying, \xe2\x80\x9cYou'll be late for\r\nbreakfast.\xe2\x80\x9d With that he also took himself away.\r\n\r\nThe ladies were inclined to be scandalized, but not the Judge. When my\r\nwhole story was done, he brought his fist down on the table, and not\r\nlightly this time. \xe2\x80\x9cI'd make him lieutenant general if the ranch offered\r\nthat position!\xe2\x80\x9d he declared.\r\n\r\nMiss Molly Wood said nothing at the time. But in the afternoon, by her\r\nwish, she went fishing, with the Virginian deputed to escort her. I\r\nrode with them, for a while. I was not going to continue a third in that\r\nparty; the Virginian was too becomingly dressed, and I saw KENILWORTH\r\npeeping out of his pocket. I meant to be fishing by myself when that\r\nvolume was returned.\r\n\r\nBut Miss Wood talked with skilful openness as we rode. \xe2\x80\x9cI've heard all\r\nabout you and Dr. MacBride,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cHow could you do it, when the\r\nJudge places such confidence in you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked pleased. \xe2\x80\x9cI reckon,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI couldn't be so good if I\r\nwasn't bad onced in a while.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, there's a skunk,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, noticing the pretty little animal\r\ntrotting in front of us at the edge of the thickets.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, where is it? Don't let me see it!\xe2\x80\x9d screamed Molly. And at this\r\ndeeply feminine remark, the Virginian looked at her with such a smile\r\nthat, had I been a woman, it would have made me his to do what he\r\npleased with on the spot.\r\n\r\nUpon the lady, however, it seemed to make less impression. Or rather, I\r\nhad better say, whatever were her feelings, she very naturally made no\r\ndisplay of them, and contrived not to be aware of that expression which\r\nhad passed over the Virginian's face.\r\n\r\nIt was later that these few words reached me while I was fishing alone:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you anything different to tell me yet?\xe2\x80\x9d I heard him say.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; I have.\xe2\x80\x9d She spoke in accents light and well intrenched. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish\r\nto say that I have never liked any man better than you. But I expect\r\nto!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe must have drawn small comfort from such an answer as that. But he\r\nlaughed out indomitably: \xe2\x80\x9cDon't yu' go betting on any such expectation!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n And then their words ceased to be distinct, and it was only their two\r\nvoices that I heard wandering among the windings of the stream.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXII. \xe2\x80\x9cWHAT IS A RUSTLER?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nWe all know what birds of a feather do. And it may be safely surmised\r\nthat if a bird of any particular feather has been for a long while\r\nunable to see other birds of its kind, it will flock with them all the\r\nmore assiduously when they happen to alight in its vicinity.\r\n\r\nNow the Ogdens were birds of Molly's feather. They wore Eastern, and not\r\nWestern, plumage, and their song was a different song from that which\r\nthe Bear Creek birds sang. To be sure, the piping of little George\r\nTaylor was full of hopeful interest; and many other strains, both\r\nstriking and melodious, were lifted in Cattle Land, and had given\r\npleasure to Molly's ear. But although Indians, and bears, and mavericks,\r\nmake worthy themes for song, these are not the only songs in the world.\r\nTherefore the Eastern warblings of the Ogdens sounded doubly sweet to\r\nMolly Wood. Such words as Newport, Bar Harbor, and Tiffany's thrilled\r\nher exceedingly. It made no difference that she herself had never been\r\nto Newport or Bar Harbor, and had visited Tiffany's more often to admire\r\nthan to purchase. On the contrary, this rather added a dazzle to the\r\nmusic of the Ogdens. And Molly, whose Eastern song had been silent in\r\nthis strange land, began to chirp it again during the visit that she\r\nmade at the Sunk Creek Ranch.\r\n\r\nThus the Virginian's cause by no means prospered at this time. His\r\nforces were scattered, while Molly's were concentrated. The girl was\r\nnot at that point where absence makes the heart grow fonder. While the\r\nVirginian was trundling his long, responsible miles in the caboose,\r\ndelivering the cattle at Chicago, vanquishing Trampas along the\r\nYellowstone, she had regained herself.\r\n\r\nThus it was that she could tell him so easily during those first hours\r\nthat they were alone after his return, \xe2\x80\x9cI expect to like another man\r\nbetter than you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAbsence had recruited her. And then the Ogdens had reenforced her. They\r\nbrought the East back powerfully to her memory, and her thoughts filled\r\nwith it. They did not dream that they were assisting in any battle. No\r\none ever had more unconscious allies than did Molly at that time. But\r\nshe used them consciously, or almost consciously. She frequented them;\r\nshe spoke of Eastern matters; she found that she had acquaintances whom\r\nthe Ogdens also knew, and she often brought them into the conversation.\r\nFor it may be said, I think, that she was fighting a battle--nay, a\r\ncampaign. And perhaps this was a hopeful sign for the Virginian (had he\r\nbut known it), that the girl resorted to allies. She surrounded herself,\r\nshe steeped herself, with the East, to have, as it were, a sort of\r\ncounteractant against the spell of the black-haired horse man.\r\n\r\nAnd his forces were, as I have said, scattered. For his promotion gave\r\nhim no more time for love-making. He was foreman now. He had said to\r\nJudge Henry, \xe2\x80\x9cI'll try to please yu'.\xe2\x80\x9d And after the throb of emotion\r\nwhich these words had both concealed and conveyed, there came to him\r\nthat sort of intention to win which amounts to a certainty. Yes, he\r\nwould please Judge Henry!\r\n\r\nHe did not know how much he had already pleased him. He did not know\r\nthat the Judge was humorously undecided which of his new foreman's first\r\nacts had the more delighted him: his performance with the missionary, or\r\nhis magnanimity to Trampas.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood feeling is a great thing in any one,\xe2\x80\x9d the Judge would say; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\r\nlike to know that my foreman has so much sense.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am personally very grateful to him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Henry.\r\n\r\nAnd indeed so was the whole company. To be afflicted with Dr. MacBride\r\nfor one night instead of six was a great liberation.\r\n\r\nBut the Virginian never saw his sweetheart alone again; while she was at\r\nthe Sunk Creek Ranch, his duties called him away so much that there was\r\nno chance for him. Worse still, that habit of birds of a feather brought\r\nabout a separation more considerable. She arranged to go East with the\r\nOgdens. It was so good an opportunity to travel with friends, instead of\r\nmaking the journey alone!\r\n\r\nMolly's term of ministration at the schoolhouse had so pleased Bear\r\nCreek that she was warmly urged to take a holiday. School could afford\r\nto begin a little late. Accordingly, she departed.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian hid his sore heart from her during the moment of farewell\r\nthat they had.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I'll not want any more books,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9ctill yu' come back.\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\nthen he made cheerfulness. \xe2\x80\x9cIt's just the other way round!\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the other way round?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, last time it was me that went travelling, and you that stayed\r\nbehind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it was!\xe2\x80\x9d And here she gave him a last scratch. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you'll be busier\r\nthan ever,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cno spare time to grieve about me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe could wound him, and she knew it. Nobody else could. That is why she\r\ndid it.\r\n\r\nBut he gave her something to remember, too.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNext time,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cneither of us will stay behind. We'll both go\r\ntogether.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd with these words he gave her no laughing glance. It was a look that\r\nmingled with the words; so that now and again in the train, both came\r\nback to her, and she sat pensive, drawing near to Bennington and hearing\r\nhis voice and seeing his eyes.\r\n\r\nHow is it that this girl could cry at having to tell Sam Bannett she\r\ncould not think of him, and then treat another lover as she treated the\r\nVirginian? I cannot tell you, having never (as I said before) been a\r\nwoman myself.\r\n\r\nBennington opened its arms to its venturesome daughter. Much was made\r\nof Molly Wood. Old faces and old places welcomed her. Fatted calves of\r\nvarying dimensions made their appearance. And although the fatted calf\r\nis an animal that can assume more divergent shapes than any other known\r\ncreature,--being sometimes champagne and partridges, and again cake and\r\ncurrant wine,--through each disguise you can always identify the same\r\ncalf. The girl from Bear Creek met it at every turn.\r\n\r\nThe Bannetts at Hoosic Falls offered a large specimen to Molly--a dinner\r\n(perhaps I should say a banquet) of twenty-four. And Sam Bannett of\r\ncourse took her to drive more than once.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to see the Hoosic Bridge,\xe2\x80\x9d she would say. And when they reached\r\nthat well-remembered point, \xe2\x80\x9cHow lovely it is!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed. And as\r\nshe gazed at the view up and down the valley, she would grow pensive.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow natural the church looks,\xe2\x80\x9d she continued. And then, having crossed\r\nboth bridges, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, there's the dear old lodge gate!\xe2\x80\x9d Or again, while\r\nthey drove up the valley of the little Hoosic: \xe2\x80\x9cI had forgotten it was\r\nso nice and lonely. But after all, no woods are so interesting as\r\nthose where you might possibly see a bear or an elk.\xe2\x80\x9d And upon another\r\noccasion, after a cry of enthusiasm at the view from the top of Mount\r\nAnthony, \xe2\x80\x9cIt's lovely, lovely, lovely,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with diminishing\r\ncadence, ending in pensiveness once more. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you see that little bit\r\njust there? No, not where the trees are--that bare spot that looks\r\nbrown and warm in the sun. With a little sagebrush, that spot would look\r\nsomething like a place I know on Bear Creek. Only of course you don't\r\nget the clear air here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't forget you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you remember me? Or is it out of\r\nsight out of mind?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd with this beginning he renewed his suit. She told him that she\r\nforgot no one; that she should return always, lest they might forget\r\nher.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReturn always!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cYou talk as if your anchor was\r\ndragging.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWas it? At all events, Sam failed in his suit.\r\n\r\nOver in the house at Dunbarton, the old lady held Molly's hand and\r\nlooked a long while at her. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have changed very much,\xe2\x80\x9d she said\r\nfinally.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am a year older,\xe2\x80\x9d said the girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPshaw, my dear!\xe2\x80\x9d said the great-aunt. \xe2\x80\x9cWho is he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Molly, with indignation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you shouldn't answer so loud,\xe2\x80\x9d said the great-aunt.\r\n\r\nThe girl suddenly hid her face. \xe2\x80\x9cI don't believe I can love any one,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n she said, \xe2\x80\x9cexcept myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then that old lady, who in her day had made her courtesy to\r\nLafayette, began to stroke her niece's buried head, because she more\r\nthan half understood. And understanding thus much, she asked no prying\r\nquestions, but thought of the days of her own youth, and only spoke a\r\nlittle quiet love and confidence to Molly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am an old, old woman,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I haven't forgotten about it.\r\nThey objected to him because he had no fortune. But he was brave and\r\nhandsome, and I loved him, my dear. Only I ought to have loved him more.\r\nI gave him my promise to think about it. And he and his ship were lost.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n The great-aunt's voice had become very soft and low, and she spoke with\r\nmany pauses. \xe2\x80\x9cSo then I knew. If I had--if--perhaps I should have lost\r\nhim; but it would have been after--ah, well! So long as you can help\r\nit, never marry! But when you cannot help it a moment longer, then\r\nlisten to nothing but that; for, my dear, I know your choice would be\r\nworthy of the Starks. And now--let me see his picture.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, aunty!\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I won't pretend to be supernatural,\xe2\x80\x9d said the aunt, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\r\nthought you kept one back when you were showing us those Western views\r\nlast night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow this was the precise truth. Molly had brought a number of\r\nphotographs from Wyoming to show to her friends at home. These, however,\r\nwith one exception, were not portraits. They were views of scenery and\r\nof cattle round-ups, and other scenes characteristic of ranch life. Of\r\nyoung men she had in her possession several photographs, and all but one\r\nof these she had left behind her. Her aunt's penetration had in a way\r\nmesmerized the girl; she rose obediently and sought that picture of\r\nthe Virginian. It was full length, displaying him in all his cow-boy\r\ntrappings,--the leathern chaps, the belt and pistol, and in his hand a\r\ncoil of rope.\r\n\r\nNot one of her family had seen it, or suspected its existence. She now\r\nbrought it downstairs and placed it in her aunt's hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMercy!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the old lady.\r\n\r\nMolly was silent, but her eye grew warlike.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that the way--\xe2\x80\x9d began the aunt. \xe2\x80\x9cMercy!\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured; and she sat\r\nstaring at the picture.\r\n\r\nMolly remained silent.\r\n\r\nHer aunt looked slowly up at her. \xe2\x80\x9cHas a man like that presumed--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe's not a bit like that. Yes, he's exactly like that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly. And\r\nshe would have snatched the photograph away, but her aunt retained it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose there are days when he does not kill\r\npeople.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe never killed anybody!\xe2\x80\x9d And Molly laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you seriously--\xe2\x80\x9d said the old lady.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI almost might--at times. He is perfectly splendid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear, you have fallen in love with his clothes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's not his clothes. And I'm not in love. He often wears others. He\r\nwears a white collar like anybody.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen that would be a more suitable way to be photographed, I think. He\r\ncouldn't go round like that here. I could not receive him myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe'd never think of such a thing. Why, you talk as if he were a\r\nsavage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old lady studied the picture closely for a minute. \xe2\x80\x9cI think it is a\r\ngood face,\xe2\x80\x9d she finally remarked. \xe2\x80\x9cIs the fellow as handsome as that, my\r\ndear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMore so, Molly thought. And who was he, and what were his prospects?\r\nwere the aunt's next inquiries. She shook her head at the answers which\r\nshe received; and she also shook her head over her niece's emphatic\r\ndenial that her heart was lost to this man. But when their parting came,\r\nthe old lady said: \xe2\x80\x9cGod bless you and keep you, my dear. I'll not try to\r\nmanage you. They managed me--\xe2\x80\x9d A sigh spoke the rest of this sentence.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I'm not worried about you--at least, not very much. You have never\r\ndone anything that was not worthy of the Starks. And if you're going\r\nto take him, do it before I die so that I can bid him welcome for your\r\nsake. God bless you, my dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd after the girl had gone back to Bennington, the great-aunt had this\r\nthought: \xe2\x80\x9cShe is like us all. She wants a man that is a man.\xe2\x80\x9d Nor did\r\nthe old lady breathe her knowledge to any member of the family. For she\r\nwas a loyal spirit, and her girl's confidence was sacred to her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBesides,\xe2\x80\x9d she reflected, \xe2\x80\x9cif even I can do nothing with her, what a\r\nmess THEY'D make of it! We should hear of her elopement next.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo Molly's immediate family never saw that photograph, and never heard\r\na word from her upon this subject. But on the day that she left for Bear\r\nCreek, as they sat missing her and discussing her visit in the evening,\r\nMrs. Bell observed: \xe2\x80\x9cMother, how did you think she was?\xe2\x80\x9d--\xe2\x80\x9cI never saw\r\nher better, Sarah. That horrible place seems to agree with her.\xe2\x80\x9d--\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\r\nyes, agree. It seemed to me--\xe2\x80\x9c--\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d--\xe2\x80\x9cOh, just somehow that she\r\nwas thinking.\xe2\x80\x9d--\xe2\x80\x9cThinking?\xe2\x80\x9d--\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I believe she has something on her\r\nmind.\xe2\x80\x9d--\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean a man,\xe2\x80\x9d said Andrew Bell.--\xe2\x80\x9cA man, Andrew?\xe2\x80\x9d--\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\r\nMrs. Wood, that's what Sarah always means.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt may be mentioned that Sarah's surmises did not greatly contribute to\r\nher mother's happiness. And rumor is so strange a thing that presently\r\nfrom the malicious outside air came a vague and dreadful word--one of\r\nthose words that cannot be traced to its source. Somebody said to Andrew\r\nBell that they heard Miss Molly Wood was engaged to marry a RUSTLER.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeavens, Andrew!\xe2\x80\x9d said his wife; \xe2\x80\x9cwhat is a rustler?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was not in any dictionary, and current translations of it were\r\ninconsistent. A man at Hoosic Falls said that he had passed through\r\nCheyenne, and heard the term applied in a complimentary way to people\r\nwho were alive and pushing. Another man had always supposed it meant\r\nsome kind of horse. But the most alarming version of all was that a\r\nrustler was a cattle thief.\r\n\r\nNow the truth is that all these meanings were right. The word ran a sort\r\nof progress in the cattle country, gathering many meanings as it went.\r\nIt gathered more, however, in Bennington. In a very few days, gossip had\r\nit that Molly was engaged to a gambler, a gold miner, an escaped stage\r\nrobber, and a Mexican bandit; while Mrs. Flynt feared she had married a\r\nMormon.\r\n\r\nAlong Bear Creek, however, Molly and her \xe2\x80\x9crustler\xe2\x80\x9d took a ride soon\r\nafter her return. They were neither married nor engaged, and she was\r\ntelling him about Vermont.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never was there,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cNever happened to strike in that\r\ndirection.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat decided your direction?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, looking for chances. I reckon I must have been more ambitious than\r\nmy brothers--or more restless. They stayed around on farms. But I got\r\nout. When I went back again six years afterward, I was twenty. They was\r\ntalking about the same old things. Men of twenty-five and thirty--yet\r\njust sittin' and talkin' about the same old things. I told my mother\r\nabout what I'd seen here and there, and she liked it, right to her\r\ndeath. But the others--well, when I found this whole world was hawgs and\r\nturkeys to them, with a little gunnin' afteh small game throwed in, I\r\nput on my hat one mawnin' and told 'em maybe when I was fifty I'd look\r\nin on 'em again to see if they'd got any new subjects. But they'll\r\nnever. My brothers don't seem to want chances.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have lost a good many yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's correct.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, \xe2\x80\x9csometimes I think you know a great deal more than\r\nI ever shall.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, of course I do,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, quite simply. \xe2\x80\x9cI have earned my living\r\nsince I was fourteen. And that's from old Mexico to British Columbia.\r\nI have never stolen or begged a cent. I'd not want yu' to know what I\r\nknow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was looking at him, half listening and half thinking of her\r\ngreat-aunt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not losing chances any more,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you are the best\r\nI've got.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was not sorry to have Georgie Taylor come galloping along at this\r\nmoment and join them. But the Virginian swore profanely under his\r\nbreath. And on this ride nothing more happened.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXIII. VARIOUS POINTS\r\n\r\n\r\nLove had been snowbound for many weeks. Before this imprisonment its\r\ncourse had run neither smooth nor rough, so far as eye could see; it\r\nhad run either not at all, or, as an undercurrent, deep out of sight. In\r\ntheir rides, in their talks, love had been dumb, as to spoken words at\r\nleast; for the Virginian had set himself a heavy task of silence and of\r\npatience. Then, where winter barred his visits to Bear Creek, and there\r\nwas for the while no ranch work or responsibility to fill his thoughts\r\nand blood with action, he set himself a task much lighter. Often,\r\ninstead of Shakespeare and fiction, school books lay open on his cabin\r\ntable; and penmanship and spelling helped the hours to pass. Many sheets\r\nof paper did he fill with various exercises, and Mrs. Henry gave him her\r\nassistance in advice and corrections.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall presently be in love with him myself,\xe2\x80\x9d she told the Judge. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nit's time for you to become anxious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am perfectly safe,\xe2\x80\x9d he retorted. \xe2\x80\x9cThere's only one woman for him any\r\nmore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is not good enough for him,\xe2\x80\x9d declared Mrs. Henry. \xe2\x80\x9cBut he'll never\r\nsee that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo the snow fell, the world froze, and the spelling-books and exercises\r\nwent on. But this was not the only case of education which was\r\nprogressing at the Sunk Creek Ranch while love was snowbound.\r\n\r\nOne morning Scipio le Moyne entered the Virginian's sitting room--that\r\napartment where Dr. MacBride had wrestled with sin so courageously all\r\nnight.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian sat at his desk. Open books lay around him; a\r\nhalf-finished piece of writing was beneath his fist; his fingers were\r\ncoated with ink. Education enveloped him, it may be said. But there was\r\nnone in his eye. That was upon the window, looking far across the cold\r\nplain.\r\n\r\nThe foreman did not move when Scipio came in, and this humorous spirit\r\nsmiled to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cIt's Bear Creek he's havin' a vision of,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nconcluded. But he knew instantly that this was not so. The Virginian\r\nwas looking at something real, and Scipio went to the window to see for\r\nhimself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, having seen, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen is he going to leave us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe foreman continued looking at two horsemen riding together. Their\r\nshapes, small in the distance, showed black against the universal\r\nwhiteness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen d' yu' figure he'll leave us?\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Scipio.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the Virginian, always watching the distant horsemen; and\r\nagain, \xe2\x80\x9che.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nScipio sprawled down, familiarly, across a chair. He and the Virginian\r\nhad come to know each other very well since that first meeting at\r\nMedora. They were birds many of whose feathers were the same, and the\r\nVirginian often talked to Scipio without reserve. Consequently, Scipio\r\nnow understood those two syllables that the Virginian had pronounced\r\nprecisely as though the sentences which lay between them had been fully\r\nexpressed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHm,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, one will be a gain, and the other won't be no\r\nloss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor Shorty!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cPoor fool!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nScipio was less compassionate. \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he persisted, \xe2\x80\x9cI ain't sorry for\r\nhim. Any man old enough to have hair on his face ought to see through\r\nTrampas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Virginian looked out of the window again, and watched Shorty and\r\nTrampas as they rode in the distance. \xe2\x80\x9cShorty is kind to animals,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cHe has gentled that hawss Pedro he bought with his first money.\r\nGentled him wonderful. When a man is kind to dumb animals, I always say\r\nhe had got some good in him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d Scipio reluctantly admitted. \xe2\x80\x9cYes. But I always did hate a fool.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis hyeh is a mighty cruel country,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cTo\r\nanimals that is. Think of it! Think what we do to hundreds an' thousands\r\nof little calves! Throw 'em down, brand 'em, cut 'em, ear mark 'em, turn\r\n'em loose, and on to the next. It has got to be, of course. But I say\r\nthis. If a man can go jammin' hot irons on to little calves and slicin'\r\npieces off 'em with his knife, and live along, keepin' a kindness for\r\nanimals in his heart, he has got some good in him. And that's what\r\nShorty has got. But he is lettin' Trampas get a hold of him, and both of\r\nthem will leave us.\xe2\x80\x9d And the Virginian looked out across the huge winter\r\nwhiteness again. But the riders had now vanished behind some foot-hills.\r\n\r\nScipio sat silent. He had never put these thoughts about men and animals\r\nto himself, and when they were put to him, he saw that they were true.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQueer,\xe2\x80\x9d he observed finally.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing's queer,\xe2\x80\x9d stated the Virginian, \xe2\x80\x9cexcept marriage and lightning.\r\nThem two occurrences can still give me a sensation of surprise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll the same it is queer,\xe2\x80\x9d Scipio insisted\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, let her go at me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Trampas. He done you dirt. You pass that over. You could have\r\nfired him, but you let him stay and keep his job. That's goodness. And\r\nbadness is resultin' from it, straight. Badness right from goodness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou're off the trail a whole lot,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich side am I off, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNorth, south, east, and west. First point. I didn't expect to do\r\nTrampas any good by not killin' him, which I came pretty near doin'\r\nthree times. Nor I didn't expect to do Trampas any good by lettin' him\r\nkeep his job. But I am foreman of this ranch. And I can sit and tell all\r\nmen to their face: 'I was above that meanness.' Point two: it ain't any\r\nGOODNESS, it is TRAMPAS that badness has resulted from. Put him anywhere\r\nand it will be the same. Put him under my eye, and I can follow his\r\nmoves a little, anyway. You have noticed, maybe, that since you and I\r\nrun on to that dead Polled Angus cow, that was still warm when we got\r\nto her, we have found no more cows dead of sudden death. We came mighty\r\nclose to catchin' whoever it was that killed that cow and ran her calf\r\noff to his own bunch. He wasn't ten minutes ahead of us. We can prove\r\nnothin'; and he knows that just as well as we do. But our cows have all\r\nquit dyin' of sudden death. And Trampas he's gettin' ready for a change\r\nof residence. As soon as all the outfits begin hirin' new hands in the\r\nspring, Trampas will leave us and take a job with some of them. And\r\nmaybe our cows'll commence gettin' killed again, and we'll have to take\r\nsteps that will be more emphatic--maybe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nScipio meditated. \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder what killin' a man feels like?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, nothing to bother yu'--when he'd ought to have been killed. Next\r\npoint: Trampas he'll take Shorty with him, which is certainly bad for\r\nShorty. But it's me that has kept Shorty out of harm's way this long. If\r\nI had fired Trampas, he'd have worked Shorty into dissatisfaction that\r\nmuch sooner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nScipio meditated again. \xe2\x80\x9cI knowed Trampas would pull his freight,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I didn't think of Shorty. What makes you think it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe asked me for a raise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe ain't worth the pay he's getting now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrampas has told him different.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen a man ain't got no ideas of his own,\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio, \xe2\x80\x9che'd ought to\r\nbe kind o' careful who he borrows 'em from.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's mighty correct,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cPoor Shorty! He has told\r\nme about his life. It is sorrowful. And he will never get wise. It was\r\ntoo late for him to get wise when he was born. D' yu' know why he's\r\nafter higher wages? He sends most all his money East.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't see what Trampas wants him for,\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, a handy tool some day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot very handy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Trampas is aimin' to train him. Yu' see, supposin' yu' were\r\nfiguring to turn professional thief--yu'd be lookin' around for a nice\r\nyoung trustful accomplice to take all the punishment and let you take\r\nthe rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo such thing!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Scipio, angrily. \xe2\x80\x9cI'm no shirker.\xe2\x80\x9d And then,\r\nperceiving the Virginian's expression, he broke out laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nexclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cyu' fooled me that time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLooks that way. But I do mean it about Trampas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPresently Scipio rose, and noticed the half-finished exercise upon the\r\nVirginian's desk. \xe2\x80\x9cTrampas is a rolling stone,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA rolling piece of mud,\xe2\x80\x9d corrected the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMud! That's right. I'm a rolling stone. Sometimes I'd most like to quit\r\nbeing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's easy done,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo doubt, when yu've found the moss yu' want to gather.\xe2\x80\x9d As Scipio\r\nglanced at the school books again, a sparkle lurked in his bleached blue\r\neye. \xe2\x80\x9cI can cipher some,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I expect I've got my own notions\r\nabout spelling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI retain a few private ideas that way myself,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked the Virginian,\r\ninnocently; and Scipio's sparkle gathered light.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs to my geography,\xe2\x80\x9d he pursued, \xe2\x80\x9cthat's away out loose in the brush.\r\nIs Bennington the capital of Vermont? And how d' yu' spell bridegroom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLast point!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted the Virginian, letting a book fly after him:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cdon't let badness and goodness worry yu', for yu'll never be a judge of\r\nthem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Scipio had dodged the book, and was gone. As he went his way, he\r\nsaid to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cAll the same, it must pay to fall regular in love.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n At the bunk house that afternoon it was observed that he was unusually\r\nsilent. His exit from the foreman's cabin had let in a breath of winter\r\nso chill that the Virginian went to see his thermometer, a Christmas\r\npresent from Mrs. Henry. It registered twenty below zero. After reviving\r\nthe fire to a white blaze, the foreman sat thinking over the story\r\nof Shorty: what its useless, feeble past had been; what would be its\r\nuseless, feeble future. He shook his head over the sombre question,\r\nWas there any way out for Shorty? \xe2\x80\x9cIt may be,\xe2\x80\x9d he reflected, \xe2\x80\x9cthat them\r\nwhose pleasure brings yu' into this world owes yu' a living. But that\r\ndon't make the world responsible. The world did not beget you. I reckon\r\nman helps them that help themselves. As for the universe, it looks like\r\nit did too wholesale a business to turn out an article up to standard\r\nevery clip. Yes, it is sorrowful. For Shorty is kind to his hawss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the evening the Virginian brought Shorty into his room. He usually\r\nknew what he had to say, usually found it easy to arrange his thoughts;\r\nand after such arranging the words came of themselves. But as he looked\r\nat Shorty, this did not happen to him. There was not a line of badness\r\nin the face; yet also there was not a line of strength; no promise in\r\neye, or nose, or chin; the whole thing melted to a stubby, featureless\r\nmediocrity. It was a countenance like thousands; and hopelessness filled\r\nthe Virginian as he looked at this lost dog, and his dull, wistful eyes.\r\n\r\nBut some beginning must be made.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder what the thermometer has got to be,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYu' can see it,\r\nif yu'll hold the lamp to that right side of the window.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShorty held the lamp. \xe2\x80\x9cI never used any,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, looking out at the\r\ninstrument, nevertheless.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian had forgotten that Shorty could not read. So he looked\r\nout of the window himself, and found that it was twenty-two below zero.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is pretty good tobacco,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked; and Shorty helped himself,\r\nand filled his pipe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had to rub my left ear with snow to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cI was just in\r\ntime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought it looked pretty freezy out where yu' was riding,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nforeman.\r\n\r\nThe lost dog's eyes showed plain astonishment. \xe2\x80\x9cWe didn't see you out\r\nthere,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said the foreman, \xe2\x80\x9cit'll soon not be freezing any more; and then\r\nwe'll all be warm enough with work. Everybody will be working all over\r\nthe range. And I wish I knew somebody that had a lot of stable work to\r\nbe attended to. I cert'nly do for your sake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause it's the right kind of a job for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can make more--\xe2\x80\x9d began Shorty, and stopped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is a time coming,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen I'll want somebody\r\nthat knows how to get the friendship of hawsses. I'll want him to handle\r\nsome special hawsses the Judge has plans about. Judge Henry would pay\r\nfifty a month for that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can make more,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty, this time with stubbornness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, yes. Sometimes a man can--when he's not worth it, I mean. But it\r\ndon't generally last.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShorty was silent. \xe2\x80\x9cI used to make more myself,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou're making a lot more now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes. But I mean when I was fooling around the earth, jumping from\r\njob to job, and helling all over town between whiles. I was not worth\r\nfifty a month then, nor twenty-five. But there was nights I made a heap\r\nmore at cyards.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShorty's eyes grew large.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd then, bang! it was gone with treatin' the men and the girls.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't always--\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty, and stopped again.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian knew that he was thinking about the money he sent East.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter a while,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, \xe2\x80\x9cI noticed a right strange fact. The\r\nmoney I made easy that I WASN'T worth, it went like it came. I strained\r\nmyself none gettin' or spendin' it. But the money I made hard that I WAS\r\nworth, why I began to feel right careful about that. And now I have got\r\nsavings stowed away. If once yu' could know how good that feels--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo I would know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty, \xe2\x80\x9cwith your luck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat's my luck?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, sternly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if I had took up land along a creek that never goes dry and\r\nproved upon it like you have, and if I had saw that land raise its value\r\non me with me lifting no finger--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did you lift no finger?\xe2\x80\x9d cut in the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cWho stopped yu'\r\ntaking up land? Did it not stretch in front of yu', behind yu', all\r\naround yu', the biggest, baldest opportunity in sight? That was the time\r\nI lifted my finger; but yu' didn't.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShorty stood stubborn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut never mind that,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cTake my land away to-morrow,\r\nand I'd still have my savings in bank. Because, you see, I had to work\r\nright hard gathering them in. I found out what I could do, and I settled\r\ndown and did it. Now you can do that too. The only tough part is the\r\nfinding out what you're good for. And for you, that is found. If you'll\r\njust decide to work at this thing you can do, and gentle those hawsses\r\nfor the Judge, you'll be having savings in a bank yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can make more,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lost dog.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian was on the point of saying, \xe2\x80\x9cThen get out!\xe2\x80\x9d But instead,\r\nhe spoke kindness to the end. \xe2\x80\x9cThe weather is freezing yet,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand it will be for a good long while. Take your time, and tell me if\r\nyu' change your mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter that Shorty returned to the bunk house, and the Virginian knew\r\nthat the boy had learned his lesson of discontent from Trampas with\r\na thoroughness past all unteaching. This petty triumph of evil seemed\r\nscarce of the size to count as any victory over the Virginian. But all\r\nmen grasp at straws. Since that first moment, when in the Medicine Bow\r\nsaloon the Virginian had shut the mouth of Trampas by a word, the man\r\nhad been trying to get even without risk; and at each successive clash\r\nof his weapon with the Virginian's, he had merely met another public\r\nhumiliation. Therefore, now at the Sunk Creek Ranch in these cold white\r\ndays, a certain lurking insolence in his gait showed plainly his opinion\r\nthat by disaffecting Shorty he had made some sort of reprisal.\r\n\r\nYes, he had poisoned the lost dog. In the springtime, when the\r\nneighboring ranches needed additional hands, it happened as the\r\nVirginian had foreseen,--Trampas departed to a \xe2\x80\x9cbetter job,\xe2\x80\x9d as he took\r\npains to say, and with him the docile Shorty rode away upon his horse\r\nPedro.\r\n\r\nLove now was not any longer snowbound. The mountain trails were open\r\nenough for the sure feet of love's steed--that horse called Monte.\r\nBut duty blocked the path of love. Instead of turning his face to Bear\r\nCreek, the foreman had other journeys to make, full of heavy work,\r\nand watchfulness, and councils with the Judge. The cattle thieves were\r\ngrowing bold, and winter had scattered the cattle widely over the range.\r\nTherefore the Virginian, instead of going to see her, wrote a letter to\r\nhis sweetheart. It was his first.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXIV. A LETTER WITH A MORAL\r\n\r\n\r\nThe letter which the Virginian wrote to Molly Wood was, as has been\r\nstated, the first that he had ever addressed to her. I think, perhaps,\r\nhe may have been a little shy as to his skill in the epistolary art, a\r\nlittle anxious lest any sustained production from his pen might contain\r\nblunders that would too staringly remind her of his scant learning. He\r\ncould turn off a business communication about steers or stock cars, or\r\nany other of the subjects involved in his profession, with a brevity\r\nand a clearness that led the Judge to confide three-quarters of such\r\ncorrespondence to his foreman. \xe2\x80\x9cWrite to the 76 outfit,\xe2\x80\x9d the Judge would\r\nsay, \xe2\x80\x9cand tell them that my wagon cannot start for the round-up until,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n etc.; or \xe2\x80\x9cWrite to Cheyenne and say that if they will hold a meeting\r\nnext Monday week, I will,\xe2\x80\x9d etc. And then the Virginian would write such\r\ncommunications with ease.\r\n\r\nBut his first message to his lady was scarcely written with ease. It\r\nmust be classed, I think, among those productions which are styled\r\nliterary EFFORTS. It was completed in pencil before it was copied in\r\nink; and that first draft of it in pencil was well-nigh illegible with\r\nerasures and amendments. The state of mind of the writer during its\r\ncomposition may be gathered without further description on my part from\r\na slight interruption which occurred in the middle.\r\n\r\nThe door opened, and Scipio put his head in. \xe2\x80\x9cYou coming to dinner?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ninquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou go to hell,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy jinks!\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio, quietly, and he shut the door without further\r\nobservation.\r\n\r\nTo tell the truth, I doubt if this letter would ever have been\r\nundertaken, far less completed and despatched, had not the lover's heart\r\nbeen wrung with disappointment. All winter long he had looked to that\r\nday when he should knock at the girl's door, and hear her voice bid him\r\ncome in. All winter long he had been choosing the ride he would take\r\nher. He had imagined a sunny afternoon, a hidden grove, a sheltering\r\ncleft of rock, a running spring, and some words of his that should\r\nconquer her at last and leave his lips upon hers. And with this\r\ncontrolled fire pent up within him, he had counted the days, scratching\r\nthem off his calendar with a dig each night that once or twice snapped\r\nthe pen. Then, when the trail stood open, this meeting was deferred,\r\nput off for indefinite days, or weeks; he could not tell how long.\r\nSo, gripping his pencil and tracing heavy words, he gave himself what\r\nconsolation he could by writing her.\r\n\r\nThe letter, duly stamped and addressed to Bear Creek, set forth upon\r\nits travels; and these were devious and long. When it reached its\r\ndestination, it was some twenty days old. It had gone by private hand\r\nat the outset, taken the stagecoach at a way point, become late in\r\nthat stagecoach, reached a point of transfer, and waited there for the\r\npostmaster to begin, continue, end, and recover from a game of poker,\r\nmingled with whiskey. Then it once more proceeded, was dropped at\r\nthe right way point, and carried by private hand to Bear Creek. The\r\nexperience of this letter, however, was not at all a remarkable one at\r\nthat time in Wyoming.\r\n\r\nMolly Wood looked at the envelope. She had never before seen the\r\nVirginian's handwriting. She knew it instantly. She closed her door and\r\nsat down to read it with a beating heart.\r\n\r\nSUNK CREEK RANCH, May 5, 188-\r\n\r\nMy Dear Miss Wood: I am sorry about this. My plan was different. It was\r\nto get over for a ride with you about now or sooner. This year Spring is\r\nearly. The snow is off the flats this side the range and where the\r\nsun gets a chance to hit the earth strong all day it is green and has\r\nflowers too, a good many. You can see them bob and mix together in the\r\nwind. The quaking-asps down low on the South side are in small leaf and\r\nwill soon be twinkling like the flowers do now. I had planned to take a\r\nlook at this with you and that was a better plan than what I have got to\r\ndo. The water is high but I could have got over and as for the snow on\r\ntop of the mountain a man told me nobody could cross it for a week yet,\r\nbecause he had just done it himself. Was not he a funny man? You ought\r\nto see how the birds have streamed across the sky while Spring was\r\ncoming. But you have seen them on your side of the mountain. But I can't\r\ncome now Miss Wood. There is a lot for me to do that has to be done and\r\nJudge Henry needs more than two eyes just now. I could not think much of\r\nmyself if I left him for my own wishes.\r\n\r\nBut the days will be warmer when I come. We will not have to quit by\r\nfive, and we can get off and sit too. We could not sit now unless for a\r\nvery short while. If I know when I can come I will try to let you know,\r\nbut I think it will be this way. I think you will just see me coming for\r\nI have things to do of an unsure nature and a good number of such. Do\r\nnot believe reports about Indians. They are started by editors to keep\r\nthe soldiers in the country. The friends of the editors get the hay and\r\nbeef contracts. Indians do not come to settled parts like Bear Creek is.\r\nIt is all editors and politicianists.\r\n\r\nNothing has happened worth telling you. I have read that play Othello.\r\nNo man should write down such a thing. Do you know if it is true? I have\r\nseen one worse affair down in Arizona. He killed his little child as\r\nwell as his wife but such things should not be put down in fine language\r\nfor the public. I have read Romeo and Juliet. That is beautiful language\r\nbut Romeo is no man. I like his friend Mercutio that gets killed. He\r\nis a man. If he had got Juliet there would have been no foolishness and\r\ntrouble.\r\n\r\nWell Miss Wood I would like to see you to-day. Do you know what I think\r\nMonte would do if I rode him out and let the rein slack? He would come\r\nstraight to your gate for he is a horse of great judgement. (\xe2\x80\x9cThat's the\r\nfirst word he has misspelled,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly.) I suppose you are sitting\r\nwith George Taylor and those children right now. Then George will get\r\nold enough to help his father but Uncle Hewie's twins will be ready for\r\nyou about then and the supply will keep coming from all quarters all\r\nsizes for you to say big A little a to them. There is no news here. Only\r\ncalves and cows and the hens are laying now which does always seem news\r\nto a hen every time she does it. Did I ever tell you about a hen Emily\r\nwe had here? She was venturesome to an extent I have not seen in other\r\nhens only she had poor judgement and would make no family ties. She\r\nwould keep trying to get interest in the ties of others taking charge\r\nof little chicks and bantams and turkeys and puppies one time, and she\r\nthought most anything was an egg. I will tell you about her sometime.\r\nShe died without family ties one day while I was building a house for\r\nher to teach school in. (\xe2\x80\x9cThe outrageous wretch!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Molly! And her\r\ncheeks turned deep pink as she sat alone with her lover's letter.)\r\n\r\nI am coming the first day I am free. I will be a hundred miles from you\r\nmost of the time when I am not more but I will ride a hundred miles for\r\none hour and Monte is up to that. After never seeing you for so long I\r\nwill make one hour do if I have to. Here is a flower I have just been\r\nout and picked. I have kissed it now. That is the best I can do yet.\r\n\r\nMolly laid the letter in her lap and looked at the flower. Then suddenly\r\nshe jumped up and pressed it to her lips, and after a long moment held\r\nit away from her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, no.\xe2\x80\x9d She sat down.\r\n\r\nIt was some time before she finished the letter. Then once more she got\r\nup and put on her hat.\r\n\r\nMrs. Taylor wondered where the girl could be walking so fast. But she\r\nwas not walking anywhere, and in half an hour she returned, rosy with\r\nher swift exercise, but with a spirit as perturbed as when she had set\r\nout.\r\n\r\nNext morning at six, when she looked out of her window, there was Monte\r\ntied to the Taylor's gate. Ah, could he have come the day before, could\r\nshe have found him when she returned from that swift walk of hers!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXV. PROGRESS OF THE LOST DOG\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was not even an hour's visit that the Virginian was able to pay\r\nhis lady love. But neither had he come a hundred miles to see her. The\r\nnecessities of his wandering work had chanced to bring him close enough\r\nfor a glimpse of her, and this glimpse he took, almost on the wing. For\r\nhe had to rejoin a company of men at once.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYu' got my letter?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYesterday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYesterday! I wrote it three weeks ago. Well, yu' got it. This cannot\r\nbe the hour with you that I mentioned. That is coming, and maybe very\r\nsoon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe could say nothing. Relief she felt, and yet with it something like a\r\npang.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo-day does not count,\xe2\x80\x9d he told her, \xe2\x80\x9cexcept that every time I see you\r\ncounts with me. But this is not the hour that I mentioned.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhat little else was said between them upon this early morning shall be\r\ntold duly. For this visit in its own good time did count momentously,\r\nthough both of them took it lightly while its fleeting minutes passed.\r\nHe returned to her two volumes that she had lent him long ago and\r\nwith Taylor he left a horse which he had brought for her to ride. As a\r\ngood-by, he put a bunch of flowers in her hand. Then he was gone, and\r\nshe watched him going by the thick bushes along the stream. They were\r\npink with wild roses; and the meadow-larks, invisible in the grass,\r\nlike hiding choristers, sent up across the empty miles of air their\r\nunexpected song. Earth and sky had been propitious, could he have\r\nstayed; and perhaps one portion of her heart had been propitious too.\r\nSo, as he rode away on Monte, she watched him, half chilled by reason,\r\nhalf melted by passion, self-thwarted, self-accusing, unresolved.\r\nTherefore the days that came for her now were all of them unhappy ones,\r\nwhile for him they were filled with work well done and with changeless\r\nlonging.\r\n\r\nOne day it seemed as if a lull was coming, a pause in which he could\r\nat last attain that hour with her. He left the camp and turned his face\r\ntoward Bear Creek. The way led him along Butte Creek. Across the stream\r\nlay Balaam's large ranch; and presently on the other bank he saw Balaam\r\nhimself, and reined in Monte for a moment to watch what Balaam was\r\ndoing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's what I've heard,\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered to himself. For Balaam had led\r\nsome horses to the water, and was lashing them heavily because they\r\nwould not drink. He looked at this spectacle so intently that he did not\r\nsee Shorty approaching along the trail.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMorning,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty to him, with some constraint.\r\n\r\nBut the Virginian gave him a pleasant greeting, \xe2\x80\x9cI was afraid I'd not\r\ncatch you so quick,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is for you.\xe2\x80\x9d He handed his\r\nrecent foreman a letter of much battered appearance. It was from the\r\nJudge. It had not come straight, but very gradually, in the pockets of\r\nthree successive cow-punchers. As the Virginian glanced over it and saw\r\nthat the enclosure it contained was for Balaam, his heart fell. Here\r\nwere new orders for him, and he could not go to see his sweetheart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello, Shorty!\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam, from over the creek. To the Virginian he\r\ngave a slight nod. He did not know him, although he knew well enough who\r\nhe was.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHyeh's a letter from Judge Henry for yu'\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, and he\r\ncrossed the creek.\r\n\r\nMany weeks before, in the early spring, Balaam had borrowed two horses\r\nfrom the Judge, promising to return them at once. But the Judge, of\r\ncourse, wrote very civilly. He hoped that \xe2\x80\x9cthis dunning reminder\xe2\x80\x9d might\r\nbe excused. As Balaam read the reminder, he wished that he had sent the\r\nhorses before. The Judge was a greater man than he in the Territory.\r\nBalaam could not but excuse the \xe2\x80\x9cdunning reminder,\xe2\x80\x9d--but he was ready to\r\nbe disagreeable to somebody at once.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, musing aloud in his annoyance, \xe2\x80\x9cJudge Henry wants them\r\nby the 30th. Well, this is the 24th, and time enough yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is the 27th,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, briefly.\r\n\r\nThat made a difference! Not so easy to reach Sunk Creek in good order by\r\nthe 30th! Balaam had drifted three sunrises behind the progress of the\r\nmonth. Days look alike, and often lose their very names in the quiet\r\ndepths of Cattle Land. The horses were not even here at the ranch.\r\nBalaam was ready to be very disagreeable now. Suddenly he perceived the\r\ndate of the Judge's letter. He held it out to the Virginian, and struck\r\nthe paper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat's your idea in bringing this here two weeks late?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nNow, when he had struck that paper, Shorty looked at the Virginian. But\r\nnothing happened beyond a certain change of light in the Southerner's\r\neyes. And when the Southerner spoke, it was with his usual gentleness\r\nand civility. He explained that the letter had been put in his hands\r\njust now by Shorty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam. He looked at Shorty. How had he come to be a\r\nmessenger? \xe2\x80\x9cYou working for the Sunk Creek outfit again?\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty.\r\n\r\nBalaam turned to the Virginian again. \xe2\x80\x9cHow do you expect me to get those\r\nhorses to Sunk Creek by the 30th?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Virginian levelled a lazy eye on Balaam. \xe2\x80\x9cI ain' doin' any\r\nexpecting,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. His native dialect was on top to-day. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Judge\r\nhas friends goin' to arrive from New Yawk for a trip across the Basin,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n he added. \xe2\x80\x9cThe hawsses are for them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBalaam grunted with displeasure, and thought of the sixty or seventy\r\ndays since he had told the Judge he would return the horses at once.\r\nHe looked across at Shorty seated in the shade, and through his uneasy\r\nthoughts his instinct irrelevantly noted what a good pony the youth\r\nrode. It was the same animal he had seen once or twice before. But\r\nsomething must be done. The Judge's horses were far out on the big\r\nrange, and must be found and driven in, which would take certainly the\r\nrest of this day, possibly part of the next.\r\n\r\nBalaam called to one of his men and gave some sharp orders, emphasizing\r\ndetails, and enjoining haste, while the Virginian leaned slightly\r\nagainst his horse, with one arm over the saddle, hearing and\r\nunderstanding, but not smiling outwardly. The man departed to saddle up\r\nfor his search on the big range, and Balaam resumed the unhitching of\r\nhis team.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you're not working for the Sunk Creek outfit now?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired of\r\nShorty. He ignored the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cWorking for the Goose Egg?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSand Hill outfit, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty.\r\n\r\nBalaam grinned. He noticed how Shorty's yellow hair stuck through a hole\r\nin his hat, and how old and battered were Shorty's overalls. Shorty had\r\nbeen glad to take a little accidental pay for becoming the bearer of the\r\nletter which he had delivered to the Virginian. But even that sum was no\r\nlonger in his possession. He had passed through Drybone on his way, and\r\nat Drybone there had been a game of poker. Shorty's money was now in the\r\npocket of Trampas. But he had one valuable possession in the world left\r\nto him, and that was his horse Pedro.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood pony of yours,\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam to him now, from across Butte Creek.\r\nThen he struck his own horse in the jaw because he held back from coming\r\nto the water as the other had done.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour trace ain't unhitched,\xe2\x80\x9d commented the Virginian, pointing.\r\n\r\nBalaam loosed the strap he had forgotten, and cut the horse again for\r\nconsistency's sake. The animal, bewildered, now came down to the water,\r\nwith its head in the air, and snuffing as it took short, nervous steps.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian looked on at this, silent and sombre. He could scarcely\r\ninterfere between another man and his own beast. Neither he nor Balaam\r\nwas among those who say their prayers. Yet in this omission they were\r\nnot equal. A half-great poet once had a wholly great day, and in that\r\ngreat day he was able to write a poem that has lived and become, with\r\nmany, a household word. He called it The Rime of the Ancient Mariner.\r\nAnd it is rich with many lines that possess the memory; but these are\r\nthe golden ones:\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cHe prayeth well who loveth well\r\n     Both man and bird and beast.\r\n\r\n     He prayeth best who loveth best\r\n     All things both great and small;\r\n     For the dear God who loveth us,\r\n     He made and loveth all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThese lines are the pure gold. They are good to teach children; because\r\nafter the children come to be men, they may believe at least some part\r\nof them still. The Virginian did not know them,--but his heart had\r\ntaught him many things. I doubt if Balaam knew them either. But on him\r\nthey would have been as pearls to swine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you've quit the round-up?\xe2\x80\x9d he resumed to Shorty.\r\n\r\nShorty nodded and looked sidewise at the Virginian.\r\n\r\nFor the Virginian knew that he had been turned off for going to sleep\r\nwhile night-herding.\r\n\r\nThen Balaam threw another glance on Pedro the horse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello, Shorty!\xe2\x80\x9d he called out, for the boy was departing. \xe2\x80\x9cDon't you\r\nlike dinner any more? It's ready about now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShorty forded the creek and slung his saddle off, and on invitation\r\nturned Pedro, his buckskin pony, into Balaam's pasture. This was green,\r\nthe rest of the wide world being yellow, except only where Butte Creek,\r\nwith its bordering cottonwoods, coiled away into the desert distance\r\nlike a green snake without end. The Virginian also turned his horse into\r\nthe pasture. He must stay at the ranch till the Judge's horses should be\r\nfound.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Balaam's East yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said her lord, leading the way to his dining\r\nroom.\r\n\r\nHe wanted Shorty to dine with him, and could not exclude the Virginian,\r\nmuch as he should have enjoyed this.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee any Indians?\xe2\x80\x9d he enquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNa-a!\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty, in disdain of recent rumors.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey're headin' the other way,\xe2\x80\x9d observed the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cBow Laig Range\r\nis where they was repawted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat business have they got off the reservation, I'd like to know,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said the ranchman, \xe2\x80\x9cBow Leg, or anywhere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it's just a hunt, and a kind of visitin' their friends on the South\r\nReservation,\xe2\x80\x9d Shorty explained. \xe2\x80\x9cSquaws along and all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if the folks at Washington don't keep squaws and all where they\r\nbelong,\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam, in a rage, \xe2\x80\x9cthe folks in Wyoming Territory 'ill do\r\na little job that way themselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere's a petition out,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty. \xe2\x80\x9cPaper's goin' East with a lot of\r\nnames to it. But they ain't no harm, them Indians ain't.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo harm?\xe2\x80\x9d rasped out Balaam. \xe2\x80\x9cWas it white men druv off the O. C.\r\nyearlings?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBalaam's Eastern grammar was sometimes at the mercy of his Western\r\nfeelings. The thought of the perennial stultification of Indian affairs\r\nat Washington, whether by politician or philanthropist, was always sure\r\nto arouse him. He walked impatiently about while he spoke, and halted\r\nimpatiently at the window. Out in the world the unclouded day was\r\nshining, and Balaam's eye travelled across the plains to where a blue\r\nline, faint and pale, lay along the end of the vast yellow distance.\r\nThat was the beginning of the Bow Leg Mountains. Somewhere over there\r\nwere the red men, ranging in unfrequented depths of rock and pine--their\r\nforbidden ground.\r\n\r\nDinner was ready, and they sat down.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d Balaam continued, still hot on the subject, \xe2\x80\x9cyou'd\r\nclaim Indians object to killing a white man when they run on to him good\r\nand far from human help? These peaceable Indians are just the worst in\r\nthe business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's so,\xe2\x80\x9d assented the easy-opinioned Shorty, exactly as if he had\r\nalways maintained this view. \xe2\x80\x9cChap started for Sunk Creek three weeks\r\nago. Trapper he was; old like, with a red shirt. One of his horses come\r\ninto the round-up Toosday. Man ain't been heard from.\xe2\x80\x9d He ate in silence\r\nfor a while, evidently brooding in his childlike mind. Then he said,\r\nquerulously, \xe2\x80\x9cI'd sooner trust one of them Indians than I would\r\nTrampas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBalaam slanted his fat bullet head far to one side, and laying his spoon\r\ndown (he had opened some canned grapes) laughed steadily at his guest\r\nwith a harsh relish of irony.\r\n\r\nThe guest ate a grape, and perceiving he was seen through, smiled back\r\nrather miserably.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, Shorty,\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam, his head still slanted over, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat's the\r\nfigures of your bank balance just now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ain't usin' banks,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the youth.\r\n\r\nBalaam put some more grapes on Shorty's plate, and drawing a cigar from\r\nhis waistcoat, sent it rolling to his guest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMatches are behind you,\xe2\x80\x9d he added. He gave a cigar to the Virginian as\r\nan afterthought, but to his disgust, the Southerner put it in his pocket\r\nand lighted a pipe.\r\n\r\nBalaam accompanied his guest, Shorty, when he went to the pasture to\r\nsaddle up and depart. \xe2\x80\x9cGot a rope?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked the guest, as they lifted\r\ndown the bars.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't need to rope him. I can walk right up to Pedro. You stay back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHiding his bridle behind him, Shorty walked to the river-bank, where the\r\npony was switching his long tail in the shade; and speaking persuasively\r\nto him, he came nearer, till he laid his hand on Pedro's dusky mane,\r\nwhich was many shades darker than his hide. He turned expectantly, and\r\nhis master came up to his expectations with a piece of bread.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEats that, does he?\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam, over the bars.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLikes the salt,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, n-n-ow, here! Yu' don't guess\r\nyu'll be bridled, don't you? Open your teeth! Yu'd like to play yu' was\r\nnobody's horse and live private? Or maybe yu'd prefer ownin' a saloon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPedro evidently enjoyed this talk, and the dodging he made about the\r\nbit. Once fairly in his mouth, he accepted the inevitable, and followed\r\nShorty to the bars. Then Shorty turned and extended his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShake!\xe2\x80\x9d he said to his pony, who lifted his forefoot quietly and put it\r\nin his master's hand. Then the master tickled his nose, and he wrinkled\r\nit and flattened his ears, pretending to bite. His face wore an\r\nexpression of knowing relish over this performance. \xe2\x80\x9cNow the other\r\nhoof,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty; and the horse and master shook hands with their\r\nleft. \xe2\x80\x9cI learned him that,\xe2\x80\x9d said the cow-boy, with pride and affection.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, Pede,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, in Pedro's ear, \xe2\x80\x9cain't yu' the best little\r\nhorse in the country? What? Here, now! Keep out of that, you dead-beat!\r\nThere ain't no more bread.\xe2\x80\x9d He pinched the pony's nose, one quarter of\r\nwhich was wedged into his pocket.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite a lady's little pet!\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam, with the rasp in his voice.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPity this isn't New York, now, where there's a big market for harmless\r\nhorses. Gee-gees, the children call them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe ain't no gee-gee,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty, offended. \xe2\x80\x9cHe'll beat any cow-pony\r\nworkin' you've got. Yu' can turn him on a half-dollar. Don't need to\r\ntouch the reins. Hang 'em on one finger and swing your body, and he'll\r\nturn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBalaam knew this, and he knew that the pony was only a four-year-old.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cDrybone's had no circus this season. Maybe they'd buy\r\ntickets to see Pedro. He's good for that, anyway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShorty became gloomy. The Virginian was grimly smoking. Here was\r\nsomething else going on not to his taste, but none of his business.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTry a circus,\xe2\x80\x9d persisted Balaam. \xe2\x80\x9cAlter your plans for spending cash in\r\ntown, and make a little money instead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShorty having no plans to alter and no cash to spend, grew still more\r\ngloomy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat'll you take for that pony?\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam.\r\n\r\nShorty spoke up instantly. \xe2\x80\x9cA hundred dollars couldn't buy that piece\r\nof stale mud off his back,\xe2\x80\x9d he asserted, looking off into the sky\r\ngrandiosely.\r\n\r\nBut Balaam looked at Shorty, \xe2\x80\x9cYou keep the mud,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cand I'll give\r\nyou thirty dollars for the horse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShorty did a little professional laughing, and began to walk toward his\r\nsaddle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive you thirty dollars,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Balaam, picking a stone up and\r\nslinging it into the river.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow far do yu' call it to Drybone?\xe2\x80\x9d Shorty remarked, stooping to\r\ninvestigate the bucking-strap on his saddle--a superfluous performance,\r\nfor Pedro never bucked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou won't have to walk,\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam. \xe2\x80\x9cStay all night, and I'll send\r\nyou over comfortably in the morning, when the wagon goes for the mail.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWalk?\xe2\x80\x9d Shorty retorted. \xe2\x80\x9cDrybone's twenty-five miles.
