Pedro'll put me\r\nthere in three hours and not know he done it.\xe2\x80\x9d He lifted the saddle on\r\nthe horse's back. \xe2\x80\x9cCome, Pedro,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, Pedro!\xe2\x80\x9d mocked Balaam.\r\n\r\nThere followed a little silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d mumbled Shorty, with his head under Pedro's belly, busily\r\ncinching. \xe2\x80\x9cA hundred dollars is bottom figures.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBalaam, in his turn, now duly performed some professional laughing,\r\nwhich was noted by Shorty under the horse's belly. He stood up and\r\nsquared round on Balaam. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, then,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat'll yu give for\r\nhim?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThirty dollars,\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam, looking far off into the sky, as Shorty\r\nhad looked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, come, now,\xe2\x80\x9d expostulated Shorty.\r\n\r\nIt was he who now did the feeling for an offer and this was what Balaam\r\nliked to see. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy yes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cthirty,\xe2\x80\x9d and looked surprised that he\r\nshould have to mention the sum so often.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought yu'd quit them first figures,\xe2\x80\x9d said the cow-puncher, \xe2\x80\x9cfor yu'\r\ncan see I ain't goin' to look at em.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBalaam climbed on the fence and sat there \xe2\x80\x9cI'm not crying for your\r\nPedro,\xe2\x80\x9d he observed dispassionately. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly it struck me you were dead\r\nbroke, and wanted to raise cash and keep yourself going till you hunted\r\nup a job and could buy him back.\xe2\x80\x9d He hooked his right thumb inside his\r\nwaistcoat pocket. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I'm not cryin' for him,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cHe'd stay\r\nright here, of course. I wouldn't part with him. Why does he stand that\r\nway? Hello!\xe2\x80\x9d Balaam suddenly straightened himself, like a man who has\r\nmade a discovery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello, what?\xe2\x80\x9d said Shorty, on the defensive.\r\n\r\nBalaam was staring at Pedro with a judicial frown. Then he stuck out a\r\nfinger at the horse, keeping the thumb hooked in his pocket. So meagre\r\na gesture was felt by the ruffled Shorty to be no just way to point at\r\nPedro. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat's the matter with that foreleg there?\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich? Nothin's the matter with it!\xe2\x80\x9d snapped Shorty.\r\n\r\nBalaam climbed down from his fence and came over with elaborate\r\ndeliberation. He passed his hand up and down the off foreleg. Then he\r\nspit slenderly. \xe2\x80\x9cMm!\xe2\x80\x9d he said thoughtfully; and added, with a shade of\r\nsadness, \xe2\x80\x9cthat's always to be expected when they're worked too young.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShorty slid his hand slowly over the disputed leg. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat's to be\r\nexpected?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired--\xe2\x80\x9cthat they'll eat hearty? Well, he does.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this retort the Virginian permitted himself to laugh in audible\r\nsympathy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSprung,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Balaam, with a sigh. \xe2\x80\x9cWhirling round short when his\r\nbones were soft did that. Yes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSprung!\xe2\x80\x9d Shorty said, with a bark of indignation. \xe2\x80\x9cCome on, Pede; you\r\nand me'll spring for town.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe caught the horn of the saddle, and as he swung into place the horse\r\nrushed away with him. \xe2\x80\x9cO-ee! yoi-yup, yup, yup!\xe2\x80\x9d sang Shorty, in the\r\nshrill cow dialect. He made Pedro play an exhibition game of speed,\r\nbringing him round close to Balaam in a wide circle, and then he\r\nvanished in dust down the left-bank trail.\r\n\r\nBalaam looked after him and laughed harshly. He had seen trout dash\r\nabout like that when the hook in their jaw first surprised them. He knew\r\nShorty would show the pony off, and he knew Shorty's love for Pedro\r\nwas not equal to his need of money. He called to one of his men, asked\r\nsomething about the dam at the mouth of the canyon, where the main\r\nirrigation ditch began, made a remark about the prolonged drought, and\r\nthen walked to his dining-room door, where, as he expected, Shorty met\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay,\xe2\x80\x9d said the youth, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you consider that's any way to talk about a\r\ngood horse?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAny dude could see the leg's sprung,\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam. But he looked at\r\nPedro's shoulder, which was well laid back; and he admired his points,\r\ndark in contrast with the buckskin, and also the width between the eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow you know,\xe2\x80\x9d whined Shorty, \xe2\x80\x9cthat it ain't sprung any more than your\r\nleg's cork. If you mean the right leg ain't plumb straight, I can tell\r\nyou he was born so. That don't make no difference, for it ain't weak.\r\nTry him onced. Just as sound and strong as iron. Never stumbles. And he\r\ndon't never go to jumpin' with yu'. He's kind and he's smart.\xe2\x80\x9d And the\r\nmaster petted his pony, who lifted a hoof for another handshake.\r\n\r\nOf course Balaam had never thought the leg was sprung, and he now took\r\non an unprejudiced air of wanting to believe Shorty's statements if he\r\nonly could.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe there's two years' work left in that leg,\xe2\x80\x9d he now observed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetter give your hawss away, Shorty,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs this your deal, my friend?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Balaam. And he slanted his\r\nbullet head at the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive him away, Shorty,\xe2\x80\x9d drawled the Southerner. \xe2\x80\x9cHis laig is busted.\r\nMr. Balaam says so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBalaam's face grew evil with baffled fury. But the Virginian was gravely\r\nconsidering Pedro. He, too, was not pleased. But he could not interfere.\r\nAlready he had overstepped the code in these matters. He would have\r\ndearly liked--for reasons good and bad, spite and mercy mingled--to\r\nhave spoiled Balaam's market, to have offered a reasonable or even an\r\nunreasonable price for Pedro, and taken possession of the horse himself.\r\nBut this might not be. In bets, in card games, in all horse transactions\r\nand other matters of similar business, a man must take care of himself,\r\nand wiser onlookers must suppress their wisdom and hold their peace.\r\n\r\nThat evening Shorty again had a cigar. He had parted with Pedro\r\nfor forty dollars, a striped Mexican blanket, and a pair of spurs.\r\nUndressing over in the bunk house, he said to the Virginian, \xe2\x80\x9cI'll sure\r\nbuy Pedro back off him just as soon as ever I rustle some cash.\xe2\x80\x9d The\r\nVirginian grunted. He was thinking he should have to travel hard to\r\nget the horses to the Judge by the 30th; and below that thought lay his\r\naching disappointment and his longing for Bear Creek.\r\n\r\nIn the early dawn Shorty sat up among his blankets on the floor of the\r\nbunk house and saw the various sleepers coiled or sprawled in their\r\nbeds; their breathing had not yet grown restless at the nearing of day.\r\nHe stepped to the door carefully, and saw the crowding blackbirds begin\r\ntheir walk and chatter in the mud of the littered and trodden corrals.\r\nFrom beyond among the cottonwoods, came continually the smooth\r\nunemphatic sound of the doves answering each other invisibly; and\r\nagainst the empty ridge of the river-bluff lay the moon, no longer\r\nshining, for there was established a new light through the sky. Pedro\r\nstood in the pasture close to the bars. The cow-boy slowly closed the\r\ndoor behind him, and sitting down on the step, drew his money out and\r\nidly handled it, taking no comfort just then from its possession. Then\r\nhe put it back, and after dragging on his boots, crossed to the pasture,\r\nand held a last talk with his pony, brushing the cakes of mud from his\r\nhide where he had rolled, and passing a lingering hand over his mane. As\r\nthe sounds of the morning came increasingly from tree and plain, Shorty\r\nglanced back to see that no one was yet out of the cabin, and then put\r\nhis arms round the horse's neck, laying his head against him. For a\r\nmoment the cow-boy's insignificant face was exalted by the emotion he\r\nwould never have let others see. He hugged tight this animal, who was\r\ndearer to his heart than anybody in the world.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-by, Pedro,\xe2\x80\x9d he said--\xe2\x80\x9cgood-by.\xe2\x80\x9d Pedro looked for bread.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said his master, sorrowfully, \xe2\x80\x9cnot any more. Yu' know well I'd\r\ngive it yu' if I had it. You and me didn't figure on this, did we,\r\nPedro? Good-by!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe hugged his pony again, and got as far as the bars of the pasture, but\r\nreturned once more. \xe2\x80\x9cGood-by, my little horse, my dear horse, my little,\r\nlittle Pedro,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as his tears wet the pony's neck. Then he\r\nwiped them with his hand, and got himself back to the bunk house. After\r\nbreakfast he and his belongings departed to Drybone, and Pedro from his\r\nfield calmly watched this departure; for horses must recognize even less\r\nthan men the black corners that their destinies turn. The pony stopped\r\nfeeding to look at the mail-wagon pass by; but the master sitting in the\r\nwagon forebore to turn his head.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXVI. BALAAM AND PEDRO\r\n\r\n\r\nResigned to wait for the Judge's horses, Balaam went into his office\r\nthis dry, bright morning and read nine accumulated newspapers; for\r\nhe was behindhand. Then he rode out on the ditches, and met his man\r\nreturning with the troublesome animals at last. He hastened home and\r\nsent for the Virginian. He had made a decision.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee here,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cthose horses are coming. What trail would you take\r\nover to the Judge's?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShortest trail's right through the Bow Laig Mountains,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nforeman, in his gentle voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGuess you're right. It's dinner-time. We'll start right afterward.\r\nWe'll make Little Muddy Crossing by sundown, and Sunk Creek to-morrow,\r\nand the next day'll see us through. Can a wagon get through Sunk Creek\r\nCanyon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Virginian smiled. \xe2\x80\x9cI reckon it can't, seh, and stay resembling a\r\nwagon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBalaam told them to saddle Pedro and one packhorse, and drive the bunch\r\nof horses into a corral, roping the Judge's two, who proved extremely\r\nwild. He had decided to take this journey himself on remembering certain\r\npolitics soon to be rife in Cheyenne. For Judge Henry was indeed a\r\ngreater man than Balaam. This personally conducted return of the horses\r\nwould temper its tardiness, and, moreover, the sight of some New York\r\nvisitors would be a good thing after seven months of no warmer touch\r\nwith that metropolis than the Sunday HERALD, always eight days old when\r\nit reached the Butte Creek Ranch.\r\n\r\nThey forded Butte Creek, and, crossing the well-travelled trail which\r\nfollows down to Drybone, turned their faces toward the uninhabited\r\ncountry that began immediately, as the ocean begins off a sandy shore.\r\nAnd as a single mast on which no sail is shining stands at the horizon\r\nand seems to add a loneliness to the surrounding sea, so the long gray\r\nline of fence, almost a mile away, that ended Balaam's land on this side\r\nthe creek, stretched along the waste ground and added desolation to\r\nthe plain. No solitary watercourse with margin of cottonwoods or willow\r\nthickets flowed here to stripe the dingy, yellow world with interrupting\r\ngreen, nor were cattle to be seen dotting the distance, nor moving\r\nobjects at all, nor any bird in the soundless air. The last gate was\r\nshut by the Virginian, who looked back at the pleasant trees of the\r\nranch, and then followed on in single file across the alkali of No Man's\r\nLand.\r\n\r\nNo cloud was in the sky. The desert's grim noon shone sombrely on flat\r\nand hill. The sagebrush was dull like zinc. Thick heat rose near at hand\r\nfrom the caked alkali, and pale heat shrouded the distant peaks.\r\n\r\nThere were five horses. Balaam led on Pedro, his squat figure stiff in\r\nthe saddle, but solid as a rock, and tilted a little forward, as his\r\nhabit was. One of the Judge's horses came next, a sorrel, dragging back\r\ncontinually on the rope by which he was led. After him ambled Balaam's\r\nwise pack-animal, carrying the light burden of two days' food and\r\nlodging. She was an old mare who could still go when she chose, but had\r\nbeen schooled by the years, and kept the trail, giving no trouble to the\r\nVirginian who came behind her. He also sat solid as a rock, yet subtly\r\nbending to the struggles of the wild horse he led, as a steel spring\r\nbends and balances and resumes its poise.\r\n\r\nThus they made but slow time, and when they topped the last dull rise of\r\nground and looked down on the long slant of ragged, caked earth to the\r\ncrossing of Little Muddy, with its single tree and few mean bushes, the\r\nfinal distance where eyesight ends had deepened to violet from the thin,\r\nsteady blue they had stared at for so many hours, and all heat was\r\ngone from the universal dryness. The horses drank a long time from the\r\nsluggish yellow water, and its alkaline taste and warmth were equally\r\nwelcome to the men. They built a little fire, and when supper was ended,\r\nsmoked but a short while and in silence, before they got in the blankets\r\nthat were spread in a smooth place beside the water.\r\n\r\nThey had picketed the two horses of the Judge in the best grass they\r\ncould find, letting the rest go free to find pasture where they could.\r\nWhen the first light came, the Virginian attended to breakfast, while\r\nBalaam rode away on the sorrel to bring in the loose horses. They had\r\ngone far out of sight, and when he returned with them, after some two\r\nhours, he was on Pedro. Pedro was soaking with sweat, and red froth\r\ncreamed from his mouth. The Virginian saw the horses must have been hard\r\nto drive in, especially after Balaam brought them the wild sorrel as a\r\nleader.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you'd kep' ridin' him, 'stead of changin' off on your hawss, they'd\r\nhave behaved quieter,\xe2\x80\x9d said the foreman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's good seasonable advice,\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam, sarcastically. \xe2\x80\x9cI could\r\nhave told you that now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could have told you when you started,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, heating\r\nthe coffee for Balaam.\r\n\r\nBalaam was eloquent on the outrageous conduct of the horses. He had come\r\nup with them evidently striking back for Butte Creek, with the old mare\r\nin the lead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I soon showed her the road she was to go,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as he drove\r\nthem now to the water.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian noticed the slight limp of the mare, and how her pastern\r\nwas cut as if with a stone or the sharp heel of a boot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI guess she'll not be in a hurry to travel except when she's wanted\r\nto,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Balaam. He sat down, and sullenly poured himself some\r\ncoffee. \xe2\x80\x9cWe'll be in luck if we make any Sunk Creek this night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe went on with his breakfast, thinking aloud for the benefit of his\r\ncompanion, who made no comments, preferring silence to the discomfort of\r\ntalking with a man whose vindictive humor was so thoroughly uppermost.\r\nHe did not even listen very attentively, but continued his preparations\r\nfor departure, washing the dishes, rolling the blankets, and moving\r\nabout in his usual way of easy and visible good nature.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSix o'clock, already,\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam, saddling the horses. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd we'll\r\nnot get started for ten minutes more.\xe2\x80\x9d Then he came to Pedro. \xe2\x80\x9cSo you\r\nhaven't quit fooling yet, haven't you?\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, for the pony\r\nshrank as he lifted the bridle. \xe2\x80\x9cTake that for your sore mouth!\xe2\x80\x9d and he\r\nrammed the bit in, at which Pedro flung back and reared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I never saw Pedro act that way yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, rubbish!\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam. \xe2\x80\x9cThey're all the same. Not a bastard one\r\nbut's laying for his chance to do for you. Some'll buck you off, and\r\nsome'll roll with you, and some'll fight you with their fore feet. They\r\nmay play good for a year, but the Western pony's man's enemy, and when\r\nhe judges he's got his chance, he's going to do his best. And if you\r\ncome out alive it won't be his fault.\xe2\x80\x9d Balaam paused for a while,\r\npacking. \xe2\x80\x9cYou've got to keep them afraid of you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said next; \xe2\x80\x9cthat's\r\nwhat you've got to do if you don't want trouble. That Pedro horse there\r\nhas been fed, hand-fed, and fooled with like a damn pet, and what's that\r\npolicy done? Why, he goes ugly when he thinks it's time, and decides\r\nhe'll not drive any horses into camp this morning. He knows better now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Balaam,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, \xe2\x80\x9cI'll buy that hawss off yu' right\r\nnow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBalaam shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cYou'll not do that right now or any other time,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said he. \xe2\x80\x9cI happen to want him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Virginian could do no more. He had heard cow-punchers say to\r\nrefractory ponies, \xe2\x80\x9cYou keep still, or I'll Balaam you!\xe2\x80\x9d and he now\r\nunderstood the aptness of the expression.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile Balaam began to lead Pedro to the creek for a last drink\r\nbefore starting across the torrid drought. The horse held back on the\r\nrein a little, and Balaam turned and cut the whip across his forehead.\r\nA delay of forcing and backing followed, while the Virginian, already\r\nin the saddle, waited. The minutes passed, and no immediate prospect,\r\napparently, of getting nearer Sunk Creek.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe ain' goin' to follow you while you're beatin' his haid,\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\nSoutherner at length remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think you can teach me anything about horses?\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Balaam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it don't look like I could,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, lazily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen don't try it, so long as it's not your horse, my friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain the Southerner levelled his eye on Balaam. \xe2\x80\x9cAll right,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\nin the same gentle voice. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd don't you call me your friend. You've\r\nmade that mistake twiced.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe road was shadeless, as it had been from the start, and they could\r\nnot travel fast. During the first few hours all coolness was driven out\r\nof the glassy morning, and another day of illimitable sun invested the\r\nworld with its blaze. The pale Bow Leg Range was coming nearer, but its\r\nhard hot slants and rifts suggested no sort of freshness, and even\r\nthe pines that spread for wide miles along near the summit counted for\r\nnothing in the distance and the glare, but seemed mere patches of dull\r\ndry discoloration. No talk was exchanged between the two travellers, for\r\nthe cow-puncher had nothing to say and Balaam was sulky, so they moved\r\nalong in silent endurance of each other's company and the tedium of the\r\njourney.\r\n\r\nBut the slow succession of rise and fall in the plain changed and\r\nshortened. The earth's surface became lumpy, rising into mounds and\r\nknotted systems of steep small hills cut apart by staring gashes of\r\nsand, where water poured in the spring from the melting snow. After a\r\ntime they ascended through the foot-hills till the plain below was for a\r\nwhile concealed, but came again into view in its entirety, distant and a\r\nthing of the past, while some magpies sailed down to meet them from\r\nthe new country they were entering. They passed up through a small\r\ntransparent forest of dead trees standing stark and white, and a little\r\nhigher came on a line of narrow moisture that crossed the way and formed\r\na stale pool among some willow thickets. They turned aside to water\r\ntheir horses, and found near the pool a circular spot of ashes and some\r\npoles lying, and beside these a cage-like edifice of willow wands built\r\nin the ground.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndian camp,\xe2\x80\x9d observed the Virginian.\r\n\r\nThere were the tracks of five or six horses on the farther side of the\r\npool, and they did not come into the trail, but led off among the rocks\r\non some system of their own.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey're about a week old,\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam. \xe2\x80\x9cIt's part of that outfit\r\nthat's been hunting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey've gone on to visit their friends,\xe2\x80\x9d added the cow-puncher.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, on the Southern Reservation. How far do you call Sunk Creek now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, calculating, \xe2\x80\x9cit's mighty nigh fo'ty miles\r\nfrom Muddy Crossin', an' I reckon we've come eighteen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust about. It's noon.\xe2\x80\x9d Balaam snapped his watch shut. \xe2\x80\x9cWe'll rest here\r\ntill 12:30.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen it was time to go, the Virginian looked musingly at the mountains.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe'll need to travel right smart to get through the canyon to-night,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell you what,\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam; \xe2\x80\x9cwe'll rope the Judge's horses together\r\nand drive 'em in front of us. That'll make speed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMightn't they get away on us?\xe2\x80\x9d objected the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cThey're pow'ful\r\nwild.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey can't get away from me, I guess,\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam, and the arrangement\r\nwas adopted. \xe2\x80\x9cWe're the first this season over this piece of the trail,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n he observed presently.\r\n\r\nHis companion had noticed the ground already, and assented. There were\r\nno tracks anywhere to be seen over which winter had not come and gone\r\nsince they had been made. Presently the trail wound into a sultry gulch\r\nthat hemmed in the heat and seemed to draw down the sun's rays more\r\nvertically. The sorrel horse chose this place to make a try for liberty.\r\nHe suddenly whirled from the trail, dragging with him his less inventive\r\nfellow. Leaving the Virginian with the old mare, Balaam headed them off,\r\nfor Pedro was quick, and they came jumping down the bank together, but\r\nswiftly crossed up on the other side, getting much higher before they\r\ncould be reached. It was no place for this sort of game, as the sides of\r\nthe ravine were ploughed with steep channels, broken with jutting knobs\r\nof rock, and impeded by short twisted pines that swung out from their\r\nroots horizontally over the pitch of the hill. The Virginian helped,\r\nbut used his horse with more judgment, keeping as much on the level as\r\npossible, and endeavoring to anticipate the next turn of the runaways\r\nbefore they made it, while Balaam attempted to follow them close,\r\nwheeling short when they doubled, heavily beating up the face of the\r\nslope, veering again to come down to the point he had left, and whenever\r\nhe felt Pedro begin to flag, driving his spurs into the horse and\r\nforcing him to keep up the pace. He had set out to overtake and capture\r\non the side of the mountain these two animals who had been running\r\nwild for many weeks, and now carried no weight but themselves, and\r\nthe futility of such work could not penetrate his obstinate and rising\r\ntemper. He had made up his mind not to give in. The Virginian soon\r\ndecided to move slowly along for the present, preventing the wild horses\r\nfrom passing down the gulch again, but otherwise saving his own animal\r\nfrom useless fatigue. He saw that Pedro was reeking wet, with mouth\r\nopen, and constantly stumbling, though he galloped on. The cow-puncher\r\nkept the group in sight, driving the packhorse in front of him, and\r\nwatching the tactics of the sorrel, who had now undoubtedly become\r\nthe leader of the expedition, and was at the top of the gulch, in vain\r\ntrying to find an outlet through its rocky rim to the levels above. He\r\nsoon judged this to be no thoroughfare, and changing his plan, trotted\r\ndown to the bottom and up the other side, gaining more and more; for\r\nin this new descent Pedro had fallen twice. Then the sorrel showed the\r\ncleverness of a genuinely vicious horse. The Virginian saw him stop\r\nand fall to kicking his companion with all the energy that a short rope\r\nwould permit. The rope slipped, and both, unencumbered, reached the top\r\nand disappeared. Leaving the packhorse for Balaam, the Virginian started\r\nafter them and came into a high tableland, beyond which the mountains\r\nbegan in earnest. The runaways were moving across toward these at an\r\neasy rate. He followed for a moment, then looking back, and seeing no\r\nsign of Balaam, waited, for the horses were sure not to go fast when\r\nthey reached good pasture or water.\r\n\r\nHe got out of the saddle and sat on the ground, watching, till the mare\r\ncame up slowly into sight, and Balaam behind her. When they were near,\r\nBalaam dismounted and struck Pedro fearfully, until the stick broke, and\r\nhe raised the splintered half to continue.\r\n\r\nSeeing the pony's condition, the Virginian spoke, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cI'd let\r\nthat hawss alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBalaam turned to him, but wholly possessed by passion did not seem to\r\nhear, and the Southerner noticed how white and like that of a maniac his\r\nface was. The stick slid to the ground.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe played he was tired,\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam, looking at the Virginian with\r\nglazed eyes. The violence of his rage affected him physically, like some\r\nstroke of illness. \xe2\x80\x9cHe played out on me on purpose.\xe2\x80\x9d The man's voice\r\nwas dry and light. \xe2\x80\x9cHe's perfectly fresh now,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, and turned\r\nagain to the coughing, swaying horse, whose eyes were closed. Not having\r\nthe stick, he seized the animal's unresisting head and shook it. The\r\nVirginian watched him a moment, and rose to stop such a spectacle. Then,\r\nas if conscious he was doing no real hurt, Balaam ceased, and turning\r\nagain in slow fashion looked across the level, where the runaways were\r\nstill visible.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll have to take your horse,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cmine's played out on me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ain' goin' to touch my hawss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain the words seemed not entirely to reach Balaam's understanding, so\r\ndulled by rage were his senses. He made no answer, but mounted Pedro;\r\nand the failing pony walked mechanically forward, while the Virginian,\r\npuzzled, stood looking after him. Balaam seemed without purpose of going\r\nanywhere, and stopped in a moment. Suddenly he was at work at something.\r\nThis sight was odd and new to look at. For a few seconds it had no\r\nmeaning to the Virginian as he watched. Then his mind grasped the\r\nhorror, too late. Even with his cry of execration and the tiger spring\r\nthat he gave to stop Balaam, the monstrosity was wrought. Pedro sank\r\nmotionless, his head rolling flat on the earth. Balaam was jammed\r\nbeneath him. The man had struggled to his feet before the Virginian\r\nreached the spot, and the horse then lifted his head and turned it\r\npiteously round.\r\n\r\nThen vengeance like a blast struck Balaam. The Virginian hurled him to\r\nthe ground, lifted and hurled him again, lifted him and beat his face\r\nand struck his jaw. The man's strong ox-like fighting availed nothing.\r\nHe fended his eyes as best he could against these sledge-hammer blows\r\nof justice. He felt blindly for his pistol. That arm was caught and\r\nwrenched backward, and crushed and doubled. He seemed to hear his own\r\nbones, and set up a hideous screaming of hate and pain. Then the\r\npistol at last came out, and together with the hand that grasped it was\r\ninstantly stamped into the dust. Once again the creature was lifted and\r\nslung so that he lay across Pedro's saddle a blurred, dingy, wet pulp.\r\n\r\nVengeance had come and gone. The man and the horse were motionless.\r\nAround them, silence seemed to gather like a witness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you are dead,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, \xe2\x80\x9cI am glad of it.\xe2\x80\x9d He stood\r\nlooking down at Balaam and Pedro, prone in the middle of the open\r\ntableland. Then he saw Balaam looking at him. It was the quiet stare of\r\nsight without thought or feeling, the mere visual sense alone, almost\r\nfrightful in its separation from any self. But as he watched those\r\neyes, the self came back into them. \xe2\x80\x9cI have not killed you,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nVirginian. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I ain't goin' to do any more to yu'--if that's a\r\nsatisfaction to know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he began to attend to Balaam with impersonal skill, like some one\r\nhired for the purpose. \xe2\x80\x9cHe ain't hurt bad,\xe2\x80\x9d he asserted aloud, as if\r\nthe man were some nameless patient; and then to Balaam he remarked, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nreckon it might have put a less tough man than you out of business for\r\nquite a while. I'm goin' to get some water now.\xe2\x80\x9d When he returned with\r\nthe water, Balsam was sitting up, looking about him. He had not yet\r\nspoken, nor did he now speak. The sunlight flashed on the six-shooter\r\nwhere it lay, and the Virginian secured it. \xe2\x80\x9cShe ain't so pretty as she\r\nwas,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked, as he examined the weapon. \xe2\x80\x9cBut she'll go right handy\r\nyet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStrength was in a measure returning to Pedro. He was a young horse,\r\nand the exhaustion neither of anguish nor of over-riding was enough\r\nto affect him long or seriously. He got himself on his feet and walked\r\nwaveringly over to the old mare, and stood by her for comfort. The\r\ncow-puncher came up to him, and Pedro, after starting back slightly,\r\nseemed to comprehend that he was in friendly hands. It was plain that he\r\nwould soon be able to travel slowly if no weight was on him, and that he\r\nwould be a very good horse again. Whether they abandoned the runaways or\r\nnot, there was no staying here for night to overtake them without food\r\nor water. The day was still high, and what its next few hours had in\r\nstore the Virginian could not say, and he left them to take care of\r\nthemselves, determining meanwhile that he would take command of the\r\nminutes and maintain the position he had assumed both as to Balaam and\r\nPedro. He took Pedro's saddle off, threw the mare's pack to the ground,\r\nput Balaam's saddle on her, and on that stowed or tied her original\r\npack, which he could do, since it was so light. Then he went to Balaam,\r\nwho was sitting up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon you can travel,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd your hawss can. If\r\nyou're comin' with me, you'll ride your mare. I'm goin' to trail them\r\nhawsses. If you're not comin' with me, your hawss comes with me, and\r\nyou'll take fifty dollars for him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBalaam was indifferent to this good bargain. He did not look at the\r\nother or speak, but rose and searched about him on the ground. The\r\nVirginian was also indifferent as to whether Balaam chose to answer or\r\nnot. Seeing Balaam searching the ground, he finished what he had to say.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have your six-shooter, and you'll have it when I'm ready for you to.\r\nNow, I'm goin',\xe2\x80\x9d he concluded.\r\n\r\nBalaam's intellect was clear enough now, and he saw that though the rest\r\nof this journey would be nearly intolerable, it must go on. He looked\r\nat the impassive cow-puncher getting ready to go and tying a rope on\r\nPedro's neck to lead him, then he looked at the mountains where the\r\nrunaways had vanished, and it did not seem credible to him that he had\r\ncome into such straits. He was helped stiffly on the mare, and the three\r\nhorses in single file took up their journey once more, and came slowly\r\namong the mountains. The perpetual desert was ended, and they crossed a\r\nsmall brook, where they missed the trail. The Virginian dismounted to\r\nfind where the horses had turned off, and discovered that they had gone\r\nstraight up the ridge by the watercourse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere's been a man camped in hyeh inside a month,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, kicking up\r\na rag of red flannel. \xe2\x80\x9cWhite man and two hawsses. Ours have went up his\r\nold tracks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was not easy for Balaam to speak yet, and he kept his silence. But he\r\nremembered that Shorty had spoken of a trapper who had started for Sunk\r\nCreek.\r\n\r\nFor three hours they followed the runaways' course over softer ground,\r\nand steadily ascending, passed one or two springs, at length, where\r\nthe mud was not yet settled in the hoofprints. Then they came through\r\na corner of pine forest and down a sudden bank among quaking-asps to a\r\ngreen park. Here the runaways beside a stream were grazing at ease, but\r\nsaw them coming, and started on again, following down the stream.\r\nFor the present all to be done was to keep them in sight. This creek\r\nreceived tributaries and widened, making a valley for itself. Above\r\nthe bottom, lining the first terrace of the ridge, began the pines, and\r\nstretched back, unbroken over intervening summit and basin, to cease at\r\nlast where the higher peaks presided.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis hyeh's the middle fork of Sunk Creek,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cWe'll\r\nget on to our right road again where they join.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSoon a game trail marked itself along the stream. If this would only\r\ncontinue, the runaways would be nearly sure to follow it down into the\r\ncanyon. Then there would be no way for them but to go on and come out\r\ninto their own country, where they would make for the Judge's ranch of\r\ntheir own accord. The great point was to reach the canyon before dark.\r\nThey passed into permanent shadow; for though the other side of\r\nthe creek shone in full day, the sun had departed behind the ridges\r\nimmediately above them. Coolness filled the air, and the silence, which\r\nin this deep valley of invading shadow seemed too silent, was relieved\r\nby the birds. Not birds of song, but a freakish band of gray talkative\r\nobservers, who came calling and croaking along through the pines, and\r\ninspected the cavalcade, keeping it company for a while, and then flying\r\nup into the woods again. The travellers came round a corner on a little\r\nspread of marsh, and from somewhere in the middle of it rose a buzzard\r\nand sailed on its black pinions into the air above them, wheeling\r\nand wheeling, but did not grow distant. As it swept over the trail,\r\nsomething fell from its claw, a rag of red flannel; and each man in turn\r\nlooked at it as his horse went by.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if there's plenty elk and deer hyeh?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI guess there is,\xe2\x80\x9d Balaam replied, speaking at last. The travellers had\r\nbecome strangely reconciled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere's game 'most all over these mountains,\xe2\x80\x9d the Virginian continued;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ccountry not been settled long enough to scare them out.\xe2\x80\x9d So they fell\r\ninto casual conversation, and for the first time were glad of each\r\nother's company.\r\n\r\nThe sound of a new bird came from the pines above--the hoot of an\r\nowl--and was answered from some other part of the wood. This they did\r\nnot particularly notice at first, but soon they heard the same note,\r\nunexpectedly distant, like an echo. The game trail, now quite a defined\r\npath beside the river, showed no sign of changing its course or fading\r\nout into blank ground, as these uncertain guides do so often. It led\r\nconsistently in the desired direction, and the two men were relieved to\r\nsee it continue. Not only were the runaways easier to keep track of,\r\nbut better speed was made along this valley. The pervading imminence of\r\nnight more and more dispelled the lingering afternoon, though there was\r\nyet no twilight in the open, and the high peaks opposite shone yellow\r\nin the invisible sun. But now the owls hooted again. Their music had\r\nsomething in it that caused both the Virginian and Balaam to look up at\r\nthe pines and wish that this valley would end. Perhaps it was early for\r\nnight-birds to begin; or perhaps it was that the sound never seemed to\r\nfall behind, but moved abreast of them among the trees above, as they\r\nrode on without pause down below; some influence made the faces of the\r\ntravellers grave. The spell of evil which the sight of the wheeling\r\nbuzzard had begun, deepened as evening grew, while ever and again along\r\nthe creek the singular call and answer of the owls wandered among the\r\ndarkness of the trees not far away.\r\n\r\nThe sun was gone from the peaks when at length the other side of the\r\nstream opened into a long wide meadow. The trail they followed, after\r\ncrossing a flat willow thicket by the water, ran into dense pines, that\r\nhere for the first time reached all the way down to the water's edge.\r\nThe two men came out of the willows, and saw ahead the capricious\r\nrunaways leave the bottom and go up the hill and enter the wood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must hinder that,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian; and he dropped Pedro's rope.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere's your six-shooter. You keep the trail, and camp down there\xe2\x80\x9d--he\r\npointed to where the trees came to the water--\xe2\x80\x9ctill I head them hawsses\r\noff. I may not get back right away.\xe2\x80\x9d He galloped up the open hill\r\nand went into the pine, choosing a place above where the vagrants had\r\ndisappeared.\r\n\r\nBalaam dismounted, and picking up his six-shooter, took the rope off\r\nPedro's neck and drove him slowly down toward where the wood began.\r\nIts interior was already dim, and Balaam saw that here must be their\r\nstopping-place to-night, since there was no telling how wide this pine\r\nstrip might extend along the trail before they could come out of it and\r\nreach another suitable camping-ground. Pedro had recovered his strength,\r\nand he now showed signs of restlessness. He shied where there was not\r\neven a stone in the trail, and finally turned sharply round. Balaam\r\nexpected he was going to rush back on the way they had come; but the\r\nhorse stood still, breathing excitedly. He was urged forward again,\r\nthough he turned more than once. But when they were a few paces from the\r\nwood, and Balaam had got off preparatory to camping, the horse snorted\r\nand dashed into the water, and stood still there. The astonished Balaam\r\nfollowed to turn him; but Pedro seemed to lose control of himself,\r\nand plunged to the middle of the river, and was evidently intending to\r\ncross. Fearing that he would escape to the opposite meadow and add to\r\ntheir difficulties, Balaam, with the idea of turning him round, drew his\r\nsix-shooter and fired in front of the horse, divining, even as the flash\r\ncut the dusk, the secret of all this--the Indians; but too late. His\r\nbruised hand had stiffened, marring his aim, and he saw Pedro fall over\r\nin the water then rise and struggle up the bank on the farther shore,\r\nwhere he now hurried also, to find that he had broken the pony's leg.\r\n\r\nHe needed no interpreter for the voices of the seeming owls that had\r\nhaunted the latter hour of their journey, and he knew that his beast's\r\nkeener instinct had perceived the destruction that lurked in the\r\ninterior of the wood. The history of the trapper whose horse had\r\nreturned without him might have been--might still be--his own; and he\r\nthought of the rag that had fallen from the buzzard's talons when he had\r\nbeen disturbed at his meal in the marsh. \xe2\x80\x9cPeaceable\xe2\x80\x9d Indians were still\r\nin these mountains, and some few of them had for the past hour been\r\nskirting his journey unseen, and now waited for him in the wood which\r\nthey expected him to enter. They had been too wary to use their rifles\r\nor show themselves, lest these travellers should be only part of a\r\nlarger company following, who would hear the noise of a shot, and catch\r\nthem in the act of murder. So, safe under the cover of the pines, they\r\nhad planned to sling their silent noose, and drag the white man from his\r\nhorse as he passed through the trees.\r\n\r\nBalaam looked over the river at the ominous wood, and then he looked\r\nat Pedro, the horse that he had first maimed and now ruined, to whom he\r\nprobably owed his life. He was lying on the ground, quietly looking over\r\nthe green meadow, where dusk was gathering. Perhaps he was not suffering\r\nfrom his wound yet, as he rested on the ground; and into his animal\r\nintelligence there probably came no knowledge of this final stroke of\r\nhis fate. At any rate, no sound of pain came from Pedro, whose friendly\r\nand gentle face remained turned toward the meadow. Once more Balaam\r\nfired his pistol, and this time the aim was true, and the horse rolled\r\nover, with a ball through his brain. It was the best reward that\r\nremained for him.\r\n\r\nThen Balaam rejoined the old mare, and turned from the middle fork of\r\nSunk Creek. He dashed across the wide field, and went over a ridge, and\r\nfound his way along in the night till he came to the old trail--the\r\nroad which they would never have left but for him and his obstinacy. He\r\nunsaddled the weary mare by Sunk Creek, where the canyon begins, letting\r\nher drag a rope and find pasture and water, while he, lighting no fire\r\nto betray him, crouched close under a tree till the light came. He\r\nthought of the Virginian in the wood. But what could either have done\r\nfor the other had he stayed to look for him among the pines? If the\r\ncow-puncher came back to the corner, he would follow Balaam's tracks or\r\nnot. They would meet, at any rate, where the creeks joined.\r\n\r\nBut they did not meet. And then to Balaam the prospect of going onward\r\nto the Sunk Creek Ranch became more than he could bear. To come without\r\nthe horses, to meet Judge Henry, to meet the guests of the Judge's,\r\nlooking as he did now after his punishment by the Virginian, to give the\r\nnews about the Judge's favorite man--no, how could he tell such a story\r\nas this? Balaam went no farther than a certain cabin, where he slept,\r\nand wrote a letter to the Judge. This the owner of the cabin delivered.\r\nAnd so, having spread news which would at once cause a search for the\r\nVirginian, and having constructed such sentences to the Judge as would\r\nmost smoothly explain how, being overtaken by illness, he had not wished\r\nto be a burden at Sunk Creek, Balaam turned homeward by himself. By the\r\ntime he was once more at Butte Creek, his general appearance was a thing\r\nless to be noticed. And there was Shorty, waiting!\r\n\r\nOne way and another, the lost dog had been able to gather some ready\r\nmoney. He was cheerful because of this momentary purseful of prosperity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so I come back, yu' see,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cFor I figured on getting Pedro\r\nback as soon as I could when I sold him to yu'.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou're behind the times, Shorty,\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam.\r\n\r\nShorty looked blank. \xe2\x80\x9cYou've sure not sold Pedro?\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThem Indians,\xe2\x80\x9d said Balaam, \xe2\x80\x9cgot after me on the Bow Leg trail. Got\r\nafter me and that Virginia man. But they didn't get me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBalaam wagged his bullet head to imply that this escape was due to his\r\nown superior intelligence. The Virginian had been stupid, and so the\r\nIndians had got him. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd they shot your horse,\xe2\x80\x9d Balaam finished. \xe2\x80\x9cStop\r\nand get some dinner with the boys.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHaving eaten, Shorty rode away in mournful spirits. For he had made so\r\nsure of once more riding and talking with Pedro, his friend whom he had\r\ntaught to shake hands.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXVII. GRANDMOTHER STARK\r\n\r\n\r\nExcept for its chair and bed, the cabin was stripped almost bare. Amid\r\nits emptiness of dismantled shelves and walls and floor, only the tiny\r\nancestress still hung in her place, last token of the home that had\r\nbeen. This miniature, tacked against the despoiled boards, and its\r\ndescendant, the angry girl with her hand on an open box-lid, made a sort\r\nof couple in the loneliness: she on the wall sweet and serene, she by\r\nthe box sweet and stormy. The picture was her final treasure waiting to\r\nbe packed for the journey. In whatever room she had called her own\r\nsince childhood, there it had also lived and looked at her, not quite\r\nfamiliar, not quite smiling, but in its prim colonial hues delicate as\r\nsome pressed flower. Its pale oval, of color blue and rose and\r\nflaxen, in a battered, pretty gold frame, unconquerably pervaded any\r\nsurroundings with a something like last year's lavender. Till yesterday\r\na Crow Indian war-bonnet had hung next it, a sumptuous cascade of\r\nfeathers; on the other side a bow with arrows had dangled; opposite had\r\nbeen the skin of a silver fox; over the door had spread the antlers of\r\na black-tail deer; a bearskin stretched beneath it. Thus had the whole\r\ncosey log cabin been upholstered, lavish with trophies of the frontier;\r\nand yet it was in front of the miniature that the visitors used to stop.\r\n\r\nShining quietly now in the cabin's blackness this summer day, the\r\nheirloom was presiding until the end. And as Molly Wood's eyes fell upon\r\nher ancestress of Bennington, 1777, there flashed a spark of steel in\r\nthem, alone here in the room that she was leaving forever. She was not\r\ngoing to teach school any more on Bear Creek, Wyoming; she was going\r\nhome to Bennington, Vermont. When time came for school to open again,\r\nthere should be a new schoolmarm.\r\n\r\nThis was the momentous result of that visit which the Virginian had paid\r\nher. He had told her that he was coming for his hour soon. From that\r\nhour she had decided to escape. She was running away from her own heart.\r\nShe did not dare to trust herself face to face again with her potent,\r\nindomitable lover. She longed for him, and therefore she would never see\r\nhim again. No great-aunt at Dunbarton, or anybody else that knew her and\r\nher family, should ever say that she had married below her station, had\r\nbeen an unworthy Stark! Accordingly, she had written to the Virginian,\r\nbidding him good-by, and wishing him everything in the world. As she\r\nhappened to be aware that she was taking everything in the world away\r\nfrom him, this letter was not the most easy of letters to write. But\r\nshe had made the language very kind. Yes; it was a thoroughly kind\r\ncommunication. And all because of that momentary visit, when he had\r\nbrought back to her two novels, EMMA and PRIDE AND PREJUDICE.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you like them?\xe2\x80\x9d she had then inquired; and he had smiled slowly\r\nat her. \xe2\x80\x9cYou haven't read them!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you going to tell me there has been no time?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen Molly had scolded her cow-puncher, and to this he had listened with\r\npleasure undisguised, as indeed he listened to every word that she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it has come too late,\xe2\x80\x9d he had told her when the scolding was over.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I was one of your little scholars hyeh in Bear Creek schoolhouse,\r\nyu' could learn me to like such frillery I reckon. But I'm a mighty\r\nignorant, growed-up man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo much the worse for you!\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I am pretty glad I am a man. Else I could not have learned the\r\nthing you have taught me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut she shut her lips and looked away. On the desk was a letter written\r\nfrom Vermont. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you don't tell me at once when you decide,\xe2\x80\x9d had said\r\nthe arch writer, \xe2\x80\x9cnever hope to speak to me again. Mary Wood, seriously,\r\nI am suspicious. Why do you never mention him nowadays? How exciting\r\nto have you bring a live cow-boy to Bennington! We should all come\r\nto dinner. Though of course I understand now that many of them have\r\nexcellent manners. But would he wear his pistol at table?\xe2\x80\x9d So the letter\r\nran on. It recounted the latest home gossip and jokes. In answering it\r\nMolly Wood had taken no notice of its childish tone here and there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHyeh's some of them cactus blossoms yu' wanted,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian.\r\nHis voice recalled the girl with almost a start. \xe2\x80\x9cI've brought a good\r\nhawss I've gentled for yu', and Taylor'll keep him till I need him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you so much! but I wish--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon yu' can't stop me lendin' Taylor a hawss. And you cert'nly'll\r\nget sick schoolteachin' if yu' don't keep outdoors some. Good-by--till\r\nthat next time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; there's always a next time,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered, as lightly as she\r\ncould.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere always will be. Don't yu' know that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe did not reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have discouraged spells,\xe2\x80\x9d he pursued, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I down them. For I've told\r\nyu' you were going to love me. You are goin' to learn back the thing you\r\nhave taught me. I'm not askin' anything now; I don't want you to speak a\r\nword to me. But I'm never goin' to quit till 'next time' is no more, and\r\nit's 'all the time' for you and me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith that he had ridden away, not even touching her hand. Long after\r\nhe had gone she was still in her chair, her eyes lingering upon his\r\nflowers, those yellow cups of the prickly pear. At length she had\r\nrisen impatiently, caught up the flowers, gone with them to the open\r\nwindow,--and then, after all, set them with pains in water.\r\n\r\nBut to-day Bear Creek was over. She was going home now. By the week's\r\nend she would be started. By the time the mail brought him her good-by\r\nletter she would be gone. She had acted.\r\n\r\nTo Bear Creek, the neighborly, the friendly, the not comprehending, this\r\nmove had come unlooked for, and had brought regret. Only one hard word\r\nhad been spoken to Molly, and that by her next-door neighbor and kindest\r\nfriend. In Mrs. Taylor's house the girl had daily come and gone as\r\na daughter, and that lady reached the subject thus:-- \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I took\r\nTaylor,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, sitting by as Robert Browning and Jane Austen were\r\ngoing into their box, \xe2\x80\x9cI married for love.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you wish it had been money?\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly, stooping to her industries.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know both of us better than that, child.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know I've seen people at home who couldn't possibly have had any\r\nother reason. They seemed satisfied, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe the poor ignorant things were!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so I have never been sure how I might choose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you are sure, deary. Don't you think I know you? And when it comes\r\nover Taylor once in a while, and he tells me I'm the best thing in his\r\nlife, and I tell him he ain't merely the best thing but the only thing\r\nin mine,--him and the children,--why, we just agree we'd do it all over\r\nthe same way if we had the chance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMolly continued to be industrious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that's why,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Taylor, \xe2\x80\x9cI want every girl that's anything\r\nto me to know her luck when it comes. For I was that near telling Taylor\r\nI wouldn't!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf ever my luck comes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly, with her back to her friend, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nshall say 'I will' at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you'll say it at Bennington next week.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMolly wheeled round.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you surely will. Do you expect he's going to stay here, and you in\r\nBennington?\xe2\x80\x9d And the campaigner sat back in her chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe? Goodness! Who is he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChild, child, you're talking cross to-day because you're at outs with\r\nyourself. You've been at outs ever since you took this idea of leaving\r\nthe school and us and everything this needless way. You have not treated\r\nhim right. And why, I can't make out to save me. What have you found out\r\nall of a sudden? If he was not good enough for you, I--But, oh, it's a\r\nprime one you're losing, Molly. When a man like that stays faithful to a\r\ngirl 'spite all the chances he gets, her luck is come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my luck! People have different notions of luck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNotions!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has been very kind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKind!\xe2\x80\x9d And now without further simmering, Mrs. Taylor's wrath boiled\r\nup and poured copiously over Molly Wood. \xe2\x80\x9cKind! There's a word you\r\nshouldn't use, my dear. No doubt you can spell it. But more than its\r\nspelling I guess you don't know. The children can learn what it means\r\nfrom some of the rest of us folks that don't spell so correct, maybe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Taylor, Mrs. Taylor--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can't wait, deary. Since the roughness looks bigger to you than the\r\ndiamond, you had better go back to Vermont. I expect you'll find better\r\ngrammar there, deary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe good dame stalked out, and across to her own cabin, and left the\r\nangry girl among her boxes. It was in vain she fell to work upon them.\r\nPresently something had to be done over again, and when it was the box\r\nheld several chattels less than before the readjustment. She played a\r\nsort of desperate dominos to fit these objects in the space, but here\r\nwere a paper-weight, a portfolio, with two wretched volumes that no\r\nchink would harbor; and letting them fall all at once, she straightened\r\nherself, still stormy with revolt, eyes and cheeks still hot from\r\nthe sting of long-parried truth. There, on her wall still, was the\r\nminiature, the little silent ancestress; and upon this face the girl's\r\nglance rested. It was as if she appealed to Grandmother Stark for\r\nsupport and comfort across the hundred years which lay between them. So\r\nthe flaxen girl on the wall and she among the boxes stood a moment face\r\nto face in seeming communion, and then the descendant turned again to\r\nher work. But after a desultory touch here and there she drew a long\r\nbreath and walked to the open door. What use was in finishing to-day,\r\nwhen she had nearly a week? This first spurt of toil had swept the cabin\r\nbare of all indwelling charm, and its look was chill. Across the lane\r\nhis horse, the one he had \xe2\x80\x9cgentled\xe2\x80\x9d for her, was grazing idly. She\r\nwalked there and caught him, and led him to her gate. Mrs. Taylor saw\r\nher go in, and soon come out in riding-dress; and she watched the girl\r\nthrow the saddle on with quick ease--the ease he had taught her. Mrs.\r\nTaylor also saw the sharp cut she gave the horse, and laughed grimly\r\nto herself in her window as horse and rider galloped into the beautiful\r\nsunny loneliness.\r\n\r\nTo the punished animal this switching was new! and at its third\r\nrepetition he turned his head in surprise, but was no more heeded than\r\nwere the bluffs and flowers where he was taking his own undirected\r\nchoice of way. He carried her over ground she knew by heart--Corncliff\r\nMesa, Crowheart Butte, Westfall's Crossing, Upper Canyon; open land and\r\nwoodland, pines and sage-brush, all silent and grave and lustrous in the\r\nsunshine. Once and again a ranchman greeted her, and wondered if she\r\nhad forgotten who he was; once she passed some cow-punchers with a small\r\nherd of steers, and they stared after her too. Bear Creek narrowed, its\r\nmountain-sides drew near, its little falls began to rush white in midday\r\nshadow, and the horse suddenly pricked his ears. Unguided, he was taking\r\nthis advantage to go home. Though he had made but little way--a mere\r\nbeginning yet--on this trail over to Sunk Creek, here was already a\r\nSunk Creek friend whinnying good day to him, so he whinnied back and\r\nquickened his pace, and Molly started to life. What was Monte doing\r\nhere? She saw the black horse she knew also, saddled, with reins\r\ndragging on the trail as the rider had dropped them to dismount. A cold\r\nspring bubbled out beyond the next rock, and she knew her lover's horse\r\nwas waiting for him while he drank. She pulled at the reins, but loosed\r\nthem, for to turn and escape now was ridiculous; and riding boldly round\r\nthe rock, she came upon him by the spring. One of his arms hung up to\r\nits elbow in the pool, the other was crooked beside his head, but the\r\nface was sunk downward against the shelving rock, so that she saw only\r\nhis black, tangled hair. As her horse snorted and tossed his head she\r\nlooked swiftly at Monte, as if to question him. Seeing now the sweat\r\nmatted on his coat, and noting the white rim of his eye, she sprang and\r\nran to the motionless figure. A patch of blood at his shoulder behind\r\nstained the soft flannel shirt, spreading down beneath his belt, and the\r\nman's whole strong body lay slack and pitifully helpless.\r\n\r\nShe touched the hand beside his head, but it seemed neither warm nor\r\ncold to her; she felt for the pulse, as nearly as she could remember the\r\ndoctors did, but could not tell whether she imagined or not that it was\r\nstill; twice with painful care her fingers sought and waited for the\r\nbeat, and her face seemed like one of listening. She leaned down and\r\nlifted his other arm and hand from the water, and as their ice-coldness\r\nreached her senses, clearly she saw the patch near the shoulder she\r\nhad moved grow wet with new blood, and at that sight she grasped at the\r\nstones upon which she herself now sank. She held tight by two rocks,\r\nsitting straight beside him, staring, and murmuring aloud, \xe2\x80\x9cI must\r\nnot faint; I will not faint;\xe2\x80\x9d and the standing horses looked at her,\r\npricking their ears.\r\n\r\nIn this cup-like spread of the ravine the sun shone warmly down, the\r\ntall red cliff was warm, the pines were a warm film and filter of green;\r\noutside the shade across Bear Creek rose the steep, soft, open yellow\r\nhill, warm and high to the blue, and Bear Creek tumbled upon its\r\nsunsparkling stones. The two horses on the margin trail still looked\r\nat the spring and trees, where sat the neat flaxen girl so rigid by the\r\nslack prone body in its flannel shirt and leathern chaps. Suddenly her\r\nface livened. \xe2\x80\x9cBut the blood ran!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, as if to the horses,\r\nher companions in this. She moved to him, and put her hand in through\r\nhis shirt against his heart.\r\n\r\nNext moment she had sprung up and was at his saddle, searching, then\r\nswiftly went on to her own and got her small flask and was back beside\r\nhim. Here was the cold water he had sought, and she put it against his\r\nforehead and drenched the wounded shoulder with it. Three times she\r\ntried to move him, so he might lie more easy, but his dead weight was\r\ntoo much, and desisting, she sat close and raised his head to let it\r\nrest against her. Thus she saw the blood that was running from in front\r\nof the shoulder also; but she said no more about fainting. She tore\r\nstrips from her dress and soaked them, keeping them cold and wet upon\r\nboth openings of his wound, and she drew her pocket-knife out and cut\r\nhis shirt away from the place. As she continually rinsed and cleaned\r\nit, she watched his eyelashes, long and soft and thick, but they did not\r\nstir. Again she tried the flask, but failed from being still too gentle,\r\nand her searching eyes fell upon ashes near the pool. Still undispersed\r\nby the weather lay the small charred ends of a fire he and she had made\r\nonce here together, to boil coffee and fry trout. She built another fire\r\nnow, and when the flames were going well, filled her flask-cup from the\r\nspring and set it to heat. Meanwhile, she returned to nurse his head\r\nand wound. Her cold water had stopped the bleeding. Then she poured\r\nher brandy in the steaming cup, and, made rough by her desperate\r\nhelplessness, forced some between his lips and teeth.\r\n\r\nInstantly, almost, she felt the tremble of life creeping back, and\r\nas his deep eyes opened upon her she sat still and mute. But the gaze\r\nseemed luminous with an unnoting calm, and she wondered if perhaps he\r\ncould not recognize her; she watched this internal clearness of his\r\nvision, scarcely daring to breathe, until presently he began to speak,\r\nwith the same profound and clear impersonality sounding in his slowly\r\nuttered words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought they had found me. I expected they were going to kill me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n He stopped, and she gave him more of the hot drink, which he took, still\r\nlying and looking at her as if the present did not reach his senses. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nknew hands were touching me. I reckon I was not dead. I knew about them\r\nsoon as they began, only I could not interfere.\xe2\x80\x9d He waited again. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is\r\nmighty strange where I have been. No. Mighty natural.\xe2\x80\x9d Then he went back\r\ninto his revery, and lay with his eyes still full open upon her where\r\nshe sat motionless.\r\n\r\nShe began to feel a greater awe in this living presence than when it\r\nhad been his body with an ice-cold hand; and she quietly spoke his name,\r\nventuring scarcely more than a whisper.\r\n\r\nAt this, some nearer thing wakened in his look. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it was you all\r\nalong,\xe2\x80\x9d he resumed. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is you now. You must not stay--\xe2\x80\x9d Weakness\r\novercame him, and his eyes closed. She sat ministering to him, and when\r\nhe roused again, he began anxiously at once: \xe2\x80\x9cYou must not stay. They\r\nwould get you, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe glanced at him with a sort of fierceness, then reached for his\r\npistol, in which was nothing but blackened empty cartridges. She threw\r\nthese out and drew six from his belt, loaded the weapon, and snapped\r\nshut its hinge.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease take it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, more anxious and more himself. \xe2\x80\x9cI ain't worth\r\ntryin' to keep. Look at me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you giving up?\xe2\x80\x9d she inquired, trying to put scorn in her tone. Then\r\nshe seated herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is the sense in both of us--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had better save your strength,\xe2\x80\x9d she interrupted.\r\n\r\nHe tried to sit up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLie down!\xe2\x80\x9d she ordered.\r\n\r\nHe sank obediently, and began to smile.\r\n\r\nWhen she saw that, she smiled too, and unexpectedly took his hand.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen, friend,\xe2\x80\x9d said she. \xe2\x80\x9cNobody shall get you, and nobody shall get\r\nme. Now take some more brandy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must be noon,\xe2\x80\x9d said the cow-puncher, when she had drawn her hand\r\naway from him. \xe2\x80\x9cI remember it was dark when--when--when I can remember.\r\nI reckon they were scared to follow me in so close to settlers. Else\r\nthey would have been here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must rest,\xe2\x80\x9d she observed.\r\n\r\nShe broke the soft ends of some evergreen, and putting them beneath his\r\nhead, went to the horses, loosened the cinches, took off the bridles,\r\nled them to drink, and picketed them to feed. Further still, to leave\r\nnothing undone which she could herself manage, she took the horses'\r\nsaddles off to refold the blankets when the time should come, and\r\nmeanwhile brought them for him. But he put them away from him. He was\r\nsitting up against a rock, stronger evidently, and asking for cold\r\nwater. His head was fire-hot, and the paleness beneath his swarthy skin\r\nhad changed to a deepening flush.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly five miles!\xe2\x80\x9d she said to him, bathing his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I must hold it steady,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, waving his hand at the cliff.\r\n\r\nShe told him to try and keep it steady until they got home.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly five miles. But it's fightin' to turn around.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Half aware that he was becoming light-headed, he looked from the rock to\r\nher and from her to the rock with dilating eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can hold it together,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must get on your horse.\xe2\x80\x9d She\r\ntook his handkerchief from round his neck, knotting it with her own, and\r\nto make more bandage she ran to the roll of clothes behind his saddle\r\nand tore in halves a clean shirt. A handkerchief fell from it, which\r\nshe seized also, and opening, saw her own initials by the hem. Then she\r\nremembered: she saw again their first meeting, the swollen river, the\r\noverset stage, the unknown horseman who carried her to the bank on his\r\nsaddle and went away unthanked--her whole first adventure on that\r\nfirst day of her coming to this new country--and now she knew how her\r\nlong-forgotten handkerchief had gone that day. She refolded it gently\r\nand put it back in his bundle, for there was enough bandage without it.\r\nShe said not a word to him, and he placed a wrong meaning upon the look\r\nwhich she gave him as she returned to bind his shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt don't hurt so much,\xe2\x80\x9d he assured her (though extreme pain was\r\nclearing his head for the moment, and he had been able to hold the cliff\r\nfrom turning). \xe2\x80\x9cYu' must not squander your pity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not squander your strength,\xe2\x80\x9d said she.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I could put up a pretty good fight now!\xe2\x80\x9d But he tottered in showing\r\nher how strong he was, and she told him that, after all, he was a child\r\nstill.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he slowly said, looking after her as she went to bring his horse,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthe same child that wanted to touch the moon, I guess.\xe2\x80\x9d And during the\r\nslow climb down into the saddle from a rock to which she helped him he\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cYou have got to be the man all through this mess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe saw his teeth clinched and his drooping muscles compelled by will;\r\nand as he rode and she walked to lend him support, leading her horse\r\nby a backward-stretched left hand, she counted off the distance to him\r\ncontinually--the increasing gain, the lessening road, the landmarks\r\nnearing and dropping behind; here was the tree with the wasp-nest gone;\r\nnow the burned cabin was passed; now the cottonwoods at the ford were in\r\nsight. He was silent, and held to the saddle-horn, leaning more and more\r\nagainst his two hands clasped over it; and just after they had made the\r\ncrossing he fell, without a sound slipping to the grass, and his descent\r\nbroken by her. But it started the blood a little, and she dared not\r\nleave him to seek help. She gave him the last of the flask and all the\r\nwater he craved.\r\n\r\nRevived, he managed to smile. \xe2\x80\x9cYu' see, I ain't worth keeping.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's only a mile,\xe2\x80\x9d said she. So she found a log, a fallen trunk, and he\r\ncrawled to that, and from there crawled to his saddle, and she marched\r\non with him, talking, bidding him note the steps accomplished. For the\r\nnext half-mile they went thus, the silent man clinched on the horse, and\r\nby his side the girl walking and cheering him forward, when suddenly he\r\nbegan to speak:-- \xe2\x80\x9cI will say good-by to you now, ma'am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe did not understand, at first, the significance of this.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is getting away,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cI must ask you to excuse\r\nme, ma'am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was a long while since her lord had addressed her as \xe2\x80\x9cma'am.\xe2\x80\x9d As she\r\nlooked at him in growing apprehension, he turned Monte and would have\r\nridden away, but she caught the bridle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must take me home,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, with ready inspiration. \xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid\r\nof the Indians.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you--why, they've all gone. There he goes. Ma'am--that hawss--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, holding firmly his rein and quickening her step. \xe2\x80\x9cA\r\ngentleman does not invite a lady to go out riding and leave her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis eyes lost their purpose. \xe2\x80\x9cI'll cert'nly take you home. That sorrel\r\nhas gone in there by the wallow, and Judge Henry will understand.\xe2\x80\x9d With\r\nhis eyes watching imaginary objects, he rode and rambled and it was now\r\nthe girl who was silent, except to keep his mind from its half-fixed\r\nidea of the sorrel. As he grew more fluent she hastened still more,\r\nlistening to head off that notion of return, skilfully inventing\r\nquestions to engage him, so that when she brought him to her gate\r\nshe held him in a manner subjected, answering faithfully the shrewd\r\nunrealities which she devised, whatever makeshifts she could summon to\r\nher mind; and next she had got him inside her dwelling and set him down\r\ndocile, but now completely wandering; and then--no help was at hand,\r\neven here. She had made sure of aid from next door, and there she\r\nhastened, to find the Taylor's cabin locked and silent; and this meant\r\nthat parents and children were gone to drive; nor might she be luckier\r\nat her next nearest neighbors', should she travel the intervening mile\r\nto fetch them. With a mind jostled once more into uncertainty, she\r\nreturned to her room, and saw a change in him already. Illness had\r\nstridden upon him; his face was not as she had left it, and the whole\r\nbody, the splendid supple horseman, showed sickness in every line\r\nand limb, its spurs and pistol and bold leather chaps a mockery of\r\ntrappings. She looked at him, and decision came back to her, clear and\r\nsteady. She supported him over to her bed and laid him on it. His head\r\nsank flat, and his loose, nerveless arms stayed as she left them. Then\r\namong her packing-boxes and beneath the little miniature, blue and\r\nflaxen and gold upon its lonely wall, she undressed him. He was cold,\r\nand she covered him to the face, and arranged the pillow, and got from\r\nits box her scarlet and black Navajo blanket and spread it over him.\r\nThere was no more that she could do, and she sat down by him to wait.\r\nAmong the many and many things that came into her mind was a word he\r\nsaid to her lightly a long while ago. \xe2\x80\x9cCow-punchers do not live long\r\nenough to get old,\xe2\x80\x9d he had told her. And now she looked at the head upon\r\nthe pillow, grave and strong, but still the head of splendid, unworn\r\nyouth.\r\n\r\nAt the distant jingle of the wagon in the lane she was out, and had met\r\nher returning neighbors midway. They heard her with amazement, and came\r\nin haste to the bedside; then Taylor departed to spread news of the\r\nIndians and bring the doctor, twenty-five miles away. The two women\r\nfriends stood alone again, as they had stood in the morning when anger\r\nhad been between them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKiss me, deary,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Taylor. \xe2\x80\x9cNow I will look after him--and\r\nyou'll need some looking after yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut on returning from her cabin with what store she possessed of lint\r\nand stimulants, she encountered a rebel, independent as ever. Molly\r\nwould hear no talk about saving her strength, would not be in any room\r\nbut this one until the doctor should arrive; then perhaps it would be\r\ntime to think about resting. So together the dame and the girl rinsed\r\nthe man's wound and wrapped him in clean things, and did all the little\r\nthat they knew--which was, in truth, the very thing needed. Then they\r\nsat watching him toss and mutter. It was no longer upon Indians or\r\nthe sorrel horse that his talk seemed to run, or anything recent,\r\napparently, always excepting his work. This flowingly merged with\r\nwhatever scene he was inventing or living again, and he wandered\r\nunendingly in that incompatible world we dream in. Through the medley of\r\nevents and names, often thickly spoken, but rising at times to grotesque\r\ncoherence, the listeners now and then could piece out the reference from\r\ntheir own knowledge. \xe2\x80\x9cMonte,\xe2\x80\x9d for example, continually addressed, and\r\nMolly heard her own name, but invariably as \xe2\x80\x9cMiss Wood\xe2\x80\x9d; nothing less\r\nrespectful came out, and frequently he answered some one as \xe2\x80\x9cma'am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n At these fragments of revelation Mrs. Taylor abstained from speech, but\r\neyed Molly Wood with caustic reproach. As the night wore on, short lulls\r\nof silence intervened, and the watchers were deceived into hope that the\r\nfever was abating. And when the Virginian sat quietly up in bed, essayed\r\nto move his bandage, and looked steadily at Mrs. Taylor, she rose\r\nquickly and went to him with a question as to how he was doing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRise on your laigs, you polecat,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cand tell them you're a\r\nliar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe good dame gasped, then bade him lie down, and he obeyed her with\r\nthat strange double understanding of the delirious; for even while\r\nsubmitting, he muttered \xe2\x80\x9cliar,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cpolecat,\xe2\x80\x9d and then \xe2\x80\x9cTrampas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt that name light flashed on Mrs. Taylor, and she turned to Molly; and\r\nthere was the girl struggling with a fit of mirth at his speech; but the\r\nlaughter was fast becoming a painful seizure. Mrs. Taylor walked Molly\r\nup and down, speaking mmediately to arrest her attention.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou might as well know it,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cHe would blame me for speaking\r\nof it, but where's the harm all this while after? And you would never\r\nhear it from his mouth. Molly, child, they say Trampas would kill him if\r\nhe dared, and that's on account of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never saw Trampas,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly, fixing her eyes upon the speaker.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, deary. But before a lot of men--Taylor has told me about\r\nit--Trampas spoke disrespectfully of you, and before them all he made\r\nTrampas say he was a liar. That is what he did when you were almost\r\na stranger among us, and he had not started seeing so much of you. I\r\nexpect Trampas is the only enemy he ever had in this country. But he\r\nwould never let you know about that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Molly; \xe2\x80\x9cI did not know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSteve!\xe2\x80\x9d the sick man now cried out, in poignant appeal. \xe2\x80\x9cSteve!\xe2\x80\x9d To the\r\nwomen it was a name unknown,--unknown as was also this deep inward tide\r\nof feeling which he could no longer conceal, being himself no longer.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Steve,\xe2\x80\x9d he said next, and muttering followed. \xe2\x80\x9cIt ain't so!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nshouted; and then cunningly in a lowered voice, \xe2\x80\x9cSteve, I have lied for\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn time Mrs. Taylor spoke some advice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had better go to bed, child. You look about ready for the doctor\r\nyourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I will wait for him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly.\r\n\r\nSo the two nurses continued to sit until darkness at the windows\r\nweakened into gray, and the lamp was no more needed. Their patient was\r\nrambling again. Yet, into whatever scenes he went, there in some guise\r\ndid the throb of his pain evidently follow him, and he lay hitching his\r\ngreat shoulder as if to rid it of the cumbrance. They waited for the\r\ndoctor, not daring much more than to turn pillows and give what other\r\nease they could; and then, instead of the doctor, came a messenger,\r\nabout noon, to say he was gone on a visit some thirty miles beyond,\r\nwhere Taylor had followed to bring him here as soon as might be. At this\r\nMolly consented to rest and to watch, turn about; and once she was over\r\nin her friend's house lying down, they tried to keep her there. But the\r\nrevolutionist could not be put down, and when, as a last pretext, Mrs.\r\nTaylor urged the proprieties and conventions, the pale girl from Vermont\r\nlaughed sweetly in her face and returned to sit by the sick man. With\r\nthe approach of the second night his fever seemed to rise and master\r\nhim more completely than they had yet seen it, and presently it so raged\r\nthat the women called in stronger arms to hold him down. There were\r\ntimes when he broke out in the language of the round-up, and Mrs. Taylor\r\nrenewed her protests. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly, \xe2\x80\x9cdon't you suppose I knew they\r\ncould swear?\xe2\x80\x9d So the dame, in deepening astonishment and affection, gave\r\nup these shifts at decorum. Nor did the delirium run into the intimate,\r\ncoarse matters that she dreaded. The cow-puncher had lived like his\r\nkind, but his natural daily thoughts were clean, and came from the\r\nuntamed but unstained mind of a man. And toward morning, as Mrs. Taylor\r\nsat taking her turn, suddenly he asked had he been sick long, and looked\r\nat her with a quieted eye. The wandering seemed to drop from him at a\r\nstroke, leaving him altogether himself. He lay very feeble, and inquired\r\nonce or twice of his state and how he came here; nor was anything left\r\nin his memory of even coming to the spring where he had been found.\r\n\r\nWhen the doctor arrived, he pronounced that it would be long--or very\r\nshort. He praised their clean water treatment; the wound was fortunately\r\nwell up on the shoulder, and gave so far no bad signs; there were not\r\nany bad signs; and the blood and strength of the patient had been as\r\nfew men's were; each hour was now an hour nearer certainty, and\r\nmeanwhile--meanwhile the doctor would remain as long as he could. He had\r\nmany inquiries to satisfy. Dusty fellows would ride up, listen to him,\r\nand reply, as they rode away, \xe2\x80\x9cDon't yu' let him die, Doc.\xe2\x80\x9d And Judge\r\nHenry sent over from Sunk Creek to answer for any attendance or medicine\r\nthat might help his foreman. The country was moved with concern and\r\ninterest; and in Molly's ears its words of good feeling seemed to unite\r\nand sum up a burden, \xe2\x80\x9cDon't yu' let him die, Doc.\xe2\x80\x9d The Indians who had\r\ndone this were now in military custody.
