They had come unpermitted from\r\na southern reservation, hunting, next thieving, and as the slumbering\r\nspirit roused in one or two of the young and ambitious, they had\r\nventured this in the secret mountains, and perhaps had killed a trapper\r\nfound there. Editors immediately reared a tall war out of it; but from\r\nfive Indians in a guard-house waiting punishment not even an editor\r\ncan supply spar for more than two editions, and if the recent alarm\r\nwas still a matter of talk anywhere, it was not here in the sick-room.\r\nWhichever way the case should turn, it was through Molly alone (the\r\ndoctor told her) that the wounded man had got this chance--this good\r\nchance, he related.\r\n\r\nAnd he told her she had not done a woman's part, but a man's part, and\r\nnow had no more to do; no more till the patient got well, and could\r\nthank her in his own way, said the doctor, smiling, and supposing things\r\nthat were not so--misled perhaps by Mrs. Taylor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm afraid I'll be gone by the time he is well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly, coldly;\r\nand the discreet physician said ah, and that she would find Bennington\r\nquite a change from Bear Creek.\r\n\r\nBut Mrs. Taylor spoke otherwise, and at that the girl said: \xe2\x80\x9cI shall\r\nstay as long as I am needed. I will nurse him. I want to nurse him. I\r\nwill do everything for him that I can!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, with force.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that won't be anything, deary,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Taylor, harshly. \xe2\x80\x9cA year\r\nof nursing don't equal a day of sweetheart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe girl took a walk,--she was of no more service in the room at\r\npresent,--but she turned without going far, and Mrs. Taylor spied her\r\ncome to lean over the pasture fence and watch the two horses--that one\r\nthe Virginian had \xe2\x80\x9cgentled\xe2\x80\x9d for her, and his own Monte. During this\r\nsuspense came a new call for the doctor, neighbors profiting by his\r\nvisit to Bear Creek; and in his going away to them, even under promise\r\nof quick return, Mrs. Taylor suspected a favorable sign. He kept his\r\nword as punctually as had been possible, arriving after some six hours\r\nwith a confident face, and spending now upon the patient a care not\r\nneeded, save to reassure the bystanders. He spoke his opinion that all\r\nwas even better than he could have hoped it would be, so soon. Here was\r\nnow the beginning of the fifth day; the wound's look was wholesome, no\r\nfurther delirium had come, and the fever had abated a degree while he\r\nwas absent. He believed the serious danger-line lay behind, and (short\r\nof the unforeseen) the man's deep untainted strength would reassert\r\nits control. He had much blood to make, and must be cared for during\r\nweeks--three, four, five--there was no saying how long yet. These next\r\nfew days it must be utter quiet for him; he must not talk nor hear\r\nanything likely to disturb him; and then the time for cheerfulness and\r\ngradual company would come--sooner than later, the doctor hoped. So\r\nhe departed, and sent next day some bottles, with further cautions\r\nregarding the wound and dirt, and to say he should be calling the day\r\nafter to-morrow.\r\n\r\nUpon that occasion he found two patients. Molly Wood lay in bed at Mrs.\r\nTaylor's, filled with apology and indignation. With little to do, and\r\ndeprived of the strong stimulant of anxiety and action, her strength\r\nhad quite suddenly left her, so that she had spoken only in a sort of\r\nwhisper. But upon waking from a long sleep, after Mrs. Taylor had taken\r\nher firmly, almost severely, in hand, her natural voice had returned,\r\nand now the chief treatment the doctor gave her was a sort of scolding,\r\nwhich it pleased Mrs. Taylor to hear. The doctor even dropped a phrase\r\nconcerning the arrogance of strong nerves in slender bodies, and of\r\nundertaking several people's work when several people were at hand to do\r\nit for themselves, and this pleased Mrs. Taylor remarkably. As for the\r\nwounded man, he was behaving himself properly. Perhaps in another week\r\nhe could be moved to a more cheerful room. Just now, with cleanliness\r\nand pure air, any barn would do.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are real lucky to have such a sensible doctor in the country,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs.\r\nTaylor observed, after the physician had gone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo doubt,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly. \xe2\x80\x9cHe said my room was a barn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's what you've made it, deary. But sick men don't notice much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNevertheless, one may believe, without going widely astray, that\r\nillness, so far from veiling, more often quickens the perceptions--at\r\nany rate those of the naturally keen. On a later day--and the interval\r\nwas brief--while Molly was on her second drive to take the air with Mrs.\r\nTaylor, that lady informed her that the sick man had noticed. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\r\ncould not tell him things liable to disturb him,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, \xe2\x80\x9cand so\r\nI--well, I expect I just didn't exactly tell him the facts. I said yes,\r\nyou were packing up for a little visit to your folks. They had not seen\r\nyou for quite a while, I said. And he looked at those boxes kind of\r\nsilent like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere's no need to move him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly. '\xe2\x80\x9cIt is simpler to move\r\nthem--the boxes. I could take out some of my things, you know, just\r\nwhile he has to be kept there. I mean--you see, if the doctor says the\r\nroom should be cheerful--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, deary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will ask the doctor next time,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly, \xe2\x80\x9cif he believes I\r\nam--competent to spread a rug upon a floor.\xe2\x80\x9d Molly's references to\r\nthe doctor were usually acid these days. And this he totally failed to\r\nobserve, telling her when he came, why, to be sure! the very thing!\r\nAnd if she could play cards or read aloud, or afford any other light\r\ndistractions, provided they did not lead the patient to talk and tire\r\nhimself, that she would be most useful. Accordingly she took over the\r\ncribbage board, and came with unexpected hesitation face to face again\r\nwith the swarthy man she had saved and tended. He was not so swarthy\r\nnow, but neat, with chin clean, and hair and mustache trimmed and\r\nsmooth, and he sat propped among pillows watching for her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are better,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, speaking first, and with uncertain voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. They have given me awdehs not to talk,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Southerner,\r\nsmiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes. Please do not talk--not to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Only this\xe2\x80\x9d--he looked at her, and saw her seem to shrink--\xe2\x80\x9cthank\r\nyou for what you have done,\xe2\x80\x9d he said simply.\r\n\r\nShe took tenderly the hand he stretched to her; and upon these terms\r\nthey set to work at cribbage. She won, and won again, and the third time\r\nlaid down her cards and reproached him with playing in order to lose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, and his eye wandered to the boxes. \xe2\x80\x9cBut my thoughts get\r\naway from me. I'll be strong enough to hold them on the cyards next\r\ntime, I reckon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMany tones in his voice she had heard, but never the tone of sadness\r\nuntil to-day.\r\n\r\nThen they played a little more, and she put away the board for this\r\nfirst time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are going now?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I have made this room look a little less forlorn. They haven't\r\nwanted to meddle with my things, I suppose.\xe2\x80\x9d And Molly stooped once\r\nagain among the chattels destined for Vermont. Out they came; again the\r\nbearskin was spread on the floor, various possessions and ornaments went\r\nback into their ancient niches, the shelves grew comfortable with books,\r\nand, last, some flowers were stood on the table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore like old times,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, but sadly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's too bad,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly, \xe2\x80\x9cyou had to be brought into such a looking\r\nplace.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd your folks waiting for you,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I'll pay my visit later,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly, putting the rug a trifle\r\nstraighter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I ask one thing?\xe2\x80\x9d pleaded the Virginian, and at the gentleness of\r\nhis voice her face grew rosy, and she fixed her eyes on him with a sort\r\nof dread.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnything that I can answer,\xe2\x80\x9d said she.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes. Did I tell yu' to quit me, and did yu' load up my gun and\r\nstay? Was that a real business? I have been mixed up in my haid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was real,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat else was there to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust nothing--for such as you!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cMy haid has been mighty\r\ncrazy; and that little grandmother of yours yondeh, she--but I can't\r\njust quite catch a-hold of these things\xe2\x80\x9d--he passed a hand over\r\nhis forehead--\xe2\x80\x9cso many--or else one right along--well, it's all\r\nfoolishness!\xe2\x80\x9d he concluded, with something almost savage in his tone.\r\nAnd after she had gone from the cabin he lay very still, looking at the\r\nminiature on the wall.\r\n\r\nHe was in another sort of mood the next time, cribbage not interesting\r\nhim in the least. \xe2\x80\x9cYour folks will be wondering about you,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't think they will mind which month I go to them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEspecially when they know the reason.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't let me keep you, ma'am,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. Molly stared at him; but he\r\npursued, with the same edge lurking in his slow words: \xe2\x80\x9cThough I'll\r\nnever forget. How could I forget any of all you have done--and been? If\r\nthere had been none of this, why, I had enough to remember! But please\r\ndon't stay, ma'am. We'll say I had a claim when yu' found me pretty well\r\ndead, but I'm gettin' well, yu' see--right smart, too!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can't understand, indeed I can't,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy you're talking\r\nso!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe seemed to have certain moods when he would address her as \xe2\x80\x9cma'am,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n and this she did not like, but could not prevent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, a sick man is funny. And yu' know I'm grateful to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease say no more about that, or I shall go this afternoon. I don't\r\nwant to go. I am not ready. I think I had better read something now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, yes. That's cert'nly a good notion. Why, this is the best show\r\nyou'll ever get to give me education. Won't yu' please try that EMMA\r\nbook now, ma'am? Listening to you will be different.\xe2\x80\x9d This was said with\r\nsoftness and humility.\r\n\r\nUncertain--as his gravity often left her--precisely what he meant by\r\nwhat he said, Molly proceeded with EMMA, slackly at first, but soon with\r\nthe enthusiasm that Miss Austen invariably gave her. She held the volume\r\nand read away at it, commenting briefly, and then, finishing a chapter\r\nof the sprightly classic, found her pupil slumbering peacefully. There\r\nwas no uncertainty about that.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou couldn't be doing a healthier thing for him, deary,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\nTaylor. \xe2\x80\x9cIf it gets to make him wakeful, try something harder.\xe2\x80\x9d This was\r\nthe lady's scarcely sympathetic view.\r\n\r\nBut it turned out to be not obscurity in which Miss Austen sinned.\r\n\r\nWhen Molly next appeared at the Virginian's threshold, he said\r\nplaintively, \xe2\x80\x9cI reckon I am a dunce.\xe2\x80\x9d And he sued for pardon. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I\r\nwaked up,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI was ashamed of myself for a plumb half-hour.\xe2\x80\x9d Nor\r\ncould she doubt this day that he meant what he said. His mood was again\r\nserene and gentle, and without referring to his singular words that had\r\ndistressed her, he made her feel his contrition, even in his silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am right glad you have come,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. And as he saw her going to the\r\nbookshelf, he continued, with diffidence: \xe2\x80\x9cAs regyards that EMMA book,\r\nyu' see--yu' see, the doin's and sayin's of folks like them are above\r\nme. But I think\xe2\x80\x9d (he spoke most diffidently), \xe2\x80\x9cif yu' could read me\r\nsomething that was ABOUT something, I--I'd be liable to keep awake.\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\nhe smiled with a certain shyness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething ABOUT something?\xe2\x80\x9d queried Molly, at a loss.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, yes. Shakespeare. HENRY THE FOURTH. The British king is fighting,\r\nand there is his son the prince. He cert'nly must have been a jim-dandy\r\nboy if that is all true. Only he would go around town with a mighty\r\ntriflin' gang. They sported and they held up citizens. And his father\r\nhated his travelling with trash like them. It was right natural--the boy\r\nand the old man! But the boy showed himself a man too. He killed a big\r\nfighter on the other side who was another jim-dandy--and he was sorry\r\nfor having it to do.\xe2\x80\x9d The Virginian warmed to his recital. \xe2\x80\x9cI understand\r\nmost all of that. There was a fat man kept everybody laughing. He was\r\nawful natural too; except yu' don't commonly meet 'em so fat. But the\r\nprince--that play is bed-rock, ma'am! Have you got something like that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I think so,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied. \xe2\x80\x9cI believe I see what you would\r\nappreciate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe took her Browning, her idol, her imagined affinity. For the pale\r\ndecadence of New England had somewhat watered her good old Revolutionary\r\nblood too, and she was inclined to think under glass and to live\r\nunderdone--when there were no Indians to shoot! She would have joyed to\r\nventure \xe2\x80\x9cParacelsus\xe2\x80\x9d on him, and some lengthy rhymed discourses; and she\r\nfondly turned leaves and leaves of her pet doggerel analytics. \xe2\x80\x9cPippa\r\nPasses\xe2\x80\x9d and others she had to skip, from discreet motives--pages which\r\nhe would have doubtless stayed awake at; but she chose a poem at length.\r\nThis was better than Emma, he pronounced. And short. The horse was a\r\ngood horse. He thought a man whose horse must not play out on him would\r\nwatch the ground he was galloping over for holes, and not be likely to\r\nsee what color the rims of his animal's eye-sockets were. You could not\r\nsee them if you sat as you ought to for such a hard ride. Of the next\r\npiece that she read him he thought still better. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd it is short,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nhe. \xe2\x80\x9cBut the last part drops.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMolly instantly exacted particulars.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe soldier should not have told the general he was killed,\xe2\x80\x9d stated the\r\ncow-puncher.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat should he have told him, I'd like to know?\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, just nothing. If the soldier could ride out of the battle all shot\r\nup, and tell his general about their takin' the town--that was being\r\ngritty, yu' see. But that truck at the finish--will yu' please say it\r\nagain?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo Molly read:--\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9c'You're wounded! 'Nay,' the soldier's pride\r\n     Touched to the quick, he said,\r\n     'I'm killed, sire!' And, his chief beside,\r\n     Smiling the boy fell dead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c'Nay, I'm killed, sire,'\xe2\x80\x9d drawled the Virginian, amiably; for (symptom\r\nof convalescence) his freakish irony was revived in him. \xe2\x80\x9cNow a man\r\nwho was man enough to act like he did, yu' see, would fall dead without\r\nmentioning it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNone of Molly's sweet girl friends had ever thus challenged Mr.\r\nBrowning. They had been wont to cluster over him with a joyous awe that\r\ndeepened proportionally with their misunderstanding. Molly paused to\r\nconsider this novelty of view about the soldier. \xe2\x80\x9cHe was a Frenchman,\r\nyou know,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, under inspiration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA Frenchman,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the grave cow-puncher. \xe2\x80\x9cI never knowed a\r\nFrenchman, but I reckon they might perform that class of foolishness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why was it foolish?\xe2\x80\x9d she cried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis soldier's pride--don't you see?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMolly now burst into a luxury of discussion. She leaned toward her\r\ncow-puncher with bright eyes searching his; with elbow on knee and hand\r\npropping chin, her lap became a slant, and from it Browning the poet\r\nslid and toppled, and lay unrescued. For the slow cow-puncher unfolded\r\nhis notions of masculine courage and modesty (though he did not deal in\r\nsuch high-sounding names), and Molly forgot everything to listen to him,\r\nas he forgot himself and his inveterate shyness and grew talkative to\r\nher. \xe2\x80\x9cI would never have supposed that!\xe2\x80\x9d she would exclaim as she heard\r\nhim; or, presently again, \xe2\x80\x9cI never had such an idea!\xe2\x80\x9d And her mind\r\nopened with delight to these new things which came from the man's mind\r\nso simple and direct. To Browning they did come back, but the Virginian,\r\nthough interested, conceived a dislike for him. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is a smarty,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nhe, once or twice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow here is something,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly. \xe2\x80\x9cI have never known what to think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Heavens!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the sick man, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cIs it short?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery short. Now please attend.\xe2\x80\x9d And she read him twelve lines about\r\na lover who rowed to a beach in the dusk, crossed a field, tapped at a\r\npane, and was admitted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is the best yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cThere's only one thing yu'\r\ncan think about that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut wait,\xe2\x80\x9d said the girl, swiftly. \xe2\x80\x9cHere is how they parted:--\r\n\r\n     \xe2\x80\x9cRound the cape of a sudden came the sea,\r\n     And the sun looked over the mountain's rim--\r\n     And straight was a path of gold for him,\r\n     And the need of a world of men for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is very, very true,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the Virginian, dropping his eyes\r\nfrom the girl's intent ones.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHad they quarrelled?\xe2\x80\x9d she inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon he loved her very much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you're sure they hadn't quarrelled?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDead sure, ma'am. He would come back afteh he had played some more of\r\nthe game.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe game?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLife, ma'am. Whatever he was a-doin' in the world of men. That's a\r\nbed-rock piece, ma'am!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don't see why you think it's so much better than some of the\r\nothers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could sca'cely explain,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the man. \xe2\x80\x9cBut that writer does know\r\nsomething.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad they hadn't quarrelled,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly, thoughtfully. And she\r\nbegan to like having her opinions refuted.\r\n\r\nHis bandages, becoming a little irksome, had to be shifted, and this\r\nturned their discourse from literature to Wyoming; and Molly inquired,\r\nhad he ever been shot before? Only once, he told her. \xe2\x80\x9cI have been\r\nlucky in having few fusses,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cI hate them. If a man has to be\r\nkilled--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou never--\xe2\x80\x9d broke in Molly. She had started back a little. \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nadded hastily, \xe2\x80\x9cdon't tell me if--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shouldn't wonder if I got one of those Indians,\xe2\x80\x9d he said quietly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I wasn't waitin' to see! But I came mighty near doing for a white\r\nman that day. He had been hurtin' a hawss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHurting?\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cInjurin.' I will not tell yu' about that. It would hurt yu' to hear\r\nsuch things. But hawsses--don't they depend on us? Ain't they somethin'\r\nlike children? I did not lay up the man very bad. He was able to travel\r\n'most right away. Why, you'd have wanted to kill him yourself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo the Virginian talked, nor knew what he was doing to the girl. Nor\r\nwas she aware of what she was receiving from him as he unwittingly spoke\r\nhimself out to her in these Browning meetings they had each day. But\r\nMrs. Taylor grew pleased. The kindly dame would sometimes cross the\r\nroad to see if she were needed, and steal away again after a peep at the\r\nwindow. There, inside, among the restored home treasures, sat the two:\r\nthe rosy alert girl, sweet as she talked or read to him; and he, the\r\ngrave, half-weak giant among his wraps, watching her.\r\n\r\nOf her delayed home visit he never again spoke, either to her or to Mrs.\r\nTaylor; and Molly veered aside from any trend of talk she foresaw was\r\nleading toward that subject. But in those hours when no visitors\r\ncame, and he was by himself in the quiet, he would lie often sombrely\r\ncontemplating the girl's room, her little dainty knickknacks, her home\r\nphotographs, all the delicate manifestations of what she came from and\r\nwhat she was. Strength was flowing back into him each day, and Judge\r\nHenry's latest messenger had brought him clothes and mail from Sunk\r\nCreek and many inquiries of kindness, and returned taking the news of\r\nthe cow-puncher's improvement, and how soon he would be permitted the\r\nfresh air. Hence Molly found him waiting in a flannel shirt of highly\r\nbecoming shade, and with a silk handkerchief knotted round his throat;\r\nand he told her it was good to feel respectable again.\r\n\r\nShe had come to read to him for the allotted time; and she threw around\r\nhis shoulders the scarlet and black Navajo blanket, striped with its\r\nsplendid zigzags of barbarity. Thus he half sat, half leaned, languid\r\nbut at ease. In his lap lay one of the letters brought over by the\r\nmessenger: and though she was midway in a book that engaged his full\r\nattention--DAVID COPPERFIELD--his silence and absent look this morning\r\nstopped her, and she accused him of not attending.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he admitted; \xe2\x80\x9cI am thinking of something else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked at him with that apprehension which he knew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt had to come,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd to-day I see my thoughts straighter than\r\nI've been up to managing since--since my haid got clear. And now I\r\nmust say these thoughts--if I can, if I can!\xe2\x80\x9d He stopped. His eyes were\r\nintent upon her; one hand was gripping the arm of his chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou promised--\xe2\x80\x9d trembled Molly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI promised you should love me,\xe2\x80\x9d he sternly interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cPromised that\r\nto myself. I have broken that word.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe shut DAVID COPPERFIELD mechanically, and grew white.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour letter has come to me hyeh,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, gentle again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy--\xe2\x80\x9d She had forgotten it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe letter you wrote to tell me good-by. You wrote it a little while\r\nago--not a month yet, but it's away and away long gone for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have never let you know--\xe2\x80\x9d began Molly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe doctor,\xe2\x80\x9d he interrupted once more, but very gently now, \xe2\x80\x9che gave\r\nawdehs I must be kept quiet. I reckon yu' thought tellin' me might--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cForgive me!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the girl. \xe2\x80\x9cIndeed I ought to have told you sooner!\r\nIndeed I had no excuse!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, should yu' tell me if yu' preferred not? You had written. And you\r\nspeak\xe2\x80\x9d (he lifted the letter) \xe2\x80\x9cof never being able to repay kindness;\r\nbut you have turned the tables. I can never repay you by anything! by\r\nanything! So I had figured I would just jog back to Sunk Creek and let\r\nyou get away, if you did not want to say that kind of good-by. For I saw\r\nthe boxes. Mrs. Taylor is too nice a woman to know the trick of lyin',\r\nand she could not deceive me. I have knowed yu' were going away for good\r\never since I saw those boxes. But now hyeh comes your letter, and it\r\nseems no way but I must speak. I have thought a deal, lyin' in this\r\nroom. And--to-day--I can say what I have thought. I could not make you\r\nhappy.\xe2\x80\x9d He stopped, but she did not answer. His voice had grown softer\r\nthan whispering, but yet was not a whisper. From its quiet syllables she\r\nturned away, blinded with sudden tears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnce, I thought love must surely be enough,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nI thought if I could make you love me, you could learn me to be\r\nless--less--more your kind. And I think I could give you a pretty good\r\nsort of love. But that don't help the little mean pesky things of day by\r\nday that make roughness or smoothness for folks tied together so awful\r\nclose. Mrs. Taylor hyeh--she don't know anything better than Taylor\r\ndoes. She don't want anything he can't give her. Her friends will do for\r\nhim and his for her. And when I dreamed of you in my home--\xe2\x80\x9d he closed\r\nhis eyes and drew a long breath. At last he looked at her again. \xe2\x80\x9cThis\r\nis no country for a lady. Will yu' forget and forgive the bothering I\r\nhave done?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Molly. \xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d And she put her hands to her eyes. She had\r\nrisen and stood with her face covered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI surely had to tell you this all out, didn't I?\xe2\x80\x9d said the cow-puncher,\r\nfaintly, in his chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have put it clear how it is,\xe2\x80\x9d he pursued. \xe2\x80\x9cI ought to have seen from\r\nthe start I was not the sort to keep you happy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly--\xe2\x80\x9cbut I--you ought--please try to keep me happy!\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\nsinking by his chair, she hid her face on his knees.\r\n\r\nSpeechless, he bent down and folded her round, putting his hands on the\r\nhair that had been always his delight. Presently he whispered:-- \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nhave beat me; how can I fight this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe answered nothing. The Navajo's scarlet and black folds fell over\r\nboth. Not with words, not even with meeting eyes, did the two plight\r\ntheir troth in this first new hour. So they remained long, the fair head\r\nnesting in the great arms, and the black head laid against it, while\r\nover the silent room presided the little Grandmother Stark in her frame,\r\nrosy, blue, and flaxen, not quite familiar, not quite smiling.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXVIII. NO DREAM TO WAKE FROM\r\n\r\n\r\nFor a long while after she had left him, he lay still, stretched in\r\nhis chair. His eyes were fixed steadily upon the open window and the\r\nsunshine outside. There he watched the movement of the leaves upon the\r\ngreen cottonwoods. What had she said to him when she went? She had said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I know how unhappy I have been.\xe2\x80\x9d These sweet words he repeated to\r\nhimself over and over, fearing in some way that he might lose them. They\r\nalmost slipped from him at times; but with a jump of his mind he caught\r\nthem again and held them,--and then--\xe2\x80\x9cI'm not all strong yet,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nmurmured. \xe2\x80\x9cI must have been very sick.\xe2\x80\x9d And, weak from his bullet\r\nwound and fever, he closed his eyes without knowing it. There were the\r\ncottonwoods again, waving, waving; and he felt the cool, pleasant air\r\nfrom the window. He saw the light draught stir the ashes in the great\r\nstone fireplace. \xe2\x80\x9cI have been asleep,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut she was cert'nly\r\nhere herself. Oh, yes. Surely. She always has to go away every day\r\nbecause the doctor says--why, she was readin'!\xe2\x80\x9d he broke off, aloud.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDAVID COPPERFIELD.\xe2\x80\x9d There it was on the floor. \xe2\x80\x9cAha! nailed you\r\nanyway!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut how scared I am of myself!--You're a fool. Of\r\ncourse it's so. No fever business could make yu' feel like this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis eye dwelt awhile on the fireplace, next on the deer horns, and\r\nnext it travelled toward the shelf where her books were; but it stopped\r\nbefore reaching them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetter say off the names before I look,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cI've had a heap o'\r\nmisreading visions. And--and supposin'--if this was just my sickness\r\nfooling me some more--I'd want to die. I would die! Now we'll see. If\r\nCOPPERFIELD is on the floor\xe2\x80\x9d (he looked stealthily to be sure that it\r\nwas), \xe2\x80\x9cthen she was readin' to me when everything happened, and then\r\nthere should be a hole in the book row, top, left. Top, left,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nrepeated, and warily brought his glance to the place. \xe2\x80\x9cProved!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ncried. \xe2\x80\x9cIt's all so!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe now noticed the miniature of Grandmother Stark. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are awful like\r\nher,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered. \xe2\x80\x9cYou're cert'nly awful like her. May I kiss you too,\r\nma'am?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen, tottering, he rose from his sick-chair. The Navajo blanket fell\r\nfrom his shoulders, and gradually, experimentally, he stood upright.\r\n\r\nHelping himself with his hand slowly along the wall of the room, and\r\nround to the opposite wall with many a pause, he reached the picture,\r\nand very gently touched the forehead of the ancestral dame with his\r\nlips. \xe2\x80\x9cI promise to make your little girl happy,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered.\r\n\r\nHe almost fell in stooping to the portrait, but caught himself and stood\r\ncarefully quiet, trembling, and speaking to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere is your\r\nstrength?\xe2\x80\x9d he demanded. \xe2\x80\x9cI reckon it is joy that has unsteadied your\r\nlaigs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe door opened. It was she, come back with his dinner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy Heavens!\xe2\x80\x9d she said; and setting the tray down, she rushed to him.\r\nShe helped him back to his chair, and covered him again. He had suffered\r\nno hurt, but she clung to him; and presently he moved and let himself\r\nkiss her with fuller passion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will be good,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou looked so pale!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are speakin' low like me,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cBut we have no dream we\r\ncan wake from.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHad she surrendered on this day to her cow-puncher, her wild man? Was she\r\nforever wholly his? Had the Virginian's fire so melted her heart that no\r\nrift in it remained? So she would have thought if any thought had come\r\nto her. But in his arms to-day, thought was lost in something more\r\ndivine.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXIX. WORD TO BENNINGTON\r\n\r\n\r\nThey kept their secret for a while, or at least they had that special\r\njoy of believing that no one in all the world but themselves knew this\r\nthat had happened to them. But I think that there was one person who\r\nknew how to keep a secret even better than these two lovers. Mrs. Taylor\r\nmade no remarks to any one whatever. Nobody on Bear Creek, however, was\r\nso extraordinarily cheerful and serene. That peculiar severity which she\r\nhad manifested in the days when Molly was packing her possessions,\r\nhad now altogether changed. In these days she was endlessly kind and\r\nindulgent to her \xe2\x80\x9cdeary.\xe2\x80\x9d Although, as a housekeeper, Mrs. Taylor\r\nbelieved in punctuality at meals, and visited her offspring with\r\ndiscipline when they were late without good and sufficient excuse, Molly\r\nwas now exempt from the faintest hint of reprimand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd it's not because you're not her mother,\xe2\x80\x9d said George Taylor,\r\nbitterly. \xe2\x80\x9cShe used to get it, too. And we're the only ones that get it.\r\nThere she comes, just as we're about ready to quit! Aren't you going to\r\nsay NOTHING to her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeorge,\xe2\x80\x9d said his mother, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen you've saved a man's life it'll be time\r\nfor you to talk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo Molly would come in to her meals with much irregularity; and her\r\nremarks about the imperfections of her clock met with no rejoinder. And\r\nyet one can scarcely be so severe as had been Mrs. Taylor, and become\r\nwholly as mild as milk. There was one recurrent event that could\r\ninvariably awaken hostile symptoms in the dame. Whenever she saw a\r\nletter arrive with the Bennington postmark upon it, she shook her fist\r\nat that letter. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat's family pride?\xe2\x80\x9d she would say to herself. \xe2\x80\x9cTaylor\r\ncould be a Son of the Revolution if he'd a mind to. I wonder if she has\r\ntold her folks yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd when letters directed to Bennington would go out, Mrs. Taylor would\r\ninspect every one as if its envelope ought to grow transparent beneath\r\nher eyes, and yield up to her its great secret, if it had one. But in\r\ntruth these letters had no great secret to yield up, until one day--yes;\r\none day Mrs. Taylor would have burst, were bursting a thing that people\r\noften did. Three letters were the cause of this emotion on Mrs. Taylor's\r\npart; one addressed to Bennington, one to Dunbarton, and the third--here\r\nwas the great excitement--to Bennington, but not in the little\r\nschoolmarm's delicate writing. A man's hand had traced those plain,\r\nsteady vowels and consonants.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's come!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Mrs. Taylor, at this sight. \xe2\x80\x9cHe has written to\r\nher mother himself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat is what the Virginian had done, and here is how it had come about.\r\n\r\nThe sick man's convalescence was achieved. The weeks had brought back to\r\nhim, not his whole strength yet--that could come only by many miles\r\nof open air on the back of Monte; but he was strong enough now to GET\r\nstrength. When a patient reaches this stage, he is out of the woods.\r\n\r\nHe had gone for a little walk with his nurse. They had taken (under the\r\ndoctor's recommendation) several such little walks, beginning with a\r\nfive-minute one, and at last to-day accomplishing three miles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it has not been too far,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid I could walk twice\r\nas far.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfraid?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Because it means I can go to work again. This thing we have had\r\ntogether is over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor reply, she leaned against him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook at you!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly a little while ago you had to help me\r\nstand on my laigs. And now--\xe2\x80\x9d For a while there was silence between\r\nthem. \xe2\x80\x9cI have never had a right down sickness before,\xe2\x80\x9d he presently went\r\non. \xe2\x80\x9cNot to remember, that is. If any person had told me I could ENJOY\r\nsuch a thing--\xe2\x80\x9d He said no more, for she reached up, and no more speech\r\nwas possible.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow long has it been?\xe2\x80\x9d he next asked her.\r\n\r\nShe told him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if it could be forever--no. Not forever with no more than this.\r\nI reckon I'd be sick again! But if it could be forever with just you and\r\nme, and no one else to bother with. But any longer would not be doing\r\nright by your mother. She would have a right to think ill of me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d said the girl. \xe2\x80\x9cLet us keep it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot after I am gone. Your mother must be told.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems so--can't we--oh, why need anybody know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour mother ain't 'anybody.' She is your mother. I feel mighty\r\nresponsible to her for what I have done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I did it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think so? Your mother will not think so. I am going to write to\r\nher to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou! Write to my mother! Oh, then everything will be so different! They\r\nwill all--\xe2\x80\x9d Molly stopped before the rising visions of Bennington. Upon\r\nthe fairy-tale that she had been living with her cow-boy lover broke the\r\nvoices of the world. She could hear them from afar. She could see the\r\neyes of Bennington watching this man at her side. She could imagine the\r\nears of Bennington listening for slips in his English. There loomed upon\r\nher the round of visits which they would have to make. The ringing of\r\nthe door-bells, the waiting in drawing-rooms for the mistress to descend\r\nand utter her prepared congratulations, while her secret eye devoured\r\nthe Virginian's appearance, and his manner of standing and sitting. He\r\nwould be wearing gloves, instead of fringed gauntlets of buckskin. In a\r\nsmooth black coat and waistcoat, how could they perceive the man he was?\r\nDuring those short formal interviews, what would they ever find out of\r\nthe things that she knew about him? The things for which she was proud\r\nof him? He would speak shortly and simply; they would say, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n and \xe2\x80\x9cHow different you must find this from Wyoming!\xe2\x80\x9d--and then, after\r\nthe door was shut behind his departing back they would say--He would\r\nbe totally underrated, not in the least understood. Why should he be\r\nsubjected to this? He should never be!\r\n\r\nNow in all these half-formed, hurried, distressing thoughts which\r\nstreamed through the girl's mind, she altogether forgot one truth. True\r\nit was that the voice of the world would speak as she imagined. True it\r\nwas that in the eyes of her family and acquaintance this lover of her\r\nchoice would be examined even more like a SPECIMEN than are other lovers\r\nupon these occasions: and all accepted lovers have to face this ordeal\r\nof being treated like specimens by the other family. But dear me!\r\nmost of us manage to stand it, don't we? It isn't, perhaps, the\r\nmost delicious experience that we can recall in connection with our\r\nengagement. But it didn't prove fatal. We got through it somehow. We\r\ndined with Aunt Jane, and wined with Uncle Joseph, and perhaps had two\r\nfingers given to us by old Cousin Horatio, whose enormous fortune was of\r\nthe greatest importance to everybody. And perhaps fragments of the other\r\nfamily's estimate of us subsequently reached our own ears. But if a\r\nchosen lover cannot stand being treated as a specimen by the other\r\nfamily, he's a very weak vessel, and not worth any good girl's love.\r\nThat's all I can say for him.\r\n\r\nNow the Virginian was scarcely what even his enemy would term a weak\r\nvessel; and Molly's jealousy of the impression which he might make upon\r\nBennington was vastly superfluous. She should have known that he would\r\nindeed care to make a good impression; but that such anxiety on his part\r\nwould be wholly for her sake, that in the eyes of her friends she might\r\nstand justified in taking him for her wedded husband. So far as he was\r\nconcerned apart from her, Aunt Jane and Uncle Joseph might say anything\r\nthey pleased, or think anything they pleased. His character was open for\r\ninvestigation. Judge Henry would vouch for him.\r\n\r\nThis is what he would have said to his sweetheart had she but revealed\r\nto him her perturbations. But she did not reveal them; and they were not\r\nof the order that he with his nature was likely to divine. I do not\r\nknow what good would have come from her speaking out to him, unless that\r\nperfect understanding between lovers which indeed is a good thing. But\r\nI do not believe that he could have reassured her; and I am certain that\r\nshe could not have prevented his writing to her mother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then,\xe2\x80\x9d she sighed at last, \xe2\x80\x9cif you think so, I will tell her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat sigh of hers, be it well understood, was not only because of those\r\nfar-off voices which the world would in consequence of her news be\r\nlifting presently. It came also from bidding farewell to the fairy-tale\r\nwhich she must leave now; that land in which she and he had been living\r\nclose together alone, unhindered, unmindful of all things.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you will tell her,\xe2\x80\x9d said her lover. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I must tell her too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBoth of us?\xe2\x80\x9d questioned the girl.\r\n\r\nWhat would he say to her mother? How would her mother like such a letter\r\nas he would write to her? Suppose he should misspell a word? Would not\r\nsentences from him at this time--written sentences--be a further bar to\r\nhis welcome acceptance at Bennington?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy don't you send messages by me?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked him.\r\n\r\nHe shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is not going to like it, anyway,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must speak to her direct. It would be like shirking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMolly saw how true his instinct was here; and a little flame shot upward\r\nfrom the glow of her love and pride in him. Oh, if they could all only\r\nknow that he was like this when you understood him! She did not dare say\r\nout to him what her fear was about this letter of his to her mother. She\r\ndid not dare because--well, because she lacked a little faith. That is\r\nit, I am afraid. And for that sin she was her own punishment. For in\r\nthis day, and in many days to come, the pure joy of her love was vexed\r\nand clouded, all through a little lack of faith; while for him, perfect\r\nin his faith, his joy was like crystal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me what you're going to write,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nHe smiled at her. \xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAren't you going to let me see it when it's done?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d Then a freakish look came into his eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cI'll let yu' see\r\nanything I write to other women.\xe2\x80\x9d And he gave her one of his long\r\nkisses. \xe2\x80\x9cLet's get through with it together,\xe2\x80\x9d he suggested, when they\r\nwere once more in his sick-room, that room which she had given to him.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou'll sit one side o' the table, and I'll sit the other, and we'll go\r\nahaid; and pretty soon it will be done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO dear!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, I suppose that is the best way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd so, accordingly, they took their places. The inkstand stood between\r\nthem. Beside each of them she distributed paper enough, almost, for a\r\npresidential message. And pens and pencils were in plenty. Was this not\r\nthe headquarters of the Bear Creek schoolmarm?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, aren't you going to do it in pencil first?\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, looking\r\nup from her vacant sheet. His pen was moving slowly, but steadily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don't reckon I need to,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, with his nose close to the\r\npaper. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, damnation, there's a blot!\xe2\x80\x9d He tore his spoiled beginning in\r\nsmall bits, and threw them into the fireplace. \xe2\x80\x9cYou've got it too full,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n he commented; and taking the inkstand, he tipped a little from it out\r\nof the window. She sat lost among her false starts. Had she heard him\r\nswear, she would not have minded. She rather liked it when he swore. He\r\npossessed that quality in his profanity of not offending by it. It is\r\nquite wonderful how much worse the same word will sound in one man's\r\nlips than in another's. But she did not hear him. Her mind was among a\r\nlitter of broken sentences. Each thought which she began ran out into\r\nthe empty air, or came against some stone wall. So there she sat, her\r\neyes now upon that inexorable blank sheet that lay before her, waiting,\r\nand now turned with vacant hopelessness upon the sundry objects in the\r\nroom. And while she thus sat accomplishing nothing, opposite to her the\r\nblack head bent down, and the steady pen moved from phrase to phrase.\r\n\r\nShe became aware of his gazing at her, flushed and solemn. That strange\r\ncolor of the sea-water, which she could never name, was lustrous in his\r\neyes. He was folding his letter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have finished?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d His voice was very quiet. \xe2\x80\x9cI feel like an honester man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps I can do something to-night at Mrs. Taylor's,\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\nlooking at her paper.\r\n\r\nOn it were a few words crossed out. This was all she had to show. At\r\nthis set task in letter-writing, the cow-puncher had greatly excelled\r\nthe schoolmarm!\r\n\r\nBut that night, while he lay quite fast asleep in his bed, she was\r\nkeeping vigil in her room at Mrs. Taylor's.\r\n\r\nAccordingly, the next day, those three letters departed for the mail,\r\nand Mrs. Taylor consequently made her exclamation, \xe2\x80\x9cIt's come!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn the day before the Virginian returned to take up his work at Judge\r\nHenry's ranch, he and Molly announced their news. What Molly said to\r\nMrs. Taylor and what Mrs. Taylor said to her, is of no interest to us,\r\nthough it was of much to them.\r\n\r\nBut Mr. McLean happened to make a call quite early in the morning to\r\ninquire for his friend's health.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLin,\xe2\x80\x9d began the Virginian, \xe2\x80\x9cthere is no harm in your knowing an hour or\r\nso before the rest, I am--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. McLean, indulgently. \xe2\x80\x9cEverybody has knowed that since\r\nthe day she found yu' at the spring.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was not so, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, crossly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord! Everybody has knowed it right along.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHmp!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn't know this country was that rank\r\nwith gossips.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. McLean laughed mirthfully at the lover. \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cMrs.\r\nMcLean will be glad. She told me to give yu' her congratulations quite\r\na while ago. I was to have 'em ready just as soon as ever yu' asked for\r\n'em yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d Lin had been made a happy man some twelve months previous\r\nto this. And now, by way of an exchange of news, he added: \xe2\x80\x9cWe're\r\nexpectin' a little McLean down on Box Elder. That's what you'll be\r\nexpectin' some of these days, I hope.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the Virginian, \xe2\x80\x9cI hope so too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I don't guess,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lin, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you and I will do much shufflin' of\r\nother folks' children any more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhereupon he and the Virginian shook hands silently, and understood each\r\nother very well.\r\n\r\nOn the day that the Virginian parted with Molly, beside the weight of\r\nfarewell which lay heavy on his heart, his thoughts were also grave with\r\nnews. The cattle thieves had grown more audacious. Horses and cattle\r\nboth were being missed, and each man began almost to doubt his neighbor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSteps will have to be taken soon by somebody, I reckon,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nlover.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy you?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked quickly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMost likely I'll get mixed up with it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat will you have to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan't say. I'll tell yu' when I come back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo did he part from her, leaving her more kisses than words to remember.\r\n\r\nAnd what was doing at Bennington, meanwhile, and at Dunbarton? Those\r\nthree letters which by their mere outside had so moved Mrs. Taylor,\r\nproduced by their contents much painful disturbance.\r\n\r\nIt will be remembered that Molly wrote to her mother, and to her\r\ngreat-aunt. That announcement to her mother was undertaken first. Its\r\ncomposition occupied three hours and a half, and it filled eleven pages,\r\nnot counting a postscript upon the twelfth. The letter to the great-aunt\r\ntook only ten minutes. I cannot pretend to explain why this one was\r\nso greatly superior to the other; but such is the remarkable fact. Its\r\nbeginning, to be sure, did give the old lady a start; she had dismissed\r\nthe cow-boy from her probabilities.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTut, tut, tut!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed out loud in her bedroom. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has thrown\r\nherself away on that fellow!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut some sentences at the end made her pause and sit still for a long\r\nwhile. The severity upon her face changed to tenderness, gradually. \xe2\x80\x9cAh,\r\nme,\xe2\x80\x9d she sighed. \xe2\x80\x9cIf marriage were as simple as love!\xe2\x80\x9d Then she went\r\nslowly downstairs, and out into her garden, where she walked long\r\nbetween the box borders. \xe2\x80\x9cBut if she has found a great love,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nold lady at length. And she returned to her bedroom, and opened an old\r\ndesk, and read some old letters.\r\n\r\nThere came to her the next morning a communication from Bennington. This\r\nhad been penned frantically by poor Mrs. Wood. As soon as she had been\r\nable to gather her senses after the shock of her daughter's eleven pages\r\nand the postscript, the mother had poured out eight pages herself to the\r\neldest member of the family. There had been, indeed, much excuse for the\r\npoor lady. To begin with, Molly had constructed her whole opening\r\npage with the express and merciful intention of preparing her mother.\r\nConsequently, it made no sense whatever. Its effect was the usual effect\r\nof remarks designed to break a thing gently. It merely made Mrs. Wood's\r\nhead swim, and filled her with a sickening dread. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, mercy, Sarah,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n she had cried, \xe2\x80\x9ccome here. What does this mean?\xe2\x80\x9d And then, fortified by\r\nher elder daughter, she had turned over that first page and found what\r\nit meant on the top of the second. \xe2\x80\x9cA savage with knives and pistols!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n she wailed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, mother, I always told you so,\xe2\x80\x9d said her daughter Sarah.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is a foreman?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the mother. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd who is Judge Henry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has taken a sort of upper servant,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sarah. \xe2\x80\x9cIf it is allowed\r\nto go as far as a wedding, I doubt if I can bring myself to be present.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n (This threat she proceeded to make to Molly, with results that shall be\r\nset forth in their proper place.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe man appears to have written to me himself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Wood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe knows no better,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sarah.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBosh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sarah's husband later. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was a very manly thing to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Thus did consternation rage in the house at Bennington. Molly might\r\nhave spared herself the many assurances that she gave concerning\r\nthe universal esteem in which her cow-puncher was held, and the fair\r\nprospects which were his. So, in the first throes of her despair, Mrs.\r\nWood wrote those eight not maturely considered pages to the great-aunt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTut, tut, tut!\xe2\x80\x9d said the great-aunt as she read them. Her face was much\r\nmore severe to-day. \xe2\x80\x9cYou'd suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the girl had been\r\nkidnapped! Why, she has kept him waiting three years!\xe2\x80\x9d And then she\r\nread more, but soon put the letter down with laughter. For Mrs. Wood\r\nhad repeated in writing that early outburst of hers about a savage with\r\nknives and pistols. \xe2\x80\x9cLaw!\xe2\x80\x9d said the great-aunt. \xe2\x80\x9cLaw, what a fool Lizzie\r\nis!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo she sat down and wrote to Mrs. Wood a wholesome reply about putting\r\na little more trust in her own flesh and blood, and reminding her among\r\nother things that General Stark had himself been wont to carry knives\r\nand pistols owing to the necessities of his career, but that he had\r\noccasionally taken them off, as did probably this young man in Wyoming.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had better send me the letter he has written you,\xe2\x80\x9d she concluded.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall know much better what to think after I have seen that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt is not probable that Mrs. Wood got much comfort from this\r\ncommunication; and her daughter Sarah was actually enraged by it.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe grows more perverse as she nears her dotage,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sarah. But the\r\nVirginian's letter was sent to Dunbarton, where the old lady sat herself\r\ndown to read it with much attention.\r\n\r\nHere is what the Virginian had said to the unknown mother of his\r\nsweetheart.\r\n\r\nMRS. JOHN STARK WOOD Bennington, Vermont.\r\n\r\nMadam: If your daughter Miss Wood has ever told you about her saving\r\na man's life here when some Indians had shot him that is the man who\r\nwrites to you now. I don't think she can have told you right about\r\nthat affair for she is the only one in this country who thinks it was\r\na little thing. So I must tell you it, the main points. Such an action\r\nwould have been thought highly of in a Western girl, but with Miss\r\nWood's raising nobody had a right to expect it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed!\xe2\x80\x9d snorted the great-aunt. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, he would be right, if I had not\r\nhad a good deal more to do with her 'raising' than ever Lizzie had.\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\nshe went on with the letter.\r\n\r\nI was starting in to die when she found me. I did not know anything\r\nthen, and she pulled me back from where I was half in the next world.\r\nShe did not know but what Indians would get her too but I could not make\r\nher leave me. I am a heavy man one hundred and seventy-three stripped\r\nwhen in full health. She lifted me herself from the ground me helping\r\nscarce any for there was not much help in me that day. She washed my\r\nwound and brought me to with her own whiskey. Before she could get me\r\nhome I was out of my head but she kept me on my horse somehow and talked\r\nwisely to me so I minded her and did not go clean crazy till she had got\r\nme safe to bed. The doctor says I would have died all the same if she\r\nhad not nursed me the way she did. It made me love her more which I did\r\nnot know I could. But there is no end, for this writing it down makes me\r\nlove her more as I write it.\r\n\r\nAnd now Mrs. Wood I am sorry this will be bad news for you to hear. I\r\nknow you would never choose such a man as I am for her for I have got\r\nno education and must write humble against my birth. I wish I could make\r\nthe news easier but truth is the best.\r\n\r\nI am of old stock in Virginia. English and one Scotch Irish grandmother\r\nmy father's father brought from Kentucky. We have always stayed at the\r\nsame place farmers and hunters not bettering our lot and very plain. We\r\nhave fought when we got the chance, under Old Hickory and in Mexico and\r\nmy father and two brothers were killed in the Valley sixty-four. Always\r\nwith us one son has been apt to run away and I was the one this time. I\r\nhad too much older brothering to suit me. But now I am doing well being\r\nin full sight of prosperity and not too old and very strong my health\r\nhaving stood the sundries it has been put through. She shall teach\r\nschool no more when she is mine. I wish I could make this news easier\r\nfor you Mrs. Wood. I do not like promises I have heard so many. I will\r\ntell any man of your family anything he likes to ask one, and Judge\r\nHenry would tell you about my reputation. I have seen plenty rough\r\nthings but can say I have never killed for pleasure or profit and am not\r\none of that kind, always preferring peace. I have had to live in places\r\nwhere they had courts and lawyers so called but an honest man was all\r\nthe law you could find in five hundred miles. I have not told her about\r\nthose things not because I am ashamed of them but there are so many\r\nthings too dark for a girl like her to hear about.\r\n\r\nI had better tell you the way I know I love Miss Wood. I am not a boy\r\nnow, and women are no new thing to me. A man like me who has travelled\r\nmeets many of them as he goes and passes on but I stopped when I came\r\nto Miss Wood. That is three years but I have not gone on. What right has\r\nsuch as he? you will say. So did I say it after she had saved my life.\r\nIt was hard to get to that point and keep there with her around me all\r\nday. But I said to myself you have bothered her for three years with\r\nyour love and if you let your love bother her you don't love her like\r\nyou should and you must quit for her sake who has saved your life. I did\r\nnot know what I was going to do with my life after that but I supposed\r\nI could go somewhere and work hard and so Mrs. Wood I told her I would\r\ngive her up. But she said no. It is going to be hard for her to get used\r\nto a man like me--\r\n\r\nBut at this point in the Virginian's letter, the old great-aunt could\r\nread no more. She rose, and went over to that desk where lay those faded\r\nletters of her own. She laid her head down upon the package, and as her\r\ntears flowed quietly upon it, \xe2\x80\x9cO dear,\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered, \xe2\x80\x9cO dear! And this\r\nis what I lost!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo her girl upon Bear Creek she wrote the next day. And this word from\r\nDunbarton was like balm among the harsh stings Molly was receiving. The\r\nvoices of the world reached her in gathering numbers, and not one of\r\nthem save that great-aunt's was sweet. Her days were full of hurts; and\r\nthere was no one by to kiss the hurts away. Nor did she even hear from\r\nher lover any more now. She only knew he had gone into lonely regions\r\nupon his errand.\r\n\r\nThat errand took him far:-- Across the Basin, among the secret places\r\nof Owl Creek, past the Washakie Needles, over the Divide to Gros Ventre,\r\nand so through a final barrier of peaks into the borders of East Idaho.\r\nThere, by reason of his bidding me, I met him, and came to share in a\r\npart of his errand.\r\n\r\nIt was with no guide that I travelled to him. He had named a little\r\nstation on the railroad, and from thence he had charted my route by\r\nmeans of landmarks. Did I believe in omens, the black storm that I set\r\nout in upon my horse would seem like one to-day. But I had been living\r\nin cities and smoke; and Idaho, even with rain, was delightful to me.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXX. A STABLE ON THE FLAT\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen the first landmark, the lone clump of cottonwoods, came at length\r\nin sight, dark and blurred in the gentle rain, standing out perhaps\r\na mile beyond the distant buildings, my whole weary body hailed the\r\napproach of repose. Saving the noon hour, I had been in the saddle since\r\nsix, and now six was come round again. The ranch, my resting-place for\r\nthis night, was a ruin--cabin, stable, and corral. Yet after the twelve\r\nhours of pushing on and on through silence, still to have silence, still\r\nto eat and go to sleep in it, perfectly fitted the mood of both my flesh\r\nand spirit. At noon, when for a while I had thrown off my long oilskin\r\ncoat, merely the sight of the newspaper half crowded into my pocket had\r\nbeen a displeasing reminder of the railway, and cities, and affairs. But\r\nfor its possible help to build fires, it would have come no farther with\r\nme. The great levels around me lay cooled and freed of dust by the\r\nwet weather, and full of sweet airs. Far in front the foot-hills rose\r\nthrough the rain, indefinite and mystic. I wanted no speech with any\r\none, nor to be near human beings at all. I was steeped in a revery as of\r\nthe primal earth; even thoughts themselves had almost ceased motion. To\r\nlie down with wild animals, with elk and deer, would have made my waking\r\ndream complete; and since such dream could not be, the cattle around the\r\ndeserted buildings, mere dots as yet across separating space, were my\r\nproper companions for this evening.\r\n\r\nTo-morrow night I should probably be camping with the Virginian in the\r\nfoot-hills. At his letter's bidding I had come eastward across Idaho,\r\nabandoning my hunting in the Saw Tooth Range to make this journey with\r\nhim back through the Tetons. It was a trail known to him, and not to\r\nmany other honest men. Horse Thief Pass was the name his letter gave it.\r\nBusiness (he was always brief) would call him over there at this time.\r\nReturning, he must attend to certain matters in the Wind River country.\r\nThere I could leave by stage for the railroad, or go on with him the\r\nwhole way back to Sunk Creek. He designated for our meeting the forks\r\nof a certain little stream in the foot-hills which to-day's ride had\r\nbrought in sight. There would be no chance for him to receive an answer\r\nfrom me in the intervening time. If by a certain day--which was four\r\ndays off still--I had not reached the forks, he would understand I had\r\nother plans. To me it was like living back in ages gone, this way of\r\nmeeting my friend, this choice of a stream so far and lonely that its\r\nvery course upon the maps was wrongly traced. And to leave behind all\r\nnoise and mechanisms, and set out at ease, slowly, with one packhorse,\r\ninto the wilderness, made me feel that the ancient earth was indeed my\r\nmother and that I had found her again after being lost among houses,\r\ncustoms, and restraints. I should arrive three days early at the\r\nforks--three days of margin seeming to me a wise precaution against\r\ndelays unforeseen. If the Virginian were not there, good; I could fish\r\nand be happy. If he were there but not ready to start, good; I could\r\nstill fish and be happy. And remembering my Eastern helplessness in\r\nthe year when we had met first, I enjoyed thinking how I had come to be\r\ntrusted. In those days I had not been allowed to go from the ranch for\r\nso much as an afternoon's ride unless tied to him by a string, so to\r\nspeak; now I was crossing unmapped spaces with no guidance. The man who\r\ncould do this was scarce any longer a \xe2\x80\x9ctenderfoot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMy vision, as I rode, took in serenely the dim foot-hills,--to-morrow's\r\ngoal,--and nearer in the vast wet plain the clump of cottonwoods, and\r\nstill nearer my lodging for to-night with the dotted cattle round it.\r\nAnd now my horse neighed. I felt his gait freshen for the journey's\r\nend, and leaning to pat his neck I noticed his ears no longer slack and\r\ninattentive, but pointing forward to where food and rest awaited both of\r\nus. Twice he neighed, impatiently and long; and as he quickened his gait\r\nstill more, the packhorse did the same, and I realized that there was\r\nabout me still a spice of the tenderfoot: those dots were not cattle;\r\nthey were horses.\r\n\r\nMy horse had put me in the wrong. He had known his kind from afar,\r\nand was hastening to them. The plainsman's eye was not yet mine; and I\r\nsmiled a little as I rode. When was I going to know, as by instinct, the\r\ndifferent look of horses and cattle across some two or three miles of\r\nplain?\r\n\r\nThese miles we finished soon. The buildings changed in their aspect as\r\nthey grew to my approach, showing their desolation more clearly, and in\r\nsome way bringing apprehension into my mood. And around them the horses,\r\ntoo, all standing with ears erect, watching me as I came--there was\r\nsomething about them; or was it the silence? For the silence which I had\r\nliked until now seemed suddenly to be made too great by the presence of\r\nthe deserted buildings. And then the door of the stable opened, and\r\nmen came out and stood, also watching me arrive. By the time I was\r\ndismounting more were there. It was senseless to feel as unpleasant as\r\nI did, and I strove to give to them a greeting that should sound easy.\r\nI told them that I hoped there was room for one more here to-night. Some\r\nof them had answered my greeting, but none of them answered this; and\r\nas I began to be sure that I recognized several of their strangely\r\nimperturbable faces, the Virginian came from the stable; and at that\r\nwelcome sight my relief spoke out instantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am here, you see!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I do see.\xe2\x80\x9d I looked hard at him, for in his voice was the same\r\nstrangeness that I felt in everything around me. But he was looking at\r\nhis companions. \xe2\x80\x9cThis gentleman is all right,\xe2\x80\x9d he told them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat may be,\xe2\x80\x9d said one whom I now knew that I had seen before at Sunk\r\nCreek; \xe2\x80\x9cbut he was not due to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor to-morrow,\xe2\x80\x9d said another.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor yet the day after,\xe2\x80\x9d a third added.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian fell into his drawl. \xe2\x80\x9cNone of you was ever early for\r\nanything, I presume.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOne retorted, laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, we're not suspicioning you of complicity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd another, \xe2\x80\x9cNot even when we remember how thick you and Steve used to\r\nbe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhatever jokes they meant by this he did not receive as jokes. I saw\r\nsomething like a wince pass over his face, and a flush follow it. But he\r\nnow spoke to me. \xe2\x80\x9cWe expected to be through before this,\xe2\x80\x9d he began. \xe2\x80\x9cI'm\r\nright sorry you have come to-night. I know you'd have preferred to keep\r\naway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe want him to explain himself,\xe2\x80\x9d put in one of the others. \xe2\x80\x9cIf he\r\nsatisfies us, he's free to go away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFree to go away!\xe2\x80\x9d I now exclaimed. But at the indulgence in their\r\nfrontier smile I cooled down. \xe2\x80\x9cGentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cI don't know why my\r\nmovements interest you so much. It's quite a compliment! May I get under\r\nshelter while I explain?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo request could have been more natural, for the rain had now begun to\r\nfall in straight floods. Yet there was a pause before one of them said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe might as well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Virginian chose to say nothing more; but he walked beside me into\r\nthe stable. Two men sat there together, and a third guarded them. At\r\nthat sight I knew suddenly what I had stumbled upon; and on the impulse\r\nI murmured to the Virginian, \xe2\x80\x9cYou're hanging them to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe kept his silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may have three guesses,\xe2\x80\x9d said a man behind me.\r\n\r\nBut I did not need them. And in the recoil of my insight the clump\r\nof cottonwoods came into my mind, black and grim. No other trees high\r\nenough grew within ten miles. This, then, was the business that the\r\nVirginian's letter had so curtly mentioned. My eyes went into all\r\ncorners of the stable, but no other prisoners were here. I half expected\r\nto see Trampas, and I half feared to see Shorty; for poor stupid\r\nShorty's honesty had not been proof against frontier temptations, and he\r\nhad fallen away from the company of his old friends. Often of late I\r\nhad heard talk at Sunk Creek of breaking up a certain gang of horse and\r\ncattle thieves that stole in one Territory and sold in the next, and\r\nknew where to hide in the mountains between. And now it had come to the\r\npoint; forces had been gathered, a long expedition made, and here they\r\nwere, successful under the Virginian's lead, but a little later than\r\ntheir calculations. And here was I, a little too early, and a witness in\r\nconsequence. My presence seemed a simple thing to account for; but when\r\nI had thus accounted for it, one of them said with good nature:-- \xe2\x80\x9cSo\r\nyou find us here, and we find you here. Which is the most surprised, I\r\nwonder?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere's no telling,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, keeping as amiable as I could; \xe2\x80\x9cnor any\r\ntelling which objects the most.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, there's no objection here. You're welcome to stay. But not welcome\r\nto go, I expect. He ain't welcome to go, is he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBy the answers that their faces gave him it was plain that I was not.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot till we are through,\xe2\x80\x9d said one.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe needn't to see anything,\xe2\x80\x9d' another added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetter sleep late to-morrow morning,\xe2\x80\x9d a third suggested to me.\r\n\r\nI did not wish to stay here. I could have made some sort of camp apart\r\nfrom them before dark; but in the face of their needless caution I was\r\nhelpless. I made no attempt to inquire what kind of spy they imagined I\r\ncould be, what sort of rescue I could bring in this lonely country; my\r\ntoo early appearance seemed to be all that they looked at. And again\r\nmy eyes sought the prisoners. Certainly there were only two. One was\r\nchewing tobacco, and talking now and then to his guard as if nothing\r\nwere the matter. The other sat dull in silence, not moving his eyes;\r\nbut his face worked, and I noticed how he continually moistened his dry\r\nlips. As I looked at these doomed prisoners, whose fate I was invited to\r\nsleep through to-morrow morning, the one who was chewing quietly nodded\r\nto me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don't remember me?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nIt was Steve! Steve of Medicine Bow! The pleasant Steve of my first\r\nevening in the West. Some change of beard had delayed my instant\r\nrecognition of his face. Here he sat sentenced to die. A shock, chill\r\nand painful, deprived me of speech.\r\n\r\nHe had no such weak feelings. \xe2\x80\x9cHave yu' been to Medicine Bow lately?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ninquired. \xe2\x80\x9cThat's getting to be quite a while ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI assented. I should have liked to say something natural and kind,\r\nbut words stuck against my will, and I stood awkward and ill at ease,\r\nnoticing idly that the silent one wore a gray flannel shirt like mine.\r\nSteve looked me over, and saw in my pocket the newspaper which I had\r\nbrought from the railroad and on which I had pencilled a few expenses.\r\nHe asked me, Would I mind letting him have it for a while? And I gave\r\nit to him eagerly, begging him to keep it as long as he wanted. I was\r\novereager in my embarrassment. \xe2\x80\x9cYou need not return it at all,\xe2\x80\x9d I said;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthose notes are nothing. Do keep it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe gave me a short glance and a smile. \xe2\x80\x9cThank you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cI'll not\r\nneed it beyond to-morrow morning.\xe2\x80\x9d And he began to search through it.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJake's election is considered sure,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to his companion, who\r\nmade no response. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, Fremont County owes it to Jake.\xe2\x80\x9d And I left him\r\ninterested in the local news.\r\n\r\nDead men I have seen not a few times, even some lying pale and terrible\r\nafter violent ends, and the edge of this wears off; but I hope I shall\r\nnever again have to be in the company with men waiting to be killed.\r\nBy this time to-morrow the gray flannel shirt would be buttoned round\r\na corpse. Until what moment would Steve chew? Against such fancies as\r\nthese I managed presently to barricade my mind, but I made a plea to be\r\nallowed to pass the night elsewhere, and I suggested the adjacent cabin.\r\nBy their faces I saw that my words merely helped their distrust of me.\r\nThe cabin leaked too much, they said; I would sleep drier here. One man\r\ngave it to me more directly: \xe2\x80\x9cIf you figured on camping in this stable,\r\nwhat has changed your mind?\xe2\x80\x9d How could I tell them that I shrunk from\r\nany contact with what they were doing, although I knew that only so\r\ncould justice be dealt in this country? Their wholesome frontier nerves\r\nknew nothing of such refinements.\r\n\r\nBut the Virginian understood part of it. \xe2\x80\x9cI am right sorry for your\r\nannoyance,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. And now I noticed he was under a constraint very\r\ndifferent from the ease of the others.\r\n\r\nAfter the twelve hours' ride my bones were hungry for rest. I spread my\r\nblankets on some straw in a stall by myself and rolled up in them; yet\r\nI lay growing broader awake, every inch of weariness stricken from my\r\nexcited senses. For a while they sat over their councils, whispering\r\ncautiously, so that I was made curious to hear them by not being able;\r\nwas it the names of Trampas and Shorty that were once or twice spoken--I\r\ncould not be sure. I heard the whisperers cease and separate. I heard\r\ntheir boots as they cast them off upon the ground. And I heard the\r\nbreathing of slumber begin and grow in the interior silence. To one\r\nafter one sleep came, but not to me. Outside, the dull fall of the rain\r\nbeat evenly, and in some angle dripped the spouting pulses of a leak.\r\nSometimes a cold air blew in, bearing with it the keen wet odor of the\r\nsage-brush. On hundreds of other nights this perfume had been my last\r\nwaking remembrance; it had seemed to help drowsiness; and now I lay\r\nstaring, thinking of this. Twice through the hours the thieves shifted\r\ntheir positions with clumsy sounds, exchanging muted words with their\r\nguard. So, often, had I heard other companions move and mutter in the\r\ndarkness and lie down again. It was the very naturalness and usualness\r\nof every fact of the night,--the stable straw, the rain outside, my\r\nfamiliar blankets, the cool visits of the wind,--and with all this the\r\nthought of Steve chewing and the man in the gray flannel shirt, that\r\nmade the hours unearthly and strung me tight with suspense. And at last\r\nI heard some one get up and begin to dress. In a little while I saw\r\nlight suddenly through my closed eyelids, and then darkness shut again\r\nabruptly upon them. They had swung in a lantern and found me by mistake.\r\nI was the only one they did not wish to rouse. Moving and quiet talking\r\nset up around me, and they began to go out of the stable. At the gleams\r\nof new daylight which they let in my thoughts went to the clump of\r\ncottonwoods, and I lay still with hands and feet growing steadily\r\ncold. Now it was going to happen. I wondered how they would do it; one\r\ninstance had been described to me by a witness, but that was done from a\r\nbridge, and there had been but a single victim. This morning, would one\r\nhave to wait and see the other go through with it first?\r\n\r\nThe smell of smoke reached me, and next the rattle of tin dishes.\r\nBreakfast was something I had forgotten, and one of them was cooking it\r\nnow in the dry shelter of the stable. He was alone, because the talking\r\nand the steps were outside the stable, and I could hear the sounds of\r\nhorses being driven into the corral and saddled. Then I perceived that\r\nthe coffee was ready, and almost immediately the cook called them. One\r\ncame in, shutting the door behind him as he reentered, which the rest as\r\nthey followed imitated; for at each opening of the door I saw the light\r\nof day leap into the stable and heard the louder sounds of the rain.\r\nThen the sound and the light would again be shut out, until some one at\r\nlength spoke out bluntly, bidding the door be left open on account of\r\nthe smoke. What were they hiding from? he asked. The runaways that had\r\nescaped? A laugh followed this sally, and the door was left open. Thus\r\nI learned that there had been more thieves than the two that were\r\ncaptured. It gave a little more ground for their suspicion about me and\r\nmy anxiety to pass the night elsewhere. It cost nothing to detain me,\r\nand they were taking no chances, however remote.\r\n\r\nThe fresh air and the light now filled the stable, and I lay listening\r\nwhile their breakfast brought more talk from them. They were more at\r\nease now than was I, who had nothing to do but carry out my role of\r\nslumber in the stall; they spoke in a friendly, ordinary way, as if this\r\nwere like every other morning of the week to them. They addressed the\r\nprisoners with a sort of fraternal kindness, not bringing them pointedly\r\ninto the conversation, nor yet pointedly leaving them out. I made out\r\nthat they must all be sitting round the breakfast together, those who\r\nhad to die and those who had to kill them. The Virginian I never heard\r\nspeak. But I heard the voice of Steve; he discussed with his captors the\r\nsundry points of his capture.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you remember a haystack?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked. \xe2\x80\x9cAway up the south fork of Gros\r\nVentre?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was Thursday afternoon,\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the captors. \xe2\x80\x9cThere was a\r\nshower.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. It rained. We had you fooled that time. I was laying on the ledge\r\nabove to report your movements.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSeveral of them laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cWe thought you were over on Spread Creek\r\nthen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI figured you thought so by the trail you left after the stack.\r\nSaturday we watched you turn your back on us up Spread Creek. We were\r\nsnug among the trees the other side of Snake River. That was another\r\ntime we had you fooled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey laughed again at their own expense. I have heard men pick to pieces\r\na hand of whist with more antagonism.\r\n\r\nSteve continued: \xe2\x80\x9cWould we head for Idaho? Would we swing back over the\r\nDivide? You didn't know which! And when we generalled you on to that\r\nband of horses you thought was the band you were hunting--ah, we were a\r\nstrong combination!\xe2\x80\x9d He broke off with the first touch of bitterness I\r\nhad felt in his words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing is any stronger than its weakest point.\xe2\x80\x9d It was the Virginian\r\nwho said this, and it was the first word he had spoken.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNaturally,\xe2\x80\x9d said Steve. His tone in addressing the Virginian was so\r\ndifferent, so curt, that I supposed he took the weakest point to\r\nmean himself. But the others now showed me that I was wrong in this\r\nexplanation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's so,\xe2\x80\x9d one said. \xe2\x80\x9cIts weakest point is where a rope or a gang of\r\nmen is going to break when the strain comes.
