And you was linked with a\r\npoor partner, Steve.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou're right I was,\xe2\x80\x9d said the prisoner, back in his easy, casual voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ought to have got yourself separated from him, Steve.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a pause. \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said the prisoner, moodily. \xe2\x80\x9cI'm sitting here\r\nbecause one of us blundered.\xe2\x80\x9d He cursed the blunderer. \xe2\x80\x9cLighting his\r\nfool fire queered the whole deal,\xe2\x80\x9d he added. As he again heavily cursed\r\nthe blunderer, the others murmured to each other various I told you\r\nso's.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou'd never have built that fire, Steve,\xe2\x80\x9d said one.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said that when we spied the smoke,\xe2\x80\x9d said another. \xe2\x80\x9cI said, 'That's\r\nnone of Steve's work, lighting fires and revealing to us their\r\nwhereabouts.'\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt struck me that they were plying Steve with compliments.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPretty hard to have the fool get away and you get caught,\xe2\x80\x9d a third\r\nsuggested. At this they seemed to wait. I felt something curious in all\r\nthis last talk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, did he get away?\xe2\x80\x9d said the prisoner, then.\r\n\r\nAgain they waited; and a new voice spoke huskily:-- \xe2\x80\x9cI built that fire,\r\nboys.\xe2\x80\x9d It was the prisoner in the gray flannel shirt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo late, Ed,\xe2\x80\x9d they told him kindly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou ain't a good liar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat makes you laugh, Steve?\xe2\x80\x9d said some one.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, the things I notice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMeaning Ed was pretty slow in backing up your play? The joke is really\r\non you, Steve. You'd ought never to have cursed the fire-builder if\r\nyou wanted us to believe he was present. But we'd not have done much to\r\nShorty, even if we had caught him. All he wants is to be scared good and\r\nhard, and he'll go back into virtuousness, which is his nature when not\r\ntravelling with Trampas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSteve's voice sounded hard now. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have caught Ed and me. That should\r\nsatisfy you for one gather.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we think different, Steve. Trampas escaping leaves this thing\r\nunfinished.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo Trampas escaped too, did he?\xe2\x80\x9d said the prisoner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Steve, Trampas escaped--this time; and Shorty with him--this time.\r\nWe know it most as well as if we'd seen them go. And we're glad Shorty\r\nis loose, for he'll build another fire or do some other foolishness next\r\ntime, and that's the time we'll get Trampas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTheir talk drifted to other points, and I lay thinking of the skirmish\r\nthat had played beneath the surface of their banter. Yes, the joke, as\r\nthey put it, was on Steve. He had lost one point in the game to them.\r\nThey were playing for names. He, being a chivalrous thief, was playing\r\nto hide names. They could only, among several likely confederates, guess\r\nTrampas and Shorty. So it had been a slip for him to curse the man\r\nwho built the fire. At least, they so held it. For, they with subtlety\r\nreasoned, one curses the absent. And I agreed with them that Ed did not\r\nknow how to lie well; he should have at once claimed the disgrace\r\nof having spoiled the expedition. If Shorty was the blunderer, then\r\ncertainly Trampas was the other man; for the two were as inseparable as\r\ndon and master. Trampas had enticed Shorty away from good, and trained\r\nhim in evil. It now struck me that after his single remark the Virginian\r\nhad been silent throughout their shrewd discussion.\r\n\r\nIt was the other prisoner that I heard them next address. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don't eat\r\nany breakfast, Ed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBrace up, Ed. Look at Steve, how hardy he eats!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Ed, it seemed, wanted no breakfast. And the tin dishes rattled as\r\nthey were gathered and taken to be packed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDrink this coffee, anyway,\xe2\x80\x9d another urged; \xe2\x80\x9cyou'll feel warmer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThese words almost made it seem like my own execution. My whole body\r\nturned cold in company with the prisoner's, and as if with a clank the\r\nsituation tightened throughout my senses.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon if every one's ready we'll start.\xe2\x80\x9d It was the Virginian's\r\nvoice once more, and different from the rest. I heard them rise at his\r\nbidding, and I put the blanket over my head. I felt their tread as they\r\nwalked out, passing my stall. The straw that was half under me and half\r\nout in the stable was stirred as by something heavy dragged or half\r\nlifted along over it. \xe2\x80\x9cLook out, you're hurting Ed's arm,\xe2\x80\x9d one said to\r\nanother, as the steps with tangled sounds passed slowly out. I heard\r\nanother among those who followed say, \xe2\x80\x9cPoor Ed couldn't swallow his\r\ncoffee.\xe2\x80\x9d Outside they began getting on their horses; and next their\r\nhoofs grew distant, until all was silence round the stable except the\r\ndull, even falling of the rain.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXXI. THE COTTONWOODS\r\n\r\n\r\nI do not know how long I stayed there alone. It was the Virginian who\r\ncame back, and as he stood at the foot of my blankets his eye, after\r\nmeeting mine full for a moment, turned aside. I had never seen him\r\nlook as he did now, not even in Pitchstone Canyon when we came upon the\r\nbodies of Hank and his wife. Until this moment we had found no chance of\r\nspeaking together, except in the presence of others.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeems to be raining still,\xe2\x80\x9d I began after a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. It's a wet spell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stared out of the door, smoothing his mustache.\r\n\r\nIt was again I that spoke. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat time is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe brooded over his watch. \xe2\x80\x9cTwelve minutes to seven.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI rose and stood drawing on my clothes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe fire's out,\xe2\x80\x9d said he; and he assembled some new sticks over the\r\nashes. Presently he looked round with a cup.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind that for me,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe've a long ride,\xe2\x80\x9d he suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know. I've crackers in my pocket.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMy boots being pulled on, I walked to the door and watched the clouds.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey seem as if they might lift,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. And I took out my watch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat time is it?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA quarter of--it's run down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhile I wound it he seemed to be consulting his own.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d I inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTen minutes past seven.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs I was setting my watch he slowly said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSteve wound his all regular. I had to night-guard him till two.\xe2\x80\x9d His\r\nspeech was like that of one in a trance: so, at least, it sounds in my\r\nmemory to-day.\r\n\r\nAgain I looked at the weather and the rainy immensity of the plain. The\r\nfoot-hills eastward where we were going were a soft yellow. Over the\r\ngray-green sage-brush moved shapeless places of light--not yet the\r\nuncovered sunlight, but spots where the storm was wearing thin; and\r\nwandering streams of warmth passed by slowly in the surrounding air.\r\nAs I watched the clouds and the earth, my eyes chanced to fall on the\r\ndistant clump of cottonwoods. Vapors from the enfeebled storm floated\r\nround them, and they were indeed far away; but I came inside and began\r\nrolling up my blankets.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will not change your mind?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian by the fire. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is\r\nthirty-five miles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI shook my head, feeling a certain shame that he should see how unnerved\r\nI was.\r\n\r\nHe swallowed a hot cupful, and after it sat thinking; and presently he\r\npassed his hand across his brow, shutting his eyes. Again he poured\r\nout a cup, and emptying this, rose abruptly to his feet as if shaking\r\nhimself free from something.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet's pack and quit here,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nOur horses were in the corral and our belongings in the shelter of what\r\nhad been once the cabin at this forlorn place. He collected them in\r\nsilence while I saddled my own animal, and in silence we packed the two\r\npackhorses, and threw the diamond hitch, and hauled tight the slack,\r\ndamp ropes. Soon we had mounted, and as we turned into the trail I gave\r\na look back at my last night's lodging.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian noticed me. \xe2\x80\x9cGood-by forever!\xe2\x80\x9d he interpreted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy God, I hope so!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSame here,\xe2\x80\x9d he confessed. And these were our first natural words this\r\nmorning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis will go well,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, holding my flask out to him; and both of us\r\ntook some, and felt easier for it and the natural words.\r\n\r\nFor an hour we had been shirking real talk, holding fast to the weather,\r\nor anything, and all the while that silent thing we were keeping\r\noff spoke plainly in the air around us and in every syllable that we\r\nuttered. But now we were going to get away from it; leave it behind in\r\nthe stable, and set ourselves free from it by talking it out. Already\r\nrelief had begun to stir in my spirits.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou never did this before,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I never had it to do.\xe2\x80\x9d He was riding beside me, looking down at his\r\nsaddle-horn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not think I should ever be able,\xe2\x80\x9d I pursued.\r\n\r\nDefiance sounded in his answer. \xe2\x80\x9cI would do it again this morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don't mean that. It's all right here. There's no other way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would do it all over again the same this morning. Just the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, so should I--if I could do it at all.\xe2\x80\x9d I still thought he was\r\njustifying their justice to me.\r\n\r\nHe made no answer as he rode along, looking all the while at his saddle.\r\nBut again he passed his hand over his forehead with that frown and\r\nshutting of the eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to be sure I should behave myself if I were condemned,\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nsaid next. For it now came to me--which should I resemble? Could I read\r\nthe newspaper, and be interested in county elections, and discuss coming\r\ndeath as if I had lost a game of cards? Or would they have to drag me\r\nout? That poor wretch in the gray flannel shirt--\xe2\x80\x9cIt was bad in the\r\nstable,\xe2\x80\x9d I said aloud. For an after-shiver of it went through me.\r\n\r\nA third time his hand brushed his forehead, and I ventured some\r\nsympathy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm afraid your head aches.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't want to keep seeing Steve,\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSteve!\xe2\x80\x9d I was astounded. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy he--why all I saw of him was splendid.\r\nSince it had to be. It was--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes; Ed. You're thinking about him. I'd forgot him. So you didn't\r\nenjoy Ed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this I looked at him blankly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt isn't possible that--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain he cut me short with a laugh almost savage. \xe2\x80\x9cYou needn't to worry\r\nabout Steve. He stayed game.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhat then had been the matter that he should keep seeing Steve--that his\r\nvision should so obliterate from him what I still shivered at, and so\r\nshake him now? For he seemed to be growing more stirred as I grew less.\r\nI asked him no further questions, however, and we went on for several\r\nminutes, he brooding always in the same fashion, until he resumed with\r\nthe hard indifference that had before surprised me:-- \xe2\x80\x9cSo Ed gave you\r\nfeelings! Dumb ague and so forth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo doubt we're not made the same way,\xe2\x80\x9d I retorted.\r\n\r\nHe took no notice of this. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you'd have been more comfortable if he'd\r\nacted same as Steve did. It cert'nly was bad seeing Ed take it that way,\r\nI reckon. And you didn't see him when the time came for business.\r\nWell, here's what it is: a man may be such a confirmed miscreant that\r\nkilling's the only cure for him; but still he's your own species, and\r\nyou don't want to have him fall around and grab your laigs and show you\r\nhis fear naked. It makes you feel ashamed. So Ed gave you feelings, and\r\nSteve made everything right easy for you!\xe2\x80\x9d There was irony in his voice\r\nas he surveyed me, but it fell away at once into sadness. \xe2\x80\x9cBoth was\r\nmiscreants. But if Steve had played the coward, too, it would have been\r\na whole heap easier for me.\xe2\x80\x9d He paused before adding, \xe2\x80\x9cAnd Steve was not\r\na miscreant once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis voice had trembled, and I felt the deep emotion that seemed to gain\r\nupon him now that action was over and he had nothing to do but think.\r\nAnd his view was simple enough: you must die brave. Failure is a sort\r\nof treason to the brotherhood, and forfeits pity. It was Steve's perfect\r\nbearing that had caught his heart so that he forgot even his scorn of\r\nthe other man.\r\n\r\nBut this was by no means all that was to come. He harked back to that\r\nnotion of a prisoner helping to make it easy for his executioner.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEasy plumb to the end,\xe2\x80\x9d he pursued, his mind reviewing the acts of the\r\nmorning. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, he tried to give me your newspaper. I didn't--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no,\xe2\x80\x9d I said hastily. \xe2\x80\x9cI had finished with it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, he took dying as naturally as he took living. Like a man should.\r\nLike I hope to.\xe2\x80\x9d Again he looked at the pictures in his mind. \xe2\x80\x9cNo\r\nplay-acting nor last words. He just told good-by to the boys as we led\r\nhis horse under the limb--you needn't to look so dainty,\xe2\x80\x9d he broke off.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ain't going to get any more shocking particulars.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know I'm white-livered,\xe2\x80\x9d I said with a species of laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cI never\r\ncrowd and stare when somebody is hurt in the street. I get away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe thought this over. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don't mean all of that. You'd not have spoke\r\njust that way about crowding and staring if you thought well of them\r\nthat stare. Staring ain't courage; it's trashy curiosity. Now you did\r\nnot have this thing--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe had stretched out his hand to point, but it fell, and his utterance\r\nstopped, and he jerked his horse to a stand. My nerves sprang like a\r\nwire at his suddenness, and I looked where he was looking. There were\r\nthe cottonwoods, close in front of us. As we had travelled and talked\r\nwe had forgotten them. Now they were looming within a hundred yards; and\r\nour trail lay straight through them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet's go around them,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian.\r\n\r\nWhen we had come back from our circuit into the trail he continued:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou did not have that thing to do. But a man goes through with his\r\nresponsibilities--and I reckon you could.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope so,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered. \xe2\x80\x9cHow about Ed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was not a man, though we thought he was till this. Steve and I\r\nstarted punching cattle together at the Bordeaux outfit, north of\r\nCheyenne. We did everything together in those days--work and play. Six\r\nyears ago. Steve had many good points onced.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe must have gone two miles before he spoke again. \xe2\x80\x9cYou prob'ly didn't\r\nnotice Steve? I mean the way he acted to me?\xe2\x80\x9d It was a question, but he\r\ndid not wait for my answer. \xe2\x80\x9cSteve never said a word to me all through.\r\nHe shunned it. And you saw how neighborly he talked to the other boys.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere have they all gone?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\nHe smiled at me. \xe2\x80\x9cIt cert'nly is lonesome now, for a fact.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn't know you felt it,\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFeel it!--they've went to the railroad. Three of them are witnesses\r\nin a case at Evanston, and the Judge wants our outfit at Medicine Bow.\r\nSteve shunned me. Did he think I was going back on him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat if he did? You were not. And so nobody's going to Wind River but\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Did you notice Steve would not give us any information about\r\nShorty? That was right. I would have acted that way, too.\xe2\x80\x9d Thus, each\r\ntime, he brought me back to the subject.\r\n\r\nThe sun was now shining warm during two or three minutes together, and\r\ngulfs of blue opened in the great white clouds. These moved and met\r\namong each other, and parted, like hands spread out, slowly weaving\r\na spell of sleep over the day after the wakeful night storm. The\r\nhuge contours of the earth lay basking and drying, and not one living\r\ncreature, bird or beast, was in sight. Quiet was returning to my revived\r\nspirits, but there was none for the Virginian. And as he reasoned\r\nmatters out aloud, his mood grew more overcast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have a friend, and his ways are your ways. You travel together,\r\nyou spree together confidentially, and you suit each other down to the\r\nground. Then one day you find him putting his iron on another man's\r\ncalf. You tell him fair and square those ways have never been your ways\r\nand ain't going to be your ways. Well, that does not change him any, for\r\nit seems he's disturbed over getting rich quick and being a big man\r\nin the Territory. And the years go on, until you are foreman of Judge\r\nHenry's ranch and he--is dangling back in the cottonwoods. What can he\r\nclaim? Who made the choice? He cannot say, 'Here is my old friend that I\r\nwould have stood by.' Can he say that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut he didn't say it,\xe2\x80\x9d I protested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. He shunned me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cSuppose while you were on guard he had whispered,\r\n'Get me off'--would you have done it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, hotly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen what do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you want?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe could not answer me--but I had not answered him, I saw; so I pushed\r\nit farther. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you want indorsement from the man you were hanging?\r\nThat's asking a little too much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut he had now another confusion. \xe2\x80\x9cSteve stood by Shorty,\xe2\x80\x9d he said\r\nmusingly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was Shorty's mistake cost him his life, but all the same\r\nhe didn't want us to catch--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are mixing things,\xe2\x80\x9d I interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cI never heard you mix things\r\nbefore. And it was not Shorty's mistake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe showed momentary interest. \xe2\x80\x9cWhose then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe mistake of whoever took a fool into their enterprise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's correct. Well, Trampas took Shorty in, and Steve would not tell\r\non him either.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI still tried it, saying, \xe2\x80\x9cThey were all in the same boat.\xe2\x80\x9d But logic\r\nwas useless; he had lost his bearings in a fog of sentiment. He knew,\r\nknew passionately, that he had done right; but the silence of his old\r\nfriend to him through those last hours left a sting that no reasoning\r\ncould assuage. \xe2\x80\x9cHe told good-by to the rest of the boys; but not to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n And nothing that I could point out in common sense turned him from\r\nthe thread of his own argument. He worked round the circle again to\r\nself-justification. \xe2\x80\x9cWas it him I was deserting? Was not the deserting\r\ndone by him the day I spoke my mind about stealing calves? I have kept\r\nmy ways the same. He is the one that took to new ones. The man I used to\r\ntravel with is not the man back there. Same name, to be sure. And same\r\nbody. But different in--and yet he had the memory! You can't never\r\nchange your memory!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe gave a sob. It was the first I had ever heard from him, and before\r\nI knew what I was doing I had reined my horse up to his and put my arm\r\naround his shoulders. I had no sooner touched him than he was utterly\r\novercome. \xe2\x80\x9cI knew Steve awful well,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nThus we had actually come to change places; for early in the morning he\r\nhad been firm while I was unnerved, while now it was I who attempted to\r\nsteady and comfort him.\r\n\r\nI had the sense to keep silent, and presently he shook my hand, not\r\nlooking at me as he did so. He was always very shy of demonstration.\r\nAnd he took to patting the neck of his pony. \xe2\x80\x9cYou Monte hawss,\xe2\x80\x9d said he,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cyou think you are wise, but there's a lot of things you don't savvy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Then he made a new beginning of talk between us.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is kind of pitiful about Shorty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery pitiful,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know about him?\xe2\x80\x9d the Virginian asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know there's no real harm in him, and some real good, and that he has\r\nnot got the brains necessary to be a horse thief.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's so. That's very true. Trampas has led him in deeper than his\r\nstature can stand. Now back East you can be middling and get along. But\r\nif you go to try a thing on in this Western country, you've got to do it\r\nWELL. You've got to deal cyards WELL; you've got to steal WELL; and if\r\nyou claim to be quick with your gun, you must be quick, for you're a\r\npublic temptation, and some man will not resist trying to prove he is\r\nthe quicker. You must break all the Commandments WELL in this Western\r\ncountry, and Shorty should have stayed in Brooklyn, for he will be a\r\nnovice his livelong days. You don't know about him? He has told me his\r\ncircumstances. He don't remember his father, and it was like he could\r\nhave claimed three or four. And I expect his mother was not much\r\ninterested in him before or after he was born. He ran around, and when\r\nhe was eighteen he got to be help to a grocery man. But a girl he ran\r\nwith kept taking all his pay and teasing him for more, and so one day\r\nthe grocery man caught Shorty robbing his till, and fired him. There\r\nwasn't no one to tell good-by to, for the girl had to go to the country\r\nto see her aunt, she said. So Shorty hung around the store and kissed\r\nthe grocery cat good-by. He'd been used to feeding the cat, and she'd\r\nsit in his lap and purr, he told me. He sends money back to that girl\r\nnow. This hyeh country is no country for Shorty, for he will be a\r\nconspicuous novice all his days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps he'll prefer honesty after his narrow shave,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\nBut the Virginian shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cTrampas has got hold of him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe day was now all blue above, and all warm and dry beneath. We had\r\nbegun to wind in and rise among the first slopes of the foot-hills, and\r\nwe had talked ourselves into silence. At the first running water we made\r\na long nooning, and I slept on the bare ground. My body was lodged so\r\nfast and deep in slumber that when the Virginian shook me awake I could\r\nnot come back to life at once; it was the clump of cottonwoods, small\r\nand far out in the plain below us, that recalled me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt'll not be watching us much longer,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. He made it\r\na sort of joke; but I knew that both of us were glad when presently we\r\nrode into a steeper country, and among its folds and carvings lost all\r\nsight of the plain. He had not slept, I found. His explanation was that\r\nthe packs needed better balancing, and after that he had gone up and\r\ndown the stream on the chance of trout. But his haunted eyes gave me the\r\nreal reason--they spoke of Steve, no matter what he spoke of; it was to\r\nbe no short thing with him.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXXII. SUPERSTITION TRAIL\r\n\r\n\r\nWe did not make thirty-five miles that day, nor yet twenty-five, for\r\nhe had let me sleep. We made an early camp and tried some unsuccessful\r\nfishing, over which he was cheerful, promising trout to-morrow when we\r\nshould be higher among the mountains. He never again touched or came\r\nnear the subject that was on his mind, but while I sat writing my diary,\r\nhe went off to his horse Monte, and I could hear that he occasionally\r\ntalked to that friend.\r\n\r\nNext day we swung southward from what is known to many as the Conant\r\ntrail, and headed for that short cut through the Tetons which is known\r\nto but a few. Bitch Creek was the name of the stream we now followed,\r\nand here there was such good fishing that we idled; and the horses and\r\nI at least enjoyed ourselves. For they found fresh pastures and shade in\r\nthe now plentiful woods; and the mountain odors and the mountain heights\r\nwere enough for me when the fish refused to rise. This road of ours now\r\nbecame the road which the pursuit had taken before the capture. Going\r\nalong, I noticed the footprints of many hoofs, rain-blurred but recent,\r\nand these were the tracks of the people I had met in the stable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can notice Monte's,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is the only one that\r\nhas his hind feet shod. There's several trails from this point down to\r\nwhere we have come from.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe mounted now over a long slant of rock, smooth and of wide extent.\r\nAbove us it went up easily into a little side canyon, but ahead, where\r\nour way was, it grew so steep that we got off and led our horses.\r\nThis brought us to the next higher level of the mountain, a space of\r\nsagebrush more open, where the rain-washed tracks appeared again in the\r\nsofter ground.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome one has been here since the rain,\xe2\x80\x9d I called to the Virginian, who\r\nwas still on the rock, walking up behind the packhorses.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince the rain!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cThat's not two days yet.\xe2\x80\x9d He came and\r\nexamined the footprints. \xe2\x80\x9cA man and a hawss,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, frowning. \xe2\x80\x9cGoing\r\nthe same way we are. How did he come to pass us, and us not see him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne of the other trails,\xe2\x80\x9d I reminded him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but there's not many that knows them. They are pretty rough\r\ntrails.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWorse than this one we're taking?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot much; only how does he come to know any of them? And why don't he\r\ntake the Conant trail that's open and easy and not much longer? One man\r\nand a hawss. I don't see who he is or what he wants here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cProbably a prospector,\xe2\x80\x9d I suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly one outfit of prospectors has ever been here, and they claimed\r\nthere was no mineral-bearing rock in these parts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe got back into our saddles with the mystery unsolved. To the Virginian\r\nit was a greater one, apparently, than to me; why should one have to\r\naccount for every stray traveller in the mountains?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's queer, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. He was now riding in front of\r\nme, and he stopped, looking down at the trail. \xe2\x80\x9cDon't you notice?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt did not strike me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, he keeps walking beside his hawss; he don't get on him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow we, of course, had mounted at the beginning of the better trail\r\nafter the steep rock, and that was quite half a mile back. Still, I had\r\na natural explanation. \xe2\x80\x9cHe's leading a packhorse. He's a poor trapper,\r\nand walks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPackhorses ain't usually shod before and behind,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian;\r\nand sliding to the ground he touched the footprints. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are not four\r\nhours old,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cThis bank's in shadow by one o'clock, and the sun\r\nhas not cooked them dusty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe continued on our way; and although it seemed no very particular\r\nthing to me that a man should choose to walk and lead his horse for a\r\nwhile,--I often did so to limber my muscles,--nevertheless I began to\r\ncatch the Virginian's uncertain feeling about this traveller whose steps\r\nhad appeared on our path in mid-journey, as if he had alighted from the\r\nmid-air, and to remind myself that he had come over the great face of\r\nrock from another trail and thus joined us, and that indigent trappers\r\nare to be found owning but a single horse and leading him with their\r\nbelongings through the deepest solitudes of the mountains--none of this\r\nquite brought back to me the comfort which had been mine since we left\r\nthe cottonwoods out of sight down in the plain. Hence I called out\r\nsharply, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat's the matter now?\xe2\x80\x9d when the Virginian suddenly stopped\r\nhis horse again.\r\n\r\nHe looked down at the trail, and then he very slowly turned round in his\r\nsaddle and stared back steadily at me. \xe2\x80\x9cThere's two of them,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must know whether it's two horses or two men,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, almost\r\nangrily.\r\n\r\nBut to this he made no answer, sitting quite still on his horse and\r\ncontemplating the ground. The silence was fastening on me like a spell,\r\nand I spurred my horse impatiently forward to see for myself. The\r\nfootprints of two men were there in the trail.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you say to that?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cKind of ridiculous,\r\nain't it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery quaint,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered, groping for the explanation. There was no\r\nrock here to walk over and step from into the softer trail. These second\r\nsteps came more out of the air than the first. And my brain played me\r\nthe evil trick of showing me a dead man in a gray flannel shirt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's two, you see, travelling with one hawss, and they take turns\r\nriding him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, of course!\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed; and we went along for a few paces.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere you are,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, as the trail proved him right.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNumber one has got on. My God, what's that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt a crashing in the woods very close to us we both flung round and\r\ncaught sight of a vanishing elk.\r\n\r\nIt left us confronted, smiling a little, and sounding each other with\r\nour eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, we didn't need him for meat,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA spike-horn, wasn't it?\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, just a spike-horn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a while now as we rode we kept up a cheerful conversation about elk.\r\nWe wondered if we should meet many more close to the trail like this;\r\nbut it was not long before our words died away. We had come into a\r\nveritable gulf of mountain peaks, sharp at their bare summits like\r\nteeth, holding fields of snow lower down, and glittering still in full\r\nday up there, while down among our pines and parks the afternoon was\r\ngrowing sombre. All the while the fresh hoofprints of the horse and the\r\nfresh footprints of the man preceded us. In the trees, and in the opens,\r\nacross the levels, and up the steeps, they were there. And so they were\r\nnot four hours old! Were they so much? Might we not, round some turn,\r\ncome upon the makers of them? I began to watch for this. And again my\r\nbrain played me an evil trick, against which I found myself actually\r\nreasoning thus: if they took turns riding, then walking must tire them\r\nas it did me or any man. And besides, there was a horse. With such\r\nthoughts I combated the fancy that those footprints were being made\r\nimmediately in front of us all the while, and that they were the only\r\nsign of any presence which our eyes could see. But my fancy overcame my\r\nthoughts. It was shame only which held me from asking this question of\r\nthe Virginian: Had one horse served in both cases of Justice down at\r\nthe cottonwoods? I wondered about this. One horse--or had the strangling\r\nnooses dragged two saddles empty at the same signal? Most likely; and\r\ntherefore these people up here--Was I going back to the nursery? I\r\nbrought myself up short. And I told myself to be steady; there lurked in\r\nthis brain-process which was going on beneath my reason a threat worse\r\nthan the childish apprehensions it created. I reminded myself that I was\r\na man grown, twenty-five years old, and that I must not merely seem like\r\none, but feel like one. \xe2\x80\x9cYou're not afraid of the dark, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d This\r\nI uttered aloud, unwittingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat's that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI started; but it was only the Virginian behind me. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, nothing. The\r\nair is getting colder up here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI had presently a great relief. We came to a place where again this\r\ntrail mounted so abruptly that we once more got off to lead our\r\nhorses. So likewise had our predecessors done; and as I watched the two\r\ndifferent sets of footprints, I observed something and hastened to speak\r\nof it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne man is much heavier than the other.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was hoping I'd not have to tell you that,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou're always ahead of me! Well, still my education is progressing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, yes. You'll equal an Injun if you keep on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was good to be facetious; and I smiled to myself as I trudged upward.\r\nWe came off the steep place, leaving the canyon beneath us, and took to\r\nhorseback. And as we proceeded over the final gentle slant up to the\r\nrim of the great basin that was set among the peaks, the Virginian was\r\njocular once more.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPounds has got on,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cand Ounces is walking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI glanced over my shoulder at him, and he nodded as he fixed the\r\nweather-beaten crimson handkerchief round his neck. Then he threw\r\na stone at a pack animal that was delaying on the trail. \xe2\x80\x9cDamn your\r\nbuckskin hide,\xe2\x80\x9d he drawled. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can view the scenery from the top.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was so natural, sitting loose in the saddle, and cursing in his\r\ngentle voice, that I laughed to think what visions I had been harboring.\r\nThe two dead men riding one horse through the mountains vanished, and I\r\ncame back to every day.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think we'll catch up with those people?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot likely. They're travelling about the same gait we are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOunces ought to be the best walker.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUp hill, yes. But Pounds will go down a-foggin'.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe gained the rim of the basin. It lay below us, a great cup of\r\ncountry,--rocks, woods, opens, and streams. The tall peaks rose like\r\nspires around it, magnificent and bare in the last of the sun; and we\r\nsurveyed this upper world, letting our animals get breath. Our bleak,\r\ncrumbled rim ran like a rampart between the towering tops, a half circle\r\nof five miles or six, very wide in some parts, and in some shrinking to\r\na scanty foothold, as here. Here our trail crossed over it between two\r\neroded and fantastic shapes of stone, like mushrooms, or misshapen heads\r\non pikes. Banks of snow spread up here against the black rocks, but\r\nhalf an hour would see us descended to the green and the woods. I looked\r\ndown, both of us looked down, but our forerunners were not there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey'll be camping somewhere in this basin, though,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nVirginian, staring at the dark pines. \xe2\x80\x9cThey have not come this trail by\r\naccident.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA cold little wind blew down between our stone shapes, and upward again,\r\neddying. And round a corner upward with it came fluttering a leaf of\r\nnewspaper, and caught against an edge close to me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat's the latest?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired the Virginian from his horse. For I had\r\ndismounted, and had picked up the leaf.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeems to be interesting,\xe2\x80\x9d I next heard him say. \xe2\x80\x9cCan't you tell a man\r\nwhat's making your eyes bug out so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d my voice replied to him, and it sounded like some stranger\r\nspeaking lightly near by; \xe2\x80\x9coh, yes! Decidedly interesting.\xe2\x80\x9d My voice\r\nmimicked his pronunciation. \xe2\x80\x9cIt's quite the latest, I imagine. You had\r\nbetter read it yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d And I handed it to him with a smile, watching\r\nhis countenance, while my brain felt as if clouds were rushing through\r\nit.\r\n\r\nI saw his eyes quietly run the headings over. \xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired,\r\nafter scanning it on both sides. \xe2\x80\x9cI don't seem to catch the excitement.\r\nFremont County is going to hold elections. I see they claim Jake--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's mine,\xe2\x80\x9d I cut him off. \xe2\x80\x9cMy own paper. Those are my pencil marks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI do not think that a microscope could have discerned a change in\r\nhis face. \xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d he commented, holding the paper, and fixing it with a\r\ncritical eye. \xe2\x80\x9cYou mean this is the one you lent Steve, and he wanted\r\nto give me to give back to you. And so them are your own marks.\xe2\x80\x9d For a\r\nmoment more he held it judicially, as I have seen men hold a contract\r\nupon whose terms they were finally passing. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, you have got it back\r\nnow, anyway.\xe2\x80\x9d And he handed it to me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly a piece of it!\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed, always lightly. And as I took it from\r\nhim his hand chanced to touch mine. It was cold as ice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey ain't through readin' the rest,\xe2\x80\x9d he explained easily. \xe2\x80\x9cDon't you\r\nthrow it away! After they've taken such trouble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's true,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if it's Pounds or Ounces I'm\r\nindebted to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThus we made further merriment as we rode down into the great basin.\r\nBefore us, the horse and boot tracks showed plain in the soft slough\r\nwhere melted snow ran half the day.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it's a paper chase,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, \xe2\x80\x9cthey'll drop no more along\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnless it gets dark,\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe'll camp before that. Maybe we'll see their fire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe did not see their fire. We descended in the chill silence, while the\r\nmushroom rocks grew far and the sombre woods approached. By a stream we\r\ngot off where two banks sheltered us; for a bleak wind cut down over the\r\ncrags now and then, making the pines send out a great note through the\r\nbasin, like breakers in a heavy sea. But we made cosey in the tent.\r\nWe pitched the tent this night, and I was glad to have it shut out the\r\nmountain peaks. They showed above the banks where we camped; and in the\r\nstarlight their black shapes rose stark against the sky. They, with the\r\npines and the wind, were a bedroom too unearthly this night. And as soon\r\nas our supper dishes were washed we went inside to our lantern and our\r\ngame of cribbage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is snug,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, as we played. \xe2\x80\x9cThat wind don't get\r\ndown here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSmoking is snug, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said I. And we marked our points for an hour,\r\nwith no words save about the cards.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll be pretty near glad when we get out of these mountains,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nVirginian. \xe2\x80\x9cThey're most too big.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe pines had altogether ceased; but their silence was as tremendous as\r\ntheir roar had been.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't know, though,\xe2\x80\x9d he resumed. \xe2\x80\x9cThere's times when the plains can\r\nbe awful big, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPresently we finished a hand, and he said, \xe2\x80\x9cLet me see that paper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe sat reading it apparently through, while I arranged my blankets to\r\nmake a warm bed. Then, since the paper continued to absorb him, I got\r\nmyself ready, and slid between my blankets for the night. \xe2\x80\x9cYou'll need\r\nanother candle soon in that lantern,\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\r\nHe put the paper down. \xe2\x80\x9cI would do it all over again,\xe2\x80\x9d he began. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nwhole thing just the same. He knowed the customs of the country, and he\r\nplayed the game. No call to blame me for the customs of the country. You\r\nleave other folks' cattle alone, or you take the consequences, and it\r\nwas all known to Steve from the start. Would he have me take the Judge's\r\nwages and give him the wink? He must have changed a heap from the Steve\r\nI knew if he expected that. I don't believe he expected that. He knew\r\nwell enough the only thing that would have let him off would have been\r\na regular jury. For the thieves have got hold of the juries in Johnson\r\nCounty. I would do it all over, just the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe expiring flame leaped in the lantern, and fell blue. He broke off\r\nin his words as if to arrange the light, but did not, sitting silent\r\ninstead, just visible, and seeming to watch the death struggle of the\r\nflame. I could find nothing to say to him, and I believed he was now\r\nwinning his way back to serenity by himself. He kept his outward man\r\nso nearly natural that I forgot about that cold touch of his hand, and\r\nnever guessed how far out from reason the tide of emotion was even now\r\nwhirling him. \xe2\x80\x9cI remember at Cheyenne onced,\xe2\x80\x9d he resumed. And he told\r\nme of a Thanksgiving visit to town that he had made with Steve. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\r\nwas just colts then,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. He dwelt on their coltish doings, their\r\nadventures sought and wrought in the perfect fellowship of youth. \xe2\x80\x9cFor\r\nSteve and me most always hunted in couples back in them gamesome years,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n he explained. And he fell into the elemental talk of sex, such talk\r\nas would be an elk's or tiger's; and spoken so by him, simply and\r\nnaturally, as we speak of the seasons, or of death, or of any actuality,\r\nit was without offense. It would be offense should I repeat it. Then,\r\nabruptly ending these memories of himself and Steve, he went out of the\r\ntent, and I heard him dragging a log to the fire. When it had blazed up,\r\nthere on the tent wall was his shadow and that of the log where he sat\r\nwith his half-broken heart. And all the while I supposed he was master\r\nof himself, and self-justified against Steve's omission to bid him\r\ngood-by.\r\n\r\nI must have fallen asleep before he returned, for I remember nothing\r\nexcept waking and finding him in his blankets beside me. The fire\r\nshadow was gone, and gray, cold light was dimly on the tent. He slept\r\nrestlessly, and his forehead was ploughed by lines of pain. While I\r\nlooked at him he began to mutter, and suddenly started up with violence.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried out; \xe2\x80\x9cno! Just the same!\xe2\x80\x9d and thus wakened himself,\r\nstaring. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat's the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d he demanded. He was slow in getting back\r\nto where we were; and full consciousness found him sitting up with his\r\neyes fixed on mine. They were more haunted than they had been at all,\r\nand his next speech came straight from his dream. \xe2\x80\x9cMaybe you'd better\r\nquit me. This ain't your trouble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, what is the trouble?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis eyes still intently fixed on mine. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you think if we changed our\r\ntrail we could lose them from us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was framing a jocose reply about Ounces being a good walker, when the\r\nsound of hoofs rushing in the distance stopped me, and he ran out of the\r\ntent with his rifle. When I followed with mine he was up the bank, and\r\nall his powers alert. But nothing came out of the dimness save our three\r\nstampeded horses. They crashed over fallen timber and across the open to\r\nwhere their picketed comrade grazed at the end of his rope. By him they\r\ncame to a stand, and told him, I suppose, what they had seen; for all\r\nfour now faced in the same direction, looking away into the mysterious\r\ndawn. We likewise stood peering, and my rifle barrel felt cold in my\r\nhand. The dawn was all we saw, the inscrutable dawn, coming and coming\r\nthrough the black pines and the gray open of the basin. There above\r\nlifted the peaks, no sun yet on them, and behind us our stream made a\r\nlittle tinkling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA bear, I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, at length.\r\n\r\nHis strange look fixed me again, and then his eyes went to the horses.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey smell things we can't smell,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, very slowly. \xe2\x80\x9cWill you\r\nprove to me they don't see things we can't see?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA chill shot through me, and I could not help a frightened glance where\r\nwe had been watching. But one of the horses began to graze and I had\r\na wholesome thought. \xe2\x80\x9cHe's tired of whatever he sees, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said I,\r\npointing.\r\n\r\nA smile came for a moment in the Virginian's face. \xe2\x80\x9cMust be a poor\r\nshow,\xe2\x80\x9d he observed. All the horses were grazing now, and he added, \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nain't hurt their appetites any.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe made our own breakfast then. And what uncanny dread I may have been\r\ntouched with up to this time henceforth left me in the face of a real\r\nalarm. The shock of Steve was working upon the Virginian. He was aware\r\nof it himself; he was fighting it with all his might; and he was being\r\novercome. He was indeed like a gallant swimmer against whom both wind\r\nand tide have conspired. And in this now foreboding solitude there was\r\nonly myself to throw him ropes. His strokes for safety were as bold as\r\nwas the undertow that ceaselessly annulled them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon I made a fuss in the tent?\xe2\x80\x9d said he, feeling his way with me.\r\n\r\nI threw him a rope. \xe2\x80\x9cYes. Nightmare--indigestion--too much newspaper\r\nbefore retiring.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe caught the rope. \xe2\x80\x9cThat's correct! I had a hell of a foolish dream for\r\na growed-up man. You'd not think it of me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, I should. I've had them after prolonged lobster and\r\nchampagne.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cprolonged! Prolonged is what does it.\xe2\x80\x9d He glanced\r\nbehind him. \xe2\x80\x9cSteve came back--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn your lobster dream,\xe2\x80\x9d I put in.\r\n\r\nBut he missed this rope. \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, with his eyes searching me.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd he handed me the paper--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the way, where is that?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI built the fire with it. But when I took it from him it was a\r\nsix-shooter I had hold of, and pointing at my breast. And then Steve\r\nspoke. 'Do you think you're fit to live?' Steve said; and I got hot at\r\nhim, and I reckon I must have told him what I thought of him. You heard\r\nme, I expect?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGlad I didn't. Your language sometimes is--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe laughed out. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I account for all this that's happening just like\r\nyou do. If we gave our explanations, they'd be pretty near twins.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe horses saw a bear, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe a bear. Maybe \xe2\x80\x9c--but here the tide caught him again--\xe2\x80\x9cWhat's your\r\nidea about dreams?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMy ropes were all out. \xe2\x80\x9cLiver--nerves,\xe2\x80\x9d was the best I could do.\r\n\r\nBut now he swam strongly by himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may think I'm discreditable,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I know I am. It ought\r\nto take more than--well, men have lost their friendships before. Feuds\r\nand wars have cloven a right smart of bonds in twain. And if my haid\r\nis going to get shook by a little old piece of newspaper--I'm ashamed I\r\nburned that. I'm ashamed to have been that weak.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAny man gets unstrung,\xe2\x80\x9d I told him. My ropes had become straws; and I\r\nstrove to frame some policy for the next hours.\r\n\r\nWe now finished breakfast and set forth to catch the horses. As we drove\r\nthem in I found that the Virginian was telling me a ghost story. \xe2\x80\x9cAt\r\nhalf-past three in the morning she saw her runaway daughter standing\r\nwith a babe in her arms; but when she moved it was all gone. Later they\r\nfound it was the very same hour the young mother died in Nogales. And\r\nshe sent for the child and raised it herself. I knowed them both back\r\nhome. Do you believe that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI said nothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo more do I believe it,\xe2\x80\x9d he asserted. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd see here! Nogales time\r\nis three hours different from Richmond. I didn't know about that point\r\nthen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOnce out of these mountains, I knew he could right himself; but even\r\nI, who had no Steve to dream about, felt this silence of the peaks was\r\npreying on me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHer daughter and her might have been thinkin' mighty hard about each\r\nother just then,\xe2\x80\x9d he pursued. \xe2\x80\x9cBut Steve is dead. Finished. You cert'nly\r\ndon't believe there's anything more?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish I could,\xe2\x80\x9d I told him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I'm satisfied. Heaven didn't never interest me much. But if there\r\nwas a world of dreams after you went--\xe2\x80\x9d He stopped himself and turned\r\nhis searching eyes away from mine. \xe2\x80\x9cThere's a heap o' darkness wherever\r\nyou try to step,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cand I thought I'd left off wasting thoughts\r\non the subject. You see\xe2\x80\x9d--he dexterously roped a horse, and once more\r\nhis splendid sanity was turned to gold by his imagination--\xe2\x80\x9cI expect\r\nin many growed-up men you'd call sensible there's a little boy\r\nsleepin'--the little kid they onced was--that still keeps his fear\r\nof the dark. You mentioned the dark yourself yesterday. Well, this\r\nexperience has woke up that kid in me, and blamed if I can coax the\r\nlittle cuss to go to sleep again! I keep a-telling him daylight will\r\nsure come, but he keeps a-crying and holding on to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSomewhere far in the basin there was a faint sound, and we stood still.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush!\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nBut it was like our watching the dawn; nothing more followed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have shot that bear,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked.\r\n\r\nHe did not answer, and we put the saddles on without talk. We made no\r\nhaste, but we were not over half an hour, I suppose, in getting off with\r\nthe packs. It was not a new thing to hear a shot where wild game was in\r\nplenty; yet as we rode that shot sounded already in my mind different\r\nfrom others. Perhaps I should not believe this to-day but for what I\r\nlook back to. To make camp last night we had turned off the trail, and\r\nnow followed the stream down for a while, taking next a cut through the\r\nwood. In this way we came upon the tracks of our horses where they had\r\nbeen galloping back to the camp after their fright. They had kicked up\r\nthe damp and matted pine needles very plainly all along.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing has been here but themselves, though,\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd they ain't showing signs of remembering any scare,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nVirginian.\r\n\r\nIn a little while we emerged upon an open.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere's where they was grazing,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian; and the signs were\r\nclear enough. \xe2\x80\x9cHere's where they must have got their scare,\xe2\x80\x9d he pursued.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou stay with them while I circle a little.\xe2\x80\x9d So I stayed; and certainly\r\nour animals were very calm at visiting this scene. When you bring a\r\nhorse back to where he has recently encountered a wild animal his ears\r\nand his nostrils are apt to be wide awake.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian had stopped and was beckoning to me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere's your bear,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, as I arrived. \xe2\x80\x9cTwo-legged, you see. And he\r\nhad a hawss of his own.\xe2\x80\x9d There was a stake driven down where an animal\r\nhad been picketed for the night.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLooks like Ounces,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, considering the footprints.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's Ounces. And Ounces wanted another hawss very bad, so him and\r\nPounds could travel like gentlemen should.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut Pounds doesn't seem to have been with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Pounds, he was making coffee, somewheres in yonder, when this\r\nhappened. Neither of them guessed there'd be other hawsses wandering\r\nhere in the night, or they both would have come.\xe2\x80\x9d He turned back to our\r\npack animals.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you'll not hunt for this camp to make sure?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI prefer making sure first. We might be expected at that camp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe took out his rifle from beneath his leg and set it across his saddle\r\nat half-cock. I did the same; and thus cautiously we resumed our journey\r\nin a slightly different direction. \xe2\x80\x9cThis ain't all we're going to find\r\nout,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cOunces had a good idea; but I reckon he made\r\na bad mistake later.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe had found out a good deal without any more, I thought. Ounces had\r\ngone to bring in their single horse, and coming upon three more in the\r\npasture had undertaken to catch one and failed, merely driving them\r\nwhere he feared to follow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShorty never could rope a horse alone,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian grinned. \xe2\x80\x9cShorty? Well, Shorty sounds as well as Ounces.\r\nBut that ain't the mistake I'm thinking he made.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI knew that he would not tell me, but that was just like him. For the\r\nlast twenty minutes, having something to do, he had become himself\r\nagain, had come to earth from that unsafe country of the brain where\r\nbeckoned a spectral Steve. Nothing was left but in his eyes that\r\nquestion which pain had set there; and I wondered if his friend of old,\r\nwho seemed so brave and amiable, would have dealt him that hurt at the\r\nsolemn end had he known what a poisoned wound it would be.\r\n\r\nWe came out on a ridge from which we could look down. \xe2\x80\x9cYou always want\r\nto ride on high places when there's folks around whose intentions ain't\r\nbeen declared,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. And we went along our ridge for some\r\ndistance. Then, suddenly he turned down and guided us almost at once to\r\nthe trail. \xe2\x80\x9cThat's it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cSee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe track of a horse was very fresh on the trail. But it was a galloping\r\nhorse now, and no bootprints were keeping up with it any more. No boots\r\ncould have kept up with it. The rider was making time to-day. Yesterday\r\nthat horse had been ridden up into the mountains at leisure. Who was on\r\nhim? There was never to be any certain answer to that. But who was not\r\non him? We turned back in our journey, back into the heart of that basin\r\nwith the tall peaks all rising like teeth in the cloudless sun, and the\r\nsnow-fields shining white.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was afraid of us,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cHe did not know how many of\r\nus had come up here. Three hawsses might mean a dozen more around.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe followed the backward trail in among the pines, and came after a time\r\nupon their camp. And then I understood the mistake that Shorty had made.\r\nHe had returned after his failure, and had told that other man of the\r\npresence of new horses. He should have kept this a secret; for haste had\r\nto be made at once, and two cannot get away quickly upon one horse. But\r\nit was poor Shorty's last blunder. He lay there by their extinct fire,\r\nwith his wistful, lost-dog face upward, and his thick yellow hair\r\nunparted as it had always been. The murder had been done from behind. We\r\nclosed the eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere was no natural harm in him,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you must do\r\na thing well in this country.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was not a trace, not a clew, of the other man; and we found a\r\nplace where we could soon cover Shorty with earth. As we lifted him we\r\nsaw the newspaper that he had been reading. He had brought it from the\r\nclump of cottonwoods where he and the other man had made a later visit\r\nthan ours to be sure of the fate of their friends--or possibly in hopes\r\nof another horse. Evidently, when the party were surprised, they had\r\nbeen able to escape with only one. All of the newspaper was there save\r\nthe leaf I had picked up--all and more, for this had pencil writing on\r\nit that was not mine, nor did I at first take it in. I thought it might\r\nbe a clew, and I read it aloud. \xe2\x80\x9cGood-by, Jeff,\xe2\x80\x9d it said. \xe2\x80\x9cI could not\r\nhave spoke to you without playing the baby.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho's Jeff?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked. But it came over me when I looked at the\r\nVirginian. He was standing beside me quite motionless; and then he put\r\nout his hand and took the paper, and stood still, looking at the words.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSteve used to call me Jeff,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause I was Southern. I reckon\r\nnobody else ever did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe slowly folded the message from the dead, brought by the dead, and\r\nrolled it in the coat behind his saddle. For a half-minute he stood\r\nleaning his forehead down against the saddle. After this he came back\r\nand contemplated Shorty's face awhile. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish I could thank him,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish I could.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe carried Shorty over and covered him with earth, and on that laid a\r\nfew pine branches; then we took up our journey, and by the end of the\r\nforenoon we had gone some distance upon our trail through the Teton\r\nMountains. But in front of us the hoofprints ever held their stride\r\nof haste, drawing farther from us through the hours, until by the next\r\nafternoon somewhere we noticed they were no longer to be seen; and after\r\nthat they never came upon the trail again.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXXIII. THE SPINSTER LOSES SOME SLEEP\r\n\r\n\r\nSomewhere at the eastern base of the Tetons did those hoofprints\r\ndisappear into a mountain sanctuary where many crooked paths have led.\r\nHe that took another man's possessions, or he that took another man's\r\nlife, could always run here if the law or popular justice were too hot\r\nat his heels. Steep ranges and forests walled him in from the world on\r\nall four sides, almost without a break; and every entrance lay through\r\nintricate solitudes. Snake River came into the place through canyons\r\nand mournful pines and marshes, to the north, and went out at the south\r\nbetween formidable chasms. Every tributary to this stream rose among\r\nhigh peaks and ridges, and descended into the valley by well-nigh\r\nimpenetrable courses: Pacific Creek from Two Ocean Pass, Buffalo Fork\r\nfrom no pass at all, Black Rock from the To-wo-ge-tee Pass--all these,\r\nand many more, were the waters of loneliness, among whose thousand\r\nhiding-places it was easy to be lost. Down in the bottom was a spread of\r\nlevel land, broad and beautiful, with the blue and silver Tetons rising\r\nfrom its chain of lakes to the west, and other heights presiding over\r\nits other sides. And up and down and in and out of this hollow square of\r\nmountains, where waters plentifully flowed, and game and natural pasture\r\nabounded, there skulked a nomadic and distrustful population. This in\r\ndue time built cabins, took wives, begot children, and came to speak of\r\nitself as \xe2\x80\x9cThe honest settlers of Jackson's Hole.\xe2\x80\x9d It is a commodious\r\ntitle, and doubtless to-day more accurate than it was once.\r\n\r\nInto this place the hoofprints disappeared. Not many cabins were yet\r\nbuilt there; but the unknown rider of the horse knew well that he would\r\nfind shelter and welcome among the felons of his stripe. Law and order\r\nmight guess his name correctly, but there was no next step, for lack of\r\nevidence; and he would wait, whoever he was, until the rage of popular\r\njustice, which had been pursuing him and his brother thieves, should\r\nsubside. Then, feeling his way gradually with prudence, he would let\r\nhimself be seen again.\r\n\r\nAnd now, as mysteriously as he had melted away, rumor passed over the\r\ncountry. No tongue seemed to be heard telling the first news; the news\r\nwas there, one day, a matter of whispered knowledge. On Sunk Creek and\r\non Bear Creek, and elsewhere far and wide, before men talked men seemed\r\nsecretly to know that Steve, and Ed, and Shorty, would never again be\r\nseen. Riders met each other in the road and drew rein to discuss the\r\nevent, and its bearing upon the cattle interests. In town saloons men\r\ntook each other aside, and muttered over it in corners.\r\n\r\nThus it reached the ears of Molly Wood, beginning in a veiled and\r\nharmless shape.\r\n\r\nA neighbor joined her when she was out riding by herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood morning,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cDon't you find it lonesome?\xe2\x80\x9d And when she\r\nanswered lightly, he continued, meaning well: \xe2\x80\x9cYou'll be having company\r\nagain soon now. He has finished his job. Wish he'd finished it MORE!\r\nWell, good day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMolly thought these words over. She could not tell why they gave her\r\na strange feeling. To her Vermont mind no suspicion of the truth would\r\ncome naturally. But suspicion began to come when she returned from her\r\nride. For, entering the cabin of the Taylors', she came upon several\r\npeople who all dropped their talk short, and were not skilful at\r\nresuming it. She sat there awhile, uneasily aware that all of them\r\nknew something which she did not know, and was not intended to know. A\r\nthought pierced her--had anything happened to her lover? No; that was\r\nnot it. The man she had met on horseback spoke of her having company\r\nsoon again. How soon? she wondered. He had been unable to say when\r\nhe should return, and now she suddenly felt that a great silence had\r\nenveloped him lately: not the mere silence of absence, of receiving\r\nno messages or letters, but another sort of silence which now, at this\r\nmoment, was weighing strangely upon her.\r\n\r\nAnd then the next day it came out at the schoolhouse. During that\r\ninterval known as recess, she became aware through the open window that\r\nthey were playing a new game outside. Lusty screeches of delight reached\r\nher ears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJump!\xe2\x80\x9d a voice ordered. \xe2\x80\x9cJump!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't want to,\xe2\x80\x9d returned another voice, uneasily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou said you would,\xe2\x80\x9d said several. \xe2\x80\x9cDidn't he say he would? Ah, he said\r\nhe would. Jump now, quick!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I don't want to,\xe2\x80\x9d quavered the voice in a tone so dismal that Molly\r\nwent out to see.\r\n\r\nThey had got Bob Carmody on the top of the gate by a tree, with a rope\r\nround his neck, the other end of which four little boys were joyously\r\nholding. The rest looked on eagerly, three little girls clasping their\r\nhands, and springing up and down with excitement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, children!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Molly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe's said his prayers and everything,\xe2\x80\x9d they all screamed out. \xe2\x80\x9cHe's a\r\nrustler, and we're lynchin' him. Jump, Bob!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't want--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, coward, won't take his medicine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet him go, boys,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou might really hurt him.\xe2\x80\x9d And so she\r\nbroke up this game, but not without general protest from Wyoming's young\r\nvoice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe said he would,\xe2\x80\x9d Henry Dow assured her.\r\n\r\nAnd George Taylor further explained: \xe2\x80\x9cHe said he'd be Steve. But Steve\r\ndidn't scare.\xe2\x80\x9d Then George proceeded to tell the schoolmarm, eagerly,\r\nall about Steve and Ed, while the schoolmarm looked at him with a rigid\r\nface.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou promised your mother you'd not tell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Henry Dow, after all\r\nhad been told. \xe2\x80\x9cYou've gone and done it,\xe2\x80\x9d and Henry wagged his head in a\r\nsuperior manner.\r\n\r\nThus did the New England girl learn what her cow-boy lover had done. She\r\nspoke of it to nobody; she kept her misery to herself. He was not there\r\nto defend his act. Perhaps in a way that was better. But these were\r\nhours of darkness indeed to Molly Wood.\r\n\r\nOn that visit to Dunbarton, when at the first sight of her lover's\r\nphotograph in frontier dress her aunt had exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose there\r\nare days when he does not kill people,\xe2\x80\x9d she had cried in all good faith\r\nand mirth, \xe2\x80\x9cHe never killed anybody!\xe2\x80\x9d Later, when he was lying in her\r\ncabin weak from his bullet wound, but each day stronger beneath her\r\nnursing, at a certain word of his there had gone through her a shudder\r\nof doubt. Perhaps in his many wanderings he had done such a thing in\r\nself-defence, or in the cause of popular justice. But she had pushed the\r\nidea away from her hastily, back into the days before she had ever seen\r\nhim. If this had ever happened, let her not know of it. Then, as a cruel\r\nreward for his candor and his laying himself bare to her mother, the\r\nletters from Bennington had used that very letter of his as a weapon\r\nagainst him. Her sister Sarah had quoted from it. \xe2\x80\x9cHe says with apparent\r\npride,\xe2\x80\x9d wrote Sarah, \xe2\x80\x9cthat he has never killed for pleasure or profit.'\r\nThose are his exact words, and you may guess their dreadful effect upon\r\nmother. I congratulate you, my dear, on having chosen a protector so\r\nscrupulous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThus her elder sister had seen fit to write; and letters from less near\r\nrelatives made hints at the same subject. So she was compelled to accept\r\nthis piece of knowledge thrust upon her. Yet still, still, those events\r\nhad been before she knew him. They were remote, without detail or\r\ncontext. He had been little more than a boy. No doubt it was to save his\r\nown life. And so she bore the hurt of her discovery all the more easily\r\nbecause her sister's tone roused her to defend her cow-boy.\r\n\r\nBut now!\r\n\r\nIn her cabin, alone, after midnight, she arose from her sleepless bed,\r\nand lighting the candle, stood before his photograph.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a good face,\xe2\x80\x9d her great-aunt had said, after some study of it.\r\nAnd these words were in her mind now. There his likeness stood at full\r\nlength, confronting her: the spurs on the boots, the fringed leathern\r\nchaparreros, the coiled rope in hand, the pistol at hip, the rough\r\nflannel shirt, and the scarf knotted at the throat--and then the grave\r\neyes, looking at her. It thrilled her to meet them, even so. She could\r\nread life into them. She seemed to feel passion come from them, and then\r\nsomething like reproach. She stood for a long while looking at him, and\r\nthen, beating her hands together suddenly, she blew out her light and\r\nwent back into bed, but not to sleep.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou're looking pale, deary,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Taylor to her, a few days later.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAm I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you don't eat anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, I do.\xe2\x80\x9d And Molly retired to her cabin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeorge,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Taylor, \xe2\x80\x9cyou come here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt may seem severe--I think that it was severe. That evening when\r\nMr. Taylor came home to his family, George received a thrashing for\r\ndisobedience.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Taylor to her husband, \xe2\x80\x9cthat she came out\r\njust in time to stop 'em breaking Bob Carmody's neck for him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUpon the day following Mrs. Taylor essayed the impossible. She took\r\nherself over to Molly Wood's cabin. The girl gave her a listless\r\ngreeting, and the dame sat slowly down, and surveyed the comfortable\r\nroom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA very nice home, deary,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, \xe2\x80\x9cif it was a home. But you'll fix\r\nsomething like this in your real home, I have no doubt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMolly made no answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat we're going to do without you I can't see,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Taylor.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I'd not have it different for worlds. He'll be coming back soon, I\r\nexpect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Taylor,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly, all at once, \xe2\x80\x9cplease don't say anything now.\r\nI can't stand it.\xe2\x80\x9d And she broke into wretched tears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, deary, he--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; not a word. Please, please--I'll go out if you do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe older woman went to the younger one, and then put her arms round\r\nher. But when the tears were over, they had not done any good; it was\r\nnot the storm that clears the sky--all storms do not clear the sky. And\r\nMrs. Taylor looked at the pale girl and saw that she could do nothing to\r\nhelp her toward peace of mind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to her husband, after returning from her\r\nprofitless errand, \xe2\x80\x9cyou might know she'd feel dreadful.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat about?\xe2\x80\x9d said Taylor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you know just as well as I do. And I'll say for myself, I hope\r\nyou'll never have to help hang folks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Taylor, mildly, \xe2\x80\x9cif I had to, I'd have to, I guess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don't want it to come. But that poor girl is eating her heart\r\nright out over it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does she say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's what she don't say. She'll not talk, and she'll not let me talk,\r\nand she sits and sits.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll go talk some to her,\xe2\x80\x9d said the man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Taylor, I thought you had more sense. You'd not get a word in.\r\nShe'll be sick soon if her worry ain't stopped someway, though.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does she want this country to do?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Taylor. \xe2\x80\x9cDoes she\r\nexpect it to be like Vermont when it--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can't help what she expects,\xe2\x80\x9d his wife interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I wish we\r\ncould help HER.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey could not, however; and help came from another source. Judge Henry\r\nrode by the next day. To him good Mrs. Taylor at once confided her\r\nanxiety. The Judge looked grave.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMust I meddle?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Judge, you must,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Taylor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why can't I send him over here when he gets back? Then they'll just\r\nsettle it between themselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Taylor shook her head. \xe2\x80\x9cThat would unsettle it worse than it is,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n she assured him. \xe2\x80\x9cThey mustn't meet just now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Judge sighed. \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cvery well. I'll sacrifice my\r\ncharacter, since you insist.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJudge Henry sat thinking, waiting until school should be out. He did not\r\nat all relish what lay before him. He would like to have got out of it.\r\nHe had been a federal judge; he had been an upright judge; he had met\r\nthe responsibilities of his difficult office not only with learning,\r\nwhich is desirable, but also with courage and common sense besides, and\r\nthese are essential. He had been a stanch servant of the law. And now\r\nhe was invited to defend that which, at first sight, nay, even at second\r\nand third sight, must always seem a defiance of the law more injurious\r\nthan crime itself. Every good man in this world has convictions about\r\nright and wrong. They are his soul's riches, his spiritual gold. When\r\nhis conduct is at variance with these, he knows that it is a departure,\r\na falling; and this is a simple and clear matter. If falling were all\r\nthat ever happened to a good man, all his days would be a simple matter\r\nof striving and repentance. But it is not all. There come to him certain\r\njunctures, crises, when life, like a highwayman, springs upon him,\r\ndemanding that he stand and deliver his convictions in the name of some\r\nrighteous cause, bidding him do evil that good may come. I cannot say\r\nthat I believe in doing evil that good may come. I do not. I think that\r\nany man who honestly justifies such course deceives himself. But this I\r\ncan say: to call any act evil, instantly begs the question. Many an act\r\nthat man does is right or wrong according to the time and place\r\nwhich form, so to speak, its context; strip it of its surrounding\r\ncircumstances, and you tear away its meaning. Gentlemen reformers,\r\nbeware of this common practice of yours! beware of calling an act evil\r\non Tuesday because that same act was evil on Monday!\r\n\r\nDo you fail to follow my meaning? Then here is an illustration. On\r\nMonday I walk over my neighbor's field; there is no wrong in such\r\nwalking. By Tuesday he has put up a sign that trespassers will\r\nbe prosecuted according to law. I walk again on Tuesday, and am a\r\nlaw-breaker. Do you begin to see my point? or are you inclined to object\r\nto the illustration because the walking on Tuesday was not WRONG, but\r\nmerely ILLEGAL? Then here is another illustration which you will find\r\nit a trifle more embarrassing to answer. Consider carefully, let me beg\r\nyou, the case of a young man and a young woman who walk out of a door\r\non Tuesday, pronounced man and wife by a third party inside the door.\r\nIt matters not that on Monday they were, in their own hearts, sacredly\r\nvowed to each other. If they had omitted stepping inside that door,\r\nif they had dispensed with that third party, and gone away on Monday\r\nsacredly vowed to each other in their own hearts, you would\r\nhave scarcely found their conduct moral. Consider these things\r\ncarefully,--the sign-post and the third party,--and the difference they\r\nmake. And now, for a finish, we will return to the sign-post.\r\n\r\nSuppose that I went over my neighbor's field on Tuesday, after the\r\nsign-post was put up, because I saw a murder about to be committed in\r\nthe field, and therefore ran in and stopped it. Was I doing evil that\r\ngood might come? Do you not think that to stay out and let the murder be\r\ndone would have been the evil act in this case? To disobey the sign-post\r\nwas RIGHT; and I trust that you now perceive the same act may wear as\r\nmany different hues of right or wrong as the rainbow, according to the\r\natmosphere in which it is done. It is not safe to say of any man, \xe2\x80\x9cHe\r\ndid evil that good might come.\xe2\x80\x9d Was the thing that he did, in the first\r\nplace, evil? That is the question.\r\n\r\nForgive my asking you to use your mind. It is a thing which no novelist\r\nshould expect of his reader, and we will go back at once to Judge Henry\r\nand his meditations about lynching.\r\n\r\nHe was well aware that if he was to touch at all upon this subject with\r\nthe New England girl, he could not put her off with mere platitudes and\r\nhumdrum formulas; not, at least, if he expected to do any good. She was\r\nfar too intelligent, and he was really anxious to do good. For her sake\r\nhe wanted the course of the girl's true love to run more smoothly, and\r\nstill more did he desire this for the sake of his Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI sent him myself on that business,\xe2\x80\x9d the Judge reflected uncomfortably.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am partly responsible for the lynching. It has brought him one great\r\nunhappiness already through the death of Steve. If it gets running\r\nin this girl's mind, she may--dear me!\xe2\x80\x9d the Judge broke off, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat a\r\nnuisance!\xe2\x80\x9d And he sighed. For as all men know, he also knew that many\r\nthings should be done in this world in silence, and that talking about\r\nthem is a mistake.\r\n\r\nBut when school was out, and the girl gone to her cabin, his mind had\r\nset the subject in order thoroughly, and he knocked at her door, ready,\r\nas he had put it, to sacrifice his character in the cause of true love.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, coming straight to the point, \xe2\x80\x9csome dark things have\r\nhappened.\xe2\x80\x9d And when she made no answer to this, he continued: \xe2\x80\x9cBut you\r\nmust not misunderstand us. We're too fond of you for that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJudge Henry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly Wood, also coming straight to the point, \xe2\x80\x9chave\r\nyou come to tell me that you think well of lynching?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe met her. \xe2\x80\x9cOf burning Southern negroes in public, no. Of hanging\r\nWyoming cattle thieves in private, yes. You perceive there's a\r\ndifference, don't you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot in principle,\xe2\x80\x9d said the girl, dry and short.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh--dear--me!\xe2\x80\x9d slowly exclaimed the Judge. \xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry that you cannot\r\nsee that, because I think that I can. And I think that you have just\r\nas much sense as I have.\xe2\x80\x9d The Judge made himself very grave and very\r\ngood-humored at the same time. The poor girl was strung to a high pitch,\r\nand spoke harshly in spite of herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the difference in principle?\xe2\x80\x9d she demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Judge, easy and thoughtful, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat do you mean by\r\nprinciple?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn't think you'd quibble,\xe2\x80\x9d flashed Molly. \xe2\x80\x9cI'm not a lawyer\r\nmyself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA man less wise than Judge Henry would have smiled at this, and then war\r\nwould have exploded hopelessly between them, and harm been added to what\r\nwas going wrong already.
