But the Judge knew that he must give to every\r\nword that the girl said now his perfect consideration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't mean to quibble,\xe2\x80\x9d he assured her. \xe2\x80\x9cI know the trick of escaping\r\nfrom one question by asking another. But I don't want to escape from\r\nanything you hold me to answer. If you can show me that I am wrong, I\r\nwant you to do so. But,\xe2\x80\x9d and here the Judge smiled, \xe2\x80\x9cI want you to play\r\nfair, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how am I not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want you to be just as willing to be put right by me as I am to be\r\nput right by you. And so when you use such a word as principle, you\r\nmust help me to answer by saying what principle you mean. For in all\r\nsincerity I see no likeness in principle whatever between burning\r\nSouthern negroes in public and hanging Wyoming horse-thieves in private.\r\nI consider the burning a proof that the South is semi-barbarous, and the\r\nhanging a proof that Wyoming is determined to become civilized. We\r\ndo not torture our criminals when we lynch them. We do not invite\r\nspectators to enjoy their death agony. We put no such hideous disgrace\r\nupon the United States. We execute our criminals by the swiftest means,\r\nand in the quietest way. Do you think the principle is the same?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMolly had listened to him with attention. \xe2\x80\x9cThe way is different,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nadmitted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly the way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it seems to me. Both defy law and order.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but do they both? Now we're getting near the principle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, yes. Ordinary citizens take the law in their own hands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe principle at last!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the Judge.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow tell me some more things. Out of whose hands do they take the law?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe court's.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat made the courts?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did there come to be any courts?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Constitution.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did there come to be any Constitution? Who made it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe delegates, I suppose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho made the delegates?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose they were elected, or appointed, or something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd who elected them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course the people elected them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCall them the ordinary citizens,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Judge. \xe2\x80\x9cI like your term.\r\nThey are where the law comes from, you see. For they chose the delegates\r\nwho made the Constitution that provided for the courts. There's your\r\nmachinery. These are the hands into which ordinary citizens have put the\r\nlaw. So you see, at best, when they lynch they only take back what they\r\nonce gave. Now we'll take your two cases that you say are the same in\r\nprinciple. I think that they are not. For in the South they take a negro\r\nfrom jail where he was waiting to be duly hung. The South has never\r\nclaimed that the law would let him go. But in Wyoming the law has been\r\nletting our cattle-thieves go for two years. We are in a very bad way,\r\nand we are trying to make that way a little better until civilization\r\ncan reach us. At present we lie beyond its pale. The courts, or rather\r\nthe juries, into whose hands we have put the law, are not dealing the\r\nlaw. They are withered hands, or rather they are imitation hands\r\nmade for show, with no life in them, no grip. They cannot hold a\r\ncattle-thief. And so when your ordinary citizen sees this, and sees that\r\nhe has placed justice in a dead hand, he must take justice back into his\r\nown hands where it was once at the beginning of all things. Call this\r\nprimitive, if you will. But so far from being a DEFIANCE of the law, it\r\nis an ASSERTION of it--the fundamental assertion of self governing men,\r\nupon whom our whole social fabric is based. There is your principle,\r\nMiss Wood, as I see it. Now can you help me to see anything different?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe could not.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut perhaps you are of the same opinion still?\xe2\x80\x9d the Judge inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is all terrible to me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; and so is capital punishment terrible. And so is war. And perhaps\r\nsome day we shall do without them. But they are none of them so terrible\r\nas unchecked theft and murder would be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter the Judge had departed on his way to Sunk Creek, no one spoke to\r\nMolly upon this subject. But her face did not grow cheerful at once. It\r\nwas plain from her fits of silence that her thoughts were not at rest.\r\nAnd sometimes at night she would stand in front of her lover's likeness,\r\ngazing upon it with both love and shrinking.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXXIV. TO FIT HER FINGER\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was two rings that the Virginian wrote for when next I heard from\r\nhim.\r\n\r\nAfter my dark sight of what the Cattle Land could be, I soon had\r\njourneyed home by way of Washakie and Rawlins. Steve and Shorty did not\r\nleave my memory, nor will they ever, I suppose.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian had touched the whole thing the day I left him. He had\r\nnoticed me looking a sort of farewell at the plains and mountains.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will come back to it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIf there was a headstone for every\r\nman that once pleasured in his freedom here, yu'd see one most every\r\ntime yu' turned your head. It's a heap sadder than a graveyard--but yu'\r\nlove it all the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSadness had passed from him--from his uppermost mood, at least, when\r\nhe wrote about the rings. Deep in him was sadness of course, as well as\r\njoy. For he had known Steve, and he had covered Shorty with earth. He\r\nhad looked upon life with a marksman's eyes, very close; and no one,\r\nif he have a heart, can pass through this and not carry sadness in his\r\nspirit with him forever. But he seldom shows it openly; it bides within\r\nhim, enriching his cheerfulness and rendering him of better service to\r\nhis fellow-men.\r\n\r\nIt was a commission of cheerfulness that he now gave, being distant\r\nfrom where rings are to be bought. He could not go so far as the East\r\nto procure what he had planned. Rings were to be had in Cheyenne, and a\r\nstill greater choice in Denver; and so far as either of these towns his\r\naffairs would have permitted him to travel. But he was set upon having\r\nrings from the East. They must come from the best place in the country;\r\nnothing short of that was good enough \xe2\x80\x9cto fit her finger,\xe2\x80\x9d as he said.\r\nThe wedding ring was a simple matter. Let it be right, that was all:\r\nthe purest gold that could be used, with her initials and his together\r\ngraven round the inside, with the day of the month and the year.\r\n\r\nThe date was now set. It had come so far as this. July third was to be\r\nthe day. Then for sixty days and nights he was to be a bridegroom, free\r\nfrom his duties at Sunk Creek, free to take his bride wheresoever she\r\nmight choose to go. And she had chosen.\r\n\r\nThose voices of the world had more than angered her; for after the anger\r\na set purpose was left. Her sister should have the chance neither to\r\ncome nor to stay away. Had her mother even answered the Virginian's\r\nletter, there could have been some relenting. But the poor lady had been\r\ninadequate in this, as in all other searching moments of her life: she\r\nhad sent messages,--kind ones, to be sure,--but only messages. If this\r\nhad hurt the Virginian, no one knew it in the world, least of all the\r\ngirl in whose heart it had left a cold, frozen spot. Not a good spirit\r\nin which to be married, you will say. No; frozen spots are not good at\r\nany time. But Molly's own nature gave her due punishment. Through all\r\nthese days of her warm happiness a chill current ran, like those which\r\ninterrupt the swimmer's perfect joy. The girl was only half as happy\r\nas her lover; but she hid this deep from him,--hid it until that final,\r\nfierce hour of reckoning that her nature had with her,--nay, was bound\r\nto have with her, before the punishment was lifted, and the frozen spot\r\nmelted at length from her heart.\r\n\r\nSo, meanwhile, she made her decree against Bennington. Not Vermont,\r\nbut Wyoming, should be her wedding place. No world's voices should be\r\nwhispering, no world's eyes should be looking on, when she made her vow\r\nto him and received his vow. Those voices should be spoken and that ring\r\nput on in this wild Cattle Land, where first she had seen him ride into\r\nthe flooded river, and lift her ashore upon his horse. It was this open\r\nsky which should shine down on them, and this frontier soil upon which\r\ntheir feet should tread. The world should take its turn second.\r\n\r\nAfter a month with him by stream and canyon, a month far deeper into the\r\nmountain wilds than ever yet he had been free to take her, a month with\r\nsometimes a tent and sometimes the stars above them, and only their\r\nhorses besides themselves--after such a month as this, she would take\r\nhim to her mother and to Bennington; and the old aunt over at Dunbarton\r\nwould look at him, and be once more able to declare that the Starks had\r\nalways preferred a man who was a man.\r\n\r\nAnd so July third was to be engraved inside the wedding ring. Upon the\r\nother ring the Virginian had spent much delicious meditation, all in his\r\nsecret mind. He had even got the right measure of her finger without her\r\nsuspecting the reason. But this step was the final one in his plan.\r\n\r\nDuring the time that his thoughts had begun to be busy over the other\r\nring, by a chance he had learned from Mrs. Henry a number of old fancies\r\nregarding precious stones. Mrs. Henry often accompanied the Judge in\r\nventuresome mountain climbs, and sometimes the steepness of the rocks\r\nrequired her to use her hands for safety. One day when the Virginian\r\nwent with them to help mark out certain boundary corners, she removed\r\nher rings lest they should get scratched; and he, being just behind her,\r\ntook them during the climb.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see you're looking at my topaz,\xe2\x80\x9d she had said, as he returned them.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I could have chosen, it would have been a ruby. But I was born in\r\nNovember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe did not understand her in the least, but her words awakened exceeding\r\ninterest in him; and they had descended some five miles of mountain\r\nbefore he spoke again. Then he became ingenious, for he had half worked\r\nout what Mrs. Henry's meaning must be; but he must make quite sure.\r\nTherefore, according to his wild, shy nature, he became ingenious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMen wear rings,\xe2\x80\x9d he began. \xe2\x80\x9cSome of the men on the ranch do. I don't\r\nsee any harm in a man's wearin' a ring. But I never have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lady, not yet suspecting that he was undertaking to\r\ncircumvent her, \xe2\x80\x9cprobably those men have sweethearts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, ma'am. Not sweethearts worth wearin' rings for--in two cases,\r\nanyway. They won 'em at cyards. And they like to see 'em shine. I never\r\nsaw a man wear a topaz.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Henry did not have any further remark to make.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was born in January myself,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued the Virginian, very\r\nthoughtfully.\r\n\r\nThen the lady gave him one look, and without further process of mind\r\nperceived exactly what he was driving at.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's very extravagant for rings,\xe2\x80\x9d said she. \xe2\x80\x9cJanuary is diamonds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDiamonds,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the Virginian, more and more thoughtfully. \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\r\nit don't matter, for I'd not wear a ring. And November is--what did yu'\r\nsay, ma'am?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTopaz.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Well, jewels are cert'nly pretty things. In the Spanish Missions\r\nyu'll see large ones now and again. And they're not glass, I think. And\r\nso they have got some jewel that kind of belongs to each month right\r\naround the twelve?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Henry, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cOne for each month. But the opal is\r\nwhat you want.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked at her, and began to blush.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOctober is the opal,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, and she laughed outright, for Miss\r\nWood's birthday was on the fifteenth of that month.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian smiled guiltily at her through his crimson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI've no doubt you can beat around the bush very well with men,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMrs. Henry. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it's perfectly transparent with us--in matters of\r\nsentiment, at least.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I am sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d he presently said. \xe2\x80\x9cI don't want to give her an\r\nopal. I have no superstition, but I don't want to give her an opal. If\r\nher mother did, or anybody like that, why, all right. But not from me.\r\nD' yu' understand, ma'am?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Henry did understand this subtle trait in the wild man, and she\r\nrejoiced to be able to give him immediate reassurance concerning opals.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't worry about that,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cThe opal is said to bring ill luck,\r\nbut not when it is your own month stone. Then it is supposed to be not\r\nonly deprived of evil influence, but to possess peculiarly fortunate\r\npower. Let it be an opal ring.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he asked her boldly various questions, and she showed him her\r\nrings, and gave him advice about the setting. There was no special\r\ncustom, she told him, ruling such rings as this he desired to bestow.\r\nThe gem might be the lady's favorite or the lover's favorite; and to\r\nchoose the lady's month stone was very well indeed.\r\n\r\nVery well indeed, the Virginian thought. But not quite well enough for\r\nhim. His mind now busied itself with this lore concerning jewels, and\r\nsoon his sentiment had suggested something which he forthwith carried\r\nout.\r\n\r\nWhen the ring was achieved, it was an opal, but set with four small\r\nembracing diamonds. Thus was her month stone joined with his, that their\r\nluck and their love might be inseparably clasped.\r\n\r\nHe found the size of her finger one day when winter had departed, and\r\nthe early grass was green. He made a ring of twisted grass for her,\r\nwhile she held her hand for him to bind it. He made another for himself.\r\nThen, after each had worn their grass ring for a while, he begged her\r\nto exchange. He did not send his token away from him, but most carefully\r\nmeasured it. Thus the ring fitted her well, and the lustrous flame\r\nwithin the opal thrilled his heart each time he saw it. For now June was\r\nnear its end; and that other plain gold ring, which, for safe keeping,\r\nhe cherished suspended round his neck day and night, seemed to burn with\r\nan inward glow that was deeper than the opal's.\r\n\r\nSo in due course arrived the second of July. Molly's punishment had got\r\nas far as this: she longed for her mother to be near her at this time;\r\nbut it was too late.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXXV. WITH MALICE AFORETHOUGHT\r\n\r\n\r\nTown lay twelve straight miles before the lover and his sweetheart, when\r\nthey came to the brow of the last long hill. All beneath them was like\r\na map: neither man nor beast distinguishable, but the veined and tinted\r\nimage of a country, knobs and flats set out in order clearly, shining\r\nextensive and motionless in the sun. It opened on the sight of the\r\nlovers as they reached the sudden edge of the tableland, where since\r\nmorning they had ridden with the head of neither horse ever in advance\r\nof the other.\r\n\r\nAt the view of their journey's end, the Virginian looked down at his\r\ngirl beside him, his eyes filled with a bridegroom's light, and, hanging\r\nsafe upon his breast, he could feel the gold ring that he would slowly\r\npress upon her finger to-morrow. He drew off the glove from her left\r\nhand, and stooping, kissed the jewel in that other ring which he had\r\ngiven her. The crimson fire in the opal seemed to mingle with that in\r\nhis heart, and his arm lifted her during a moment from the saddle as he\r\nheld her to him. But in her heart the love of him was troubled by that\r\ncold pang of loneliness which had crept upon her like a tide as the day\r\ndrew near. None of her own people were waiting in that distant town to\r\nsee her become his bride. Friendly faces she might pass on the way; but\r\nall of them new friends, made in this wild country: not a face of her\r\nchildhood would smile upon her; and deep within her, a voice cried for\r\nthe mother who was far away in Vermont. That she would see Mrs. Taylor's\r\nkind face at her wedding was no comfort now.\r\n\r\nThere lay the town in the splendor of Wyoming space. Around it spread\r\nthe watered fields, westward for a little way, eastward to a great\r\ndistance, making squares of green and yellow crops; and the town was but\r\na poor rag in the midst of this quilted harvest. After the fields to the\r\neast, the tawny plain began; and with one faint furrow of river lining\r\nits undulations, it stretched beyond sight. But west of the town rose\r\nthe Bow Leg Mountains, cool with their still unmelted snows and their\r\ndull blue gulfs of pine. From three canyons flowed three clear forks\r\nwhich began the river. Their confluence was above the town a good two\r\nmiles; it looked but a few paces from up here, while each side the river\r\nstraggled the margin cottonwoods, like thin borders along a garden walk.\r\nOver all this map hung silence like a harmony, tremendous yet serene.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow beautiful! how I love it!\xe2\x80\x9d whispered the girl. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, oh, how big it\r\nis!\xe2\x80\x9d And she leaned against her lover for an instant. It was her spirit\r\nseeking shelter. To-day, this vast beauty, this primal calm, had in it\r\nfor her something almost of dread. The small, comfortable, green hills\r\nof home rose before her. She closed her eyes and saw Vermont: a village\r\nstreet, and the post-office, and ivy covering an old front door, and her\r\nmother picking some yellow roses from a bush.\r\n\r\nAt a sound, her eyes quickly opened; and here was her lover turned in\r\nhis saddle, watching another horseman approach. She saw the Virginian's\r\nhand in a certain position, and knew that his pistol was ready. But the\r\nother merely overtook and passed them, as they stood at the brow of the\r\nhill.\r\n\r\nThe man had given one nod to the Virginian, and the Virginian one to\r\nhim; and now he was already below them on the descending road. To Molly\r\nWood he was a stranger; but she had seen his eyes when he nodded to her\r\nlover, and she knew, even without the pistol, that this was not enmity\r\nat first sight. It was not indeed. Five years of gathered hate had\r\nlooked out of the man's eyes. And she asked her lover who this was.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, easily, \xe2\x80\x9cjust a man I see now and then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs his name Trampas?\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly Wood.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian looked at her in surprise. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, where have you seen him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever till now. But I knew.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy gracious! Yu' never told me yu' had mind-reading powers.\xe2\x80\x9d And he\r\nsmiled serenely at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew it was Trampas as soon as I saw his eyes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy gracious!\xe2\x80\x9d her lover repeated with indulgent irony. \xe2\x80\x9cI must be\r\nmighty careful of my eyes when you're lookin' at 'em.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe he did that murder,\xe2\x80\x9d said the girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhose mind are yu' readin' now?\xe2\x80\x9d he drawled affectionately.\r\n\r\nBut he could not joke her off the subject. She took his strong hand in\r\nhers, tremulously, so much of it as her little hand could hold. \xe2\x80\x9cI know\r\nsomething about that--that--last autumn,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, shrinking from words\r\nmore definite. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I know that you only did--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat I had to,\xe2\x80\x9d he finished, very sadly, but sternly, too.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d she asserted, keeping hold of his hand. \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose\r\nthat--lynching--\xe2\x80\x9d (she almost whispered the word) \xe2\x80\x9cis the only way. But\r\nwhen they had to die just for stealing horses, it seems so wicked that\r\nthis murderer--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho can prove it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut don't you know it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know a heap o' things inside my heart. But that's not proving. There\r\nwas only the body, and the hoofprints--and what folks guessed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was never even arrested!\xe2\x80\x9d the girl said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. He helped elect the sheriff in that county.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen Molly ventured a step inside the border of her lover's reticence.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI saw--\xe2\x80\x9d she hesitated, \xe2\x80\x9cjust now, I saw what you did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe returned to his caressing irony. \xe2\x80\x9cYou'll have me plumb scared if you\r\nkeep on seein' things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had your pistol ready for him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I believe I did. It was mighty unnecessary.\xe2\x80\x9d And the Virginian\r\ntook out the pistol again, and shook his head over it, like one who has\r\nbeen caught in a blunder.\r\n\r\nShe looked at him, and knew that she must step outside his reticence\r\nagain. By love and her surrender to him their positions had been\r\nexchanged.\r\n\r\nHe was not now, as through his long courting he had been, her\r\nhalf-obeying, half-refractory worshipper. She was no longer his\r\nhalf-indulgent, half-scornful superior. Her better birth and schooling\r\nthat had once been weapons to keep him at his distance, or bring her off\r\nvictorious in their encounters, had given way before the onset of\r\nthe natural man himself. She knew her cow-boy lover, with all that he\r\nlacked, to be more than ever she could be, with all that she had. He was\r\nher worshipper still, but her master, too. Therefore now, against the\r\nbaffling smile he gave her, she felt powerless. And once again a pang of\r\nyearning for her mother to be near her to-day shot through the girl. She\r\nlooked from her untamed man to the untamed desert of Wyoming, and the\r\ntown where she was to take him as her wedded husband. But for his sake\r\nshe would not let him guess her loneliness.\r\n\r\nHe sat on his horse Monte, considering the pistol. Then he showed her a\r\nrattlesnake coiled by the roots of some sage-brush. \xe2\x80\x9cCan I hit it?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ninquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don't often miss them,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, striving to be cheerful.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I'm told getting married unstrings some men.\xe2\x80\x9d He aimed, and the\r\nsnake was shattered. \xe2\x80\x9cMaybe it's too early yet for the unstringing to\r\nbegin!\xe2\x80\x9d And with some deliberation he sent three more bullets into the\r\nsnake. \xe2\x80\x9cI reckon that's enough,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas not the first one?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, for the snake.\xe2\x80\x9d And then, with one leg crooked cow-boy fashion\r\nacross in front of his saddle-horn, he cleaned his pistol, and replaced\r\nthe empty cartridges.\r\n\r\nOnce more she ventured near the line of his reticence. \xe2\x80\x9cHas--has Trampas\r\nseen you much lately?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, no; not for a right smart while. But I reckon he has not missed\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Virginian spoke this in his gentlest voice. But his rebuffed\r\nsweetheart turned her face away, and from her eyes she brushed a tear.\r\n\r\nHe reined his horse Monte beside her, and upon her cheek she felt his\r\nkiss. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are not the only mind-reader,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, very tenderly. And\r\nat this she clung to him, and laid her head upon his breast. \xe2\x80\x9cI had been\r\nthinking,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the way our marriage is to be was the most\r\nbeautiful way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is the most beautiful,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured.\r\n\r\nHe slowly spoke out his thought, as if she had not said this. \xe2\x80\x9cNo folks\r\nto stare, no fuss, no jokes and ribbons and best bonnets, no public\r\neye nor talkin' of tongues when most yu' want to hear nothing and say\r\nnothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe answered by holding him closer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust the bishop of Wyoming to join us, and not even him after we're\r\nonce joined. I did think that would be ahead of all ways to get married\r\nI have seen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe paused again, and she made no rejoinder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we have left out your mother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked in his face with quick astonishment. It was as if his spirit\r\nhad heard the cry of her spirit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is nowhere near right,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe could never have come here,\xe2\x80\x9d said the girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe should have gone there. I don't know how I can ask her to forgive\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it was not you!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Molly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Because I did not object. I did not tell you we must go to her.\r\nI missed the point, thinking so much about my own feelings. For you\r\nsee--and I've never said this to you until now--your mother did hurt me.\r\nWhen you said you would have me after my years of waiting, and I wrote\r\nher that letter telling her all about myself, and how my family was not\r\nlike yours, and--and--all the rest I told her, why you see it hurt me\r\nnever to get a word back from her except just messages through you. For\r\nI had talked to her about my hopes and my failings. I had said more\r\nthan ever I've said to you, because she was your mother. I wanted her to\r\nforgive me, if she could, and feel that maybe I could take good care of\r\nyou after all. For it was bad enough to have her daughter quit her home\r\nto teach school out hyeh on Bear Creek. Bad enough without havin' me to\r\ncome along and make it worse. I have missed the point in thinking of my\r\nown feelings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it's not your doing!\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Molly.\r\n\r\nWith his deep delicacy he had put the whole matter as a hardship to her\r\nmother alone. He had saved her any pain of confession or denial. \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\r\nit is my doing,\xe2\x80\x9d he now said. \xe2\x80\x9cShall we give it up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive what--?\xe2\x80\x9d She did not understand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, the order we've got it fixed in. Plans are--well, they're no\r\nmore than plans. I hate the notion of changing, but I hate hurting your\r\nmother more. Or, anyway, I OUGHT to hate it more. So we can shift, if\r\nyu' say so. It's not too late.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShift?\xe2\x80\x9d she faltered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean, we can go to your home now. We can start by the stage to-night.\r\nYour mother can see us married. We can come back and finish in the\r\nmountains instead of beginning in them. It'll be just merely shifting,\r\nyu' see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe could scarcely bring himself to say this at all; yet he said it\r\nalmost as if he were urging it. It implied a renunciation that he could\r\nhardly bear to think of. To put off his wedding day, the bliss upon\r\nwhose threshold he stood after his three years of faithful battle\r\nfor it, and that wedding journey he had arranged: for there were the\r\nmountains in sight, the woods and canyons where he had planned to go\r\nwith her after the bishop had joined them; the solitudes where only the\r\nwild animals would be, besides themselves. His horses, his tent, his\r\nrifle, his rod, all were waiting ready in the town for their start\r\nto-morrow. He had provided many dainty things to make her comfortable.\r\nWell, he could wait a little more, having waited three years. It would\r\nnot be what his heart most desired: there would be the \xe2\x80\x9cpublic eye and\r\nthe talking of tongues\xe2\x80\x9d--but he could wait. The hour would come when he\r\ncould be alone with his bride at last. And so he spoke as if he urged\r\nit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried. \xe2\x80\x9cNever, never!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe pushed it from her. She would not brook such sacrifice on his part.\r\nWere they not going to her mother in four weeks? If her family had\r\nwarmly accepted him--but they had not; and in any case, it had gone too\r\nfar, it was too late. She told her lover that she would not hear him,\r\nthat if he said any more she would gallop into town separately from him.\r\nAnd for his sake she would hide deep from him this loneliness of hers,\r\nand the hurt that he had given her in refusing to share with her his\r\ntrouble with Trampas, when others must know of it.\r\n\r\nAccordingly, they descended the hill slowly together, lingering to spin\r\nout these last miles long. Many rides had taught their horses to go\r\nside by side, and so they went now: the girl sweet and thoughtful in her\r\nsedate gray habit; and the man in his leathern chaps and cartridge belt\r\nand flannel shirt, looking gravely into the distance with the level gaze\r\nof the frontier.\r\n\r\nHaving read his sweetheart's mind very plainly, the lover now broke his\r\ndearest custom. It was his code never to speak ill of any man to any\r\nwoman. Men's quarrels were not for women's ears. In his scheme, good\r\nwomen were to know only a fragment of men's lives. He had lived many\r\noutlaw years, and his wide knowledge of evil made innocence doubly\r\nprecious to him. But to-day he must depart from his code, having read\r\nher mind well. He would speak evil of one man to one woman, because his\r\nreticence had hurt her--and was she not far from her mother, and very\r\nlonely, do what he could? She should know the story of his quarrel in\r\nlanguage as light and casual as he could veil it with.\r\n\r\nHe made an oblique start. He did not say to her: \xe2\x80\x9cI'll tell you about\r\nthis. You saw me get ready for Trampas because I have been ready for him\r\nany time these five years.\xe2\x80\x9d He began far off from the point with that\r\nrooted caution of his--that caution which is shared by the primal savage\r\nand the perfected diplomat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere's cert'nly a right smart o' difference between men and women,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nobserved.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou're quite sure?\xe2\x80\x9d she retorted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAin't it fortunate?--that there's both, I mean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't know about fortunate. Machinery could probably do all the heavy\r\nwork for us without your help.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd who'd invent the machinery?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cWe shouldn't need the huge, noisy things you do. Our world\r\nwould be a gentle one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my gracious!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean by that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my gracious! Get along, Monte! A gentle world all full of ladies!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you call men gentle?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Molly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow it's a funny thing about that. Have yu' ever noticed a joke about\r\nfathers-in-law? There's just as many fathers--as mothers-in-law; but\r\nwhich side are your jokes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMolly was not vanquished. \xe2\x80\x9cThat's because the men write the comic\r\npapers,\xe2\x80\x9d said she.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear that, Monte? The men write 'em. Well, if the ladies wrote a comic\r\npaper, I expect that might be gentle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe gave up this battle in mirth; and he resumed:-- \xe2\x80\x9cBut don't you\r\nreally reckon it's uncommon to meet a father-in-law flouncin' around\r\nthe house? As for gentle--Once I had to sleep in a room next a ladies'\r\ntemperance meetin'. Oh, heavens! Well, I couldn't change my room, and\r\nthe hotel man, he apologized to me next mawnin'. Said it didn't surprise\r\nhim the husbands drank some.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere the Virginian broke down over his own fantastic inventions, and\r\ngave a joyous chuckle in company with his sweetheart. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, there's\r\na big heap o' difference between men and women,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cTake that\r\nfello' and myself, now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrampas?\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly, quickly serious. She looked along the road ahead,\r\nand discerned the figure of Trampas still visible on its way to town.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian did not wish her to be serious--more than could be helped.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, with a waving gesture at Trampas. \xe2\x80\x9cTake him and\r\nme. He don't think much o' me! How could he? And I expect he'll never.\r\nBut yu' saw just now how it was between us. We were not a bit like a\r\ntemperance meetin'.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe could not help laughing at the twist he gave to his voice. And she\r\nfelt happiness warming her; for in the Virginian's tone about Trampas\r\nwas something now that no longer excluded her. Thus he began his gradual\r\nrecital, in a cadence always easy, and more and more musical with the\r\nnative accent of the South. With the light turn he gave it, its pure\r\nugliness melted into charm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, he don't think anything of me. Once a man in the John Day Valley\r\ndidn't think much, and by Canada de Oro I met another. It will always be\r\nso here and there, but Trampas beats 'em all. For the others have always\r\nexpressed themselves--got shut of their poor opinion in the open air.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYu' see, I had to explain myself to Trampas a right smart while ago,\r\nlong before ever I laid my eyes on yu'. It was just nothing at all. A\r\nlittle matter of cyards in the days when I was apt to spend my money and\r\nmy holidays pretty headlong. My gracious, what nonsensical times I have\r\nhad! But I was apt to win at cyards, 'specially poker. And Trampas, he\r\nmet me one night, and I expect he must have thought I looked kind o'\r\nyoung. So he hated losin' his money to such a young-lookin' man, and he\r\ntook his way of sayin' as much. I had to explain myself to him plainly,\r\nso that he learned right away my age had got its growth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I expect he hated that worse, having to receive my explanation\r\nwith folks lookin' on at us publicly that-a-way, and him without further\r\nideas occurrin' to him at the moment. That's what started his poor\r\nopinion of me, not havin' ideas at the moment. And so the boys resumed\r\ntheir cyards.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'd most forgot about it. But Trampas's mem'ry is one of his strong\r\npoints. Next thing--oh, it's a good while later--he gets to losin' flesh\r\nbecause Judge Henry gave me charge of him and some other punchers taking\r\ncattle--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's not next,\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted the girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot? Why--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon't you remember?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, timid, yet eager. \xe2\x80\x9cDon't you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlamed if I do!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe first time we met?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; my mem'ry keeps that--like I keep this.\xe2\x80\x9d And he brought from his\r\npocket her own handkerchief, the token he had picked up at a river's\r\nbrink when he had carried her from an overturned stage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe did not exactly meet, then,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was at that dance. I\r\nhadn't seen you yet; but Trampas was saying something horrid about me,\r\nand you said--you said, 'Rise on your legs, you pole cat, and tell them\r\nyou're a liar.' When I heard that, I think--I think it finished me.\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\ncrimson suffused Molly's countenance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'd forgot,\xe2\x80\x9d the Virginian murmured. Then sharply, \xe2\x80\x9cHow did you hear\r\nit?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Taylor--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! Well, a man would never have told a woman that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMolly laughed triumphantly. \xe2\x80\x9cThen who told Mrs. Taylor?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBeing caught, he grinned at her. \xe2\x80\x9cI reckon husbands are a special kind\r\nof man,\xe2\x80\x9d was all that he found to say. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, since you do know about\r\nthat, it was the next move in the game. Trampas thought I had no call\r\nto stop him sayin' what he pleased about a woman who was nothin' to\r\nme--then. But all women ought to be somethin' to a man. So I had to give\r\nTrampas another explanation in the presence of folks lookin' on, and it\r\nwas just like the cyards. No ideas occurred to him again. And down goes\r\nhis opinion of me some more!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I have not been able to raise it. There has been this and that\r\nand the other,--yu' know most of the later doings yourself,--and to-day\r\nis the first time I've happened to see the man since the doings last\r\nautumn. Yu' seem to know about them, too. He knows I can't prove he\r\nwas with that gang of horse thieves. And I can't prove he killed poor\r\nShorty. But he knows I missed him awful close, and spoiled his thieving\r\nfor a while. So d' yu' wonder he don't think much of me? But if I had\r\nlived to be twenty-nine years old like I am, and with all my chances\r\nmade no enemy, I'd feel myself a failure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis story was finished. He had made her his confidant in matters he had\r\nnever spoken of before, and she was happy to be thus much nearer to him.\r\nIt diminished a certain fear that was mingled with her love of him.\r\n\r\nDuring the next several miles he was silent, and his silence was enough\r\nfor her. Vermont sank away from her thoughts, and Wyoming held less of\r\nloneliness. They descended altogether into the map which had stretched\r\nbelow them, so that it was a map no longer, but earth with growing\r\nthings, and prairie-dogs sitting upon it, and now and then a bird flying\r\nover it. And after a while she said to him, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you thinking\r\nabout?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been doing sums. Figured in hours it sounds right short. Figured\r\nin minutes it boils up into quite a mess. Twenty by sixty is twelve\r\nhundred. Put that into seconds, and yu' get seventy-two thousand\r\nseconds. Seventy-two thousand. Seventy-two thousand seconds yet before\r\nwe get married.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeconds! To think of its having come to seconds!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am thinkin' about it. I'm choppin' sixty of 'em off every minute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith such chopping time wears away. More miles of the road lay behind\r\nthem, and in the virgin wilderness the scars of new-scraped water\r\nditches began to appear, and the first wire fences. Next, they were\r\npassing cabins and occasional fields, the outposts of habitation. The\r\nfree road became wholly imprisoned, running between unbroken stretches\r\nof barbed wire. Far off to the eastward a flowing column of dust marked\r\nthe approaching stage, bringing the bishop, probably, for whose visit\r\nhere they had timed their wedding. The day still brimmed with heat and\r\nsunshine; but the great daily shadow was beginning to move from the feet\r\nof the Bow Leg Mountains outward toward the town. Presently they began\r\nto meet citizens. Some of these knew them and nodded, while some did\r\nnot, and stared. Turning a corner into the town's chief street, where\r\nstood the hotel, the bank, the drug store, the general store, and\r\nthe seven saloons, they were hailed heartily. Here were three\r\nfriends,--Honey Wiggin, Scipio Le Moyne, and Lin McLean,--all desirous\r\nof drinking the Virginian's health, if his lady--would she mind? The\r\nthree stood grinning, with their hats off; but behind their gayety the\r\nVirginian read some other purpose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe'll all be very good,\xe2\x80\x9d said Honey Wiggin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPretty good,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood,\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich is the honest man?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Molly, glad to see them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot one!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cMy old friends scare me when I think of\r\ntheir ways.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's bein' engaged scares yu',\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Mr. McLean. \xe2\x80\x9cMarriage restores\r\nyour courage, I find.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I'll trust all of you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Molly. \xe2\x80\x9cHe's going to take me to the\r\nhotel, and then you can drink his health as much as you please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a smile to them she turned to proceed, and he let his horse move\r\nwith hers; but he looked at his friends. Then Scipio's bleached blue\r\neyes narrowed to a slit, and he said what they had all come out on the\r\nstreet to say:-- \xe2\x80\x9cDon't change your clothes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d protested Molly, \xe2\x80\x9cisn't he rather dusty and countrified?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the Virginian had taken Scipio's meaning. \xe2\x80\x9cDON'T CHANGE YOUR\r\nCLOTHES.\xe2\x80\x9d Innocent Molly appreciated these words no more than the\r\naverage reader who reads a masterpiece, complacently unaware that\r\nits style differs from that of the morning paper. Such was Scipio's\r\nintention, wishing to spare her from alarm.\r\n\r\nSo at the hotel she let her lover go with a kiss, and without a thought\r\nof Trampas. She in her room unlocked the possessions which were there\r\nwaiting for her, and changed her dress.\r\n\r\nWedding garments, and other civilized apparel proper for a genuine\r\nfrontiersman when he comes to town, were also in the hotel, ready for\r\nthe Virginian to wear. It is only the somewhat green and unseasoned\r\ncow-puncher who struts before the public in spurs and deadly weapons.\r\nFor many a year the Virginian had put away these childish things. He\r\nmade a sober toilet for the streets. Nothing but his face and bearing\r\nremained out of the common when he was in a town. But Scipio had told\r\nhim not to change his clothes; therefore he went out with his pistol at\r\nhis hip. Soon he had joined his three friends.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'm obliged to yu',\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cHe passed me this mawnin'.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe don't know his intentions,\xe2\x80\x9d said Wiggin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcept that he's hangin' around,\xe2\x80\x9d said McLean.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd fillin' up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio, \xe2\x80\x9cwhich reminds me--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey strolled into the saloon of a friend, where, unfortunately, sat\r\nsome foolish people. But one cannot always tell how much of a fool a man\r\nis, at sight.\r\n\r\nIt was a temperate health-drinking that they made. \xe2\x80\x9cHere's how,\xe2\x80\x9d they\r\nmuttered softly to the Virginian; and \xe2\x80\x9cHow,\xe2\x80\x9d he returned softly, looking\r\naway from them. But they had a brief meeting of eyes, standing and\r\nlounging near each other, shyly; and Scipio shook hands with the\r\nbridegroom. \xe2\x80\x9cSome day,\xe2\x80\x9d he stated, tapping himself; for in his vagrant\r\nheart he began to envy the man who could bring himself to marry. And he\r\nnodded again, repeating, \xe2\x80\x9cHere's how.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey stood at the bar, full of sentiment, empty of words, memory\r\nand affection busy in their hearts. All of them had seen rough days\r\ntogether, and they felt guilty with emotion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's hot weather,\xe2\x80\x9d said Wiggin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHotter on Box Elder,\xe2\x80\x9d said McLean. \xe2\x80\x9cMy kid has started teething.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWords ran dry again. They shifted their positions, looked in their\r\nglasses, read the labels on the bottles. They dropped a word now and\r\nthen to the proprietor about his trade, and his ornaments.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood head,\xe2\x80\x9d commented McLean.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBig old ram,\xe2\x80\x9d assented the proprietor. \xe2\x80\x9cShot him myself on Gray Bull\r\nlast fall.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSheep was thick in the Tetons last fall,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian.\r\n\r\nOn the bar stood a machine into which the idle customer might drop his\r\nnickel. The coin then bounced among an arrangement of pegs, descending\r\nat length into one or another of various holes. You might win as much as\r\nten times your stake, but this was not the most usual result; and with\r\nnickels the three friends and the bridegroom now mildly sported for a\r\nwhile, buying them with silver when their store ran out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas it sheep you went after in the Tetons?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired the proprietor,\r\nknowing it was horse thieves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cI'll have ten more nickels.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you get all the sheep you wanted?\xe2\x80\x9d the proprietor continued.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor luck,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThink there's a friend of yours in town this afternoon,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nproprietor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid he mention he was my friend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe proprietor laughed. The Virginian watched another nickel click down\r\namong the pegs.\r\n\r\nHoney Wiggin now made the bridegroom a straight offer. \xe2\x80\x9cWe'll take this\r\nthing off your hands,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAny or all of us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lin.\r\n\r\nBut Scipio held his peace. His loyalty went every inch as far as theirs,\r\nbut his understanding of his friend went deeper. \xe2\x80\x9cDon't change your\r\nclothes,\xe2\x80\x9d was the first and the last help he would be likely to give in\r\nthis matter. The rest must be as such matters must always be, between\r\nman and man. To the other two friends, however, this seemed a very\r\nspecial case, falling outside established precedent. Therefore they\r\nventured offers of interference.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA man don't get married every day,\xe2\x80\x9d apologized McLean. \xe2\x80\x9cWe'll just run\r\nhim out of town for yu'.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSave yu' the trouble,\xe2\x80\x9d urged Wiggin. \xe2\x80\x9cSay the word.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe proprietor now added his voice. \xe2\x80\x9cIt'll sober him up to spend his\r\nnight out in the brush. He'll quit his talk then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the Virginian did not say the word, or any word. He stood playing\r\nwith the nickels.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThink of her,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered McLean.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho else would I be thinking of?\xe2\x80\x9d returned the Southerner. His face had\r\nbecome very sombre. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has been raised so different!\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured. He\r\npondered a little, while the others waited, solicitous.\r\n\r\nA new idea came to the proprietor. \xe2\x80\x9cI am acting mayor of this town,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said he. \xe2\x80\x9cI'll put him in the calaboose and keep him till you get\r\nmarried and away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay the word,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Honey Wiggin.\r\n\r\nScipio's eye met the proprietor's, and he shook his head about a quarter\r\nof an inch. The proprietor shook his to the same amount. They understood\r\neach other. It had come to that point where there was no way out, save\r\nonly the ancient, eternal way between man and man. It is only the great\r\nmediocrity that goes to law in these personal matters.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo he has talked about me some?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's the whiskey,\xe2\x80\x9d Scipio explained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI expect,\xe2\x80\x9d said McLean, \xe2\x80\x9che'd run a mile if he was in a state to\r\nappreciate his insinuations.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich we are careful not to mention to yu',\xe2\x80\x9d said Wiggin, \xe2\x80\x9cunless yu'\r\ninquire for 'em.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSome of the fools present had drawn closer to hear this interesting\r\nconversation. In gatherings of more than six there will generally be at\r\nleast one fool; and this company must have numbered twenty men.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis country knows well enough,\xe2\x80\x9d said one fool, who hungered to be\r\nimportant, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you don't brand no calves that ain't your own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe saturnine Virginian looked at him. \xe2\x80\x9cThank yu',\xe2\x80\x9d said he, gravely,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cfor your indorsement of my character.\xe2\x80\x9d The fool felt flattered. The\r\nVirginian turned to his friends. His hand slowly pushed his hat back,\r\nand he rubbed his black head in thought.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGlad to see yu've got your gun with you,\xe2\x80\x9d continued the happy fool.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know what Trampas claims about that affair of yours in the Tetons?\r\nHe claims that if everything was known about the killing of Shorty--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake one on the house,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested the proprietor to him, amiably. \xe2\x80\x9cYour\r\nnews will be fresher.\xe2\x80\x9d And he pushed him the bottle. The fool felt less\r\nimportant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis talk had went the rounds before it got to us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio, \xe2\x80\x9cor\r\nwe'd have headed it off. He has got friends in town.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPerplexity knotted the Virginian's brows. This community knew that a man\r\nhad implied he was a thief and a murderer; it also knew that he knew\r\nit. But the case was one of peculiar circumstances, assuredly. Could he\r\navoid meeting the man? Soon the stage would be starting south for the\r\nrailroad. He had already to-day proposed to his sweetheart that they\r\nshould take it. Could he for her sake leave unanswered a talking enemy\r\nupon the field? His own ears had not heard the enemy.\r\n\r\nInto these reflections the fool stepped once more. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course this\r\ncountry don't believe Trampas,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cThis country--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut he contributed no further thoughts. From somewhere in the rear of\r\nthe building, where it opened upon the tin cans and the hinder purlieus\r\nof the town, came a movement, and Trampas was among them, courageous\r\nwith whiskey.\r\n\r\nAll the fools now made themselves conspicuous. One lay on the floor,\r\nknocked there by the Virginian, whose arm he had attempted to hold.\r\nOthers struggled with Trampas, and his bullet smashed the ceiling\r\nbefore they could drag the pistol from him. \xe2\x80\x9cThere now! there now!\xe2\x80\x9d they\r\ninterposed; \xe2\x80\x9cyou don't want to talk like that,\xe2\x80\x9d for he was pouring out a\r\ntide of hate and vilification. Yet the Virginian stood quiet by the bar,\r\nand many an eye of astonishment was turned upon him. \xe2\x80\x9cI'd not stand half\r\nthat language,\xe2\x80\x9d some muttered to each other. Still the Virginian waited\r\nquietly, while the fools reasoned with Trampas. But no earthly foot can\r\nstep between a man and his destiny. Trampas broke suddenly free.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour friends have saved your life,\xe2\x80\x9d he rang out, with obscene epithets.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI'll give you till sundown to leave town.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was total silence instantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrampas,\xe2\x80\x9d spoke the Virginian, \xe2\x80\x9cI don't want trouble with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe never has wanted it,\xe2\x80\x9d Trampas sneered to the bystanders. \xe2\x80\x9cHe has\r\nbeen dodging it five years. But I've got him coralled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSome of the Trampas faction smiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrampas,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian again, \xe2\x80\x9care yu' sure yu' really mean\r\nthat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe whiskey bottle flew through the air, hurled by Trampas, and crashed\r\nthrough the saloon window behind the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was surplusage, Trampas,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cif yu' mean the other.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet out by sundown, that's all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Trampas. And wheeling, he went\r\nout of the saloon by the rear, as he had entered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, \xe2\x80\x9cI know you will all oblige me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the proprietor, heartily, \xe2\x80\x9cWe'll see that everybody\r\nlets this thing alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Virginian gave a general nod to the company, and walked out into the\r\nstreet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's a turruble shame,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Scipio, \xe2\x80\x9cthat he couldn't have postponed\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Virginian walked in the open air with thoughts disturbed. \xe2\x80\x9cI am of\r\ntwo minds about one thing,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself uneasily.\r\n\r\nGossip ran in advance of him; but as he came by, the talk fell away\r\nuntil he had passed. Then they looked after him, and their words again\r\nrose audibly. Thus everywhere a little eddy of silence accompanied his\r\nsteps.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt don't trouble him much,\xe2\x80\x9d one said, having read nothing in the\r\nVirginian's face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt may trouble his girl some,\xe2\x80\x9d said another.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe'll not know,\xe2\x80\x9d said a third, \xe2\x80\x9cuntil it's over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe'll not tell her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn't. It's no woman's business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe that's so. Well, it would have suited me to have Trampas die\r\nsooner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow would it suit you to have him live longer?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired a member of\r\nthe opposite faction, suspected of being himself a cattle thief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could answer your question, if I had other folks' calves I wanted to\r\nbrand.\xe2\x80\x9d This raised both a laugh and a silence.\r\n\r\nThus the town talked, filling in the time before sunset.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian, still walking aloof in the open air, paused at the edge\r\nof the town. \xe2\x80\x9cI'd sooner have a sickness than be undecided this way,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n he said, and he looked up and down. Then a grim smile came at his own\r\nexpense. \xe2\x80\x9cI reckon it would make me sick--but there's not time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOver there in the hotel sat his sweetheart alone, away from her mother,\r\nher friends, her home, waiting his return, knowing nothing. He looked\r\ninto the west. Between the sun and the bright ridges of the mountains\r\nwas still a space of sky; but the shadow from the mountains' feet had\r\ndrawn halfway toward the town. \xe2\x80\x9cAbout forty minutes more,\xe2\x80\x9d he said\r\naloud. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has been raised so different.\xe2\x80\x9d And he sighed as he\r\nturned back. As he went slowly, he did not know how great was his own\r\nunhappiness. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has been raised so different,\xe2\x80\x9d he said again.\r\n\r\nOpposite the post-office the bishop of Wyoming met him and greeted him.\r\nHis lonely heart throbbed at the warm, firm grasp of this friend's hand.\r\nThe bishop saw his eyes glow suddenly, as if tears were close. But none\r\ncame, and no word more open than, \xe2\x80\x9cI'm glad to see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut gossip had reached the bishop, and he was sorely troubled also.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is all this?\xe2\x80\x9d said he, coming straight to it.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian looked at the clergyman frankly. \xe2\x80\x9cYu' know just as much\r\nabout it as I do,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I'll tell yu' anything yu' ask.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you told Miss Wood?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired the bishop.\r\n\r\nThe eyes of the bridegroom fell, and the bishop's face grew at once more\r\nkeen and more troubled. Then the bridegroom raised his eyes again, and\r\nthe bishop almost loved him. He touched his arm, like a brother. \xe2\x80\x9cThis\r\nis hard luck,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nThe bridegroom could scarce keep his voice steady. \xe2\x80\x9cI want to do right\r\nto-day more than any day I have ever lived,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen go and tell her at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will just do nothing but scare her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo and tell her at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI expected you was going to tell me to run away from Trampas. I can't\r\ndo that, yu' know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe bishop did know. Never before in all his wilderness work had he\r\nfaced such a thing. He knew that Trampas was an evil in the country,\r\nand that the Virginian was a good. He knew that the cattle thieves--the\r\nrustlers--were gaining, in numbers and audacity; that they led many\r\nweak young fellows to ruin; that they elected their men to office, and\r\ncontrolled juries; that they were a staring menace to Wyoming. His heart\r\nwas with the Virginian. But there was his Gospel, that he preached, and\r\nbelieved, and tried to live. He stood looking at the ground and drawing\r\na finger along his eyebrow. He wished that he might have heard nothing\r\nabout all this. But he was not one to blink his responsibility as a\r\nChristian server of the church militant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAm I right,\xe2\x80\x9d he now slowly asked, \xe2\x80\x9cin believing that you think I am a\r\nsincere man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't believe anything about it. I know it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should run away from Trampas,\xe2\x80\x9d said the bishop.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat ain't quite fair, seh. We all understand you have got to do the\r\nthings you tell other folks to do. And you do them, seh. You never talk\r\nlike anything but a man, and you never set yourself above others. You\r\ncan saddle your own horses. And I saw yu' walk unarmed into that\r\nWhite River excitement when those two other parsons was a-foggin' and\r\na-fannin' for their own safety. Damn scoundrels!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe bishop instantly rebuked such language about brothers of his cloth,\r\neven though he disapproved both of them and their doctrines. \xe2\x80\x9cEvery one\r\nmay be an instrument of Providence,\xe2\x80\x9d he concluded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian, \xe2\x80\x9cif that is so, then Providence makes use of\r\ninstruments I'd not touch with a ten-foot pole. Now if you was me, seh,\r\nand not a bishop, would you run away from Trampas?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat's not quite fair, either!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the bishop, with a smile.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause you are asking me to take another man's convictions, and yet\r\nremain myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, seh. I am. That's so. That don't get at it. I reckon you and I\r\ncan't get at it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf the Bible,\xe2\x80\x9d said the bishop, \xe2\x80\x9cwhich I believe to be God's word, was\r\nanything to you--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is something to me, seh. I have found fine truths in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c'Thou shalt not kill,'\xe2\x80\x9d quoted the bishop. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is plain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Virginian took his turn at smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cMighty plain to me, seh. Make\r\nit plain to Trampas, and there'll be no killin'. We can't get at it that\r\nway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOnce more the bishop quoted earnestly. \xe2\x80\x9c'Vengeance is mine, I will\r\nrepay, saith the Lord.'\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow about instruments of Providence, seh? Why, we can't get at it that\r\nway. If you start usin' the Bible that way, it will mix you up mighty\r\nquick, seh.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy friend,\xe2\x80\x9d the bishop urged, and all his good, warm heart was in it,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cmy dear fellow--go away for the one night. He'll change his mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Virginian shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cHe cannot change his word, seh. Or at\r\nleast I must stay around till he does. Why, I have given him the say-so.\r\nHe's got the choice. Most men would not have took what I took from him\r\nin the saloon. Why don't you ask him to leave town?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe good bishop was at a standstill. Of all kicking against the pricks\r\nnone is so hard as this kick of a professing Christian against the whole\r\ninstinct of human man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you have helped me some,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cI will go and tell\r\nher. At least, if I think it will be good for her, I will tell her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe bishop thought that he saw one last chance to move him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou're twenty-nine,\xe2\x80\x9d he began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd a little over,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Virginian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you were fourteen when you ran away from your family.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I was weary, yu' know, of havin' elder brothers lay down my law\r\nnight and mawnin'.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know. So that your life has been your own for fifteen years. But\r\nit is not your own now. You have given it to a woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; I have given it to her. But my life's not the whole of me. I'd\r\ngive her twice my life--fifty--a thousand of 'em. But I can't give\r\nher--her nor anybody in heaven or earth--I can't give my--my--we'll\r\nnever get at it, seh! There's no good in words. Good-by.\xe2\x80\x9d The Virginian\r\nwrung the bishop's hand and left him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod bless him!\xe2\x80\x9d said the bishop. \xe2\x80\x9cGod bless him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Virginian unlocked the room in the hotel where he kept stored his\r\ntent, his blankets, his pack-saddles, and his many accoutrements for the\r\nbridal journey in the mountains. Out of the window he saw the mountains\r\nblue in shadow, but some cottonwoods distant in the flat between were\r\nstill bright green in the sun. From among his possessions he took\r\nquickly a pistol, wiping and loading it. Then from its holster he\r\nremoved the pistol which he had tried and made sure of in the morning.\r\nThis, according to his wont when going into a risk, he shoved between\r\nhis trousers and his shirt in front. The untried weapon he placed in\r\nthe holster, letting it hang visibly at his hip. He glanced out of\r\nthe window again, and saw the mountains of the same deep blue. But the\r\ncottonwoods were no longer in the sunlight. The shadow had come past\r\nthem, nearer the town; for fifteen of the forty minutes were gone. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nbishop is wrong,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is no sense in telling her.\xe2\x80\x9d And he\r\nturned to the door, just as she came to it herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried out at once, and rushed to him.\r\n\r\nHe swore as he held her close. \xe2\x80\x9cThe fools!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThe fools!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has been so frightful waiting for you,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, leaning her head\r\nagainst him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho had to tell you this?\xe2\x80\x9d he demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don't know. Somebody just came and said it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is mean luck,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured, patting her. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is mean luck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe went on: \xe2\x80\x9cI wanted to run out and find you; but I didn't! I didn't!\r\nI stayed quiet in my room till they said you had come back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is mean luck. Mighty mean,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow could you be so long?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked. \xe2\x80\x9cNever mind, I've got you now. It\r\nis over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnger and sorrow filled him. \xe2\x80\x9cI might have known some fool would tell\r\nyou,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt's all over. Never mind.\xe2\x80\x9d Her arms tightened their hold of him. Then\r\nshe let him go. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat shall we do?\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow?\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cNothing now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked at him without understanding.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it is a heap worse for you,\xe2\x80\x9d he pursued, speaking slowly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nknew it would be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it is over!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed again.\r\n\r\nHe did not understand her now. He kissed her. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you think it was\r\nover?\xe2\x80\x9d he said simply. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is some waiting still before us. I wish\r\nyou did not have to wait alone. But it will not be long.\xe2\x80\x9d He was looking\r\ndown, and did not see the happiness grow chilled upon her face, and then\r\nfade into bewildered fear. \xe2\x80\x9cI did my best,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on. \xe2\x80\x9cI think I did.\r\nI know I tried. I let him say to me before them all what no man has\r\never said, or ever will again. I kept thinking hard of you--with all\r\nmy might, or I reckon I'd have killed him right there. And I gave him a\r\nshow to change his mind. I gave it to him twice. I spoke as quiet as\r\nI am speaking to you now. But he stood to it. And I expect he knows he\r\nwent too far in the hearing of others to go back on his threat. He will\r\nhave to go on to the finish now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe finish?\xe2\x80\x9d she echoed, almost voiceless.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered very gently.\r\n\r\nHer dilated eyes were fixed upon him. \xe2\x80\x9cBut--\xe2\x80\x9d she could scarce form\r\nutterance, \xe2\x80\x9cbut you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have got myself ready,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you think--why, what did you\r\nthink?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe recoiled a step. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you going--\xe2\x80\x9d She put her two hands to her\r\nhead. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, God!\xe2\x80\x9d she almost shrieked, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are going--\xe2\x80\x9d He made a step,\r\nand would have put his arm round her, but she backed against the wall,\r\nstaring speechless at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not going to let him shoot me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said quietly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean--you mean--but you can come away!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried. \xe2\x80\x9cIt's not too\r\nlate yet. You can take yourself out of his reach. Everybody knows that\r\nyou are brave. What is he to you? You can leave him in this place. I'll\r\ngo with you anywhere. To any house, to the mountains, to anywhere away.\r\nWe'll leave this horrible place together and--and--oh, won't you listen\r\nto me?\xe2\x80\x9d She stretched her hands to him. \xe2\x80\x9cWon't you listen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe took her hands. \xe2\x80\x9cI must stay here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer hands clung to his. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, no. There's something else. There's\r\nsomething better than shedding blood in cold blood. Only think what it\r\nmeans! Only think of having to remember such a thing! Why, it's what\r\nthey hang people for! It's murder!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe dropped her hands. \xe2\x80\x9cDon't call it that name,\xe2\x80\x9d he said sternly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen there was the choice!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, half to herself, like a\r\nperson stunned and speaking to the air. \xe2\x80\x9cTo get ready for it when you\r\nhave the choice!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe did the choosing,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the Virginian. \xe2\x80\x9cListen to me. Are you\r\nlistening?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, for her gaze was dull.\r\n\r\nShe nodded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI work hyeh. I belong hyeh. It's my life. If folks came to think I was\r\na coward--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho would think you were a coward?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverybody. My friends would be sorry and ashamed, and my enemies would\r\nwalk around saying they had always said so. I could not hold up my head\r\nagain among enemies or friends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen it was explained--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere'd be nothing to explain. There'd just be the fact.\xe2\x80\x9d He was nearly\r\nangry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is a higher courage than fear of outside opinion,\xe2\x80\x9d said the New\r\nEngland girl.\r\n\r\nHer Southern lover looked at her. \xe2\x80\x9cCert'nly there is. That's what I'm\r\nshowing in going against yours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut if you know that you are brave, and if I know that you are brave,\r\noh, my dear, my dear! what difference does the world make? How much\r\nhigher courage to go your own course--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am goin' my own course,\xe2\x80\x9d he broke in. \xe2\x80\x9cCan't yu' see how it must be\r\nabout a man? It's not for their benefit, friends or enemies, that I have\r\ngot this thing to do. If any man happened to say I was a thief and I\r\nheard about it, would I let him go on spreadin' such a thing of me?\r\nDon't I owe my own honesty something better than that? Would I sit down\r\nin a corner rubbin' my honesty and whisperin' to it, 'There! there! I\r\nknow you ain't a thief?' No, seh; not a little bit! What men say about\r\nmy nature is not just merely an outside thing. For the fact that I\r\nlet 'em keep on sayin' it is a proof I don't value my nature enough to\r\nshield it from their slander and give them their punishment. And that's\r\nbeing a poor sort of a jay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had grown very white.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan't yu' see how it must be about a man?\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered, in a voice that scarcely seemed her own. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I\r\nought to, I cannot. To shed blood in cold blood. When I heard about that\r\nlast fall,--about the killing of those cattle thieves,--I kept saying to\r\nmyself: 'He had to do it. It was a public duty.' And lying sleepless I\r\ngot used to Wyoming being different from Vermont. But this--\xe2\x80\x9d she gave\r\na shudder--\xe2\x80\x9cwhen I think of to-morrow, of you and me, and of-- If you do\r\nthis, there can be no to-morrow for you and me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt these words he also turned white.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean--\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, and could go no farther.\r\n\r\nNor could she answer him, but turned her head away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis would be the end?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\nHer head faintly moved to signify yes.\r\n\r\nHe stood still, his hand shaking a little. \xe2\x80\x9cWill you look at me and\r\nsay that?\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured at length. She did not move. \xe2\x80\x9cCan you do it?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\nHis sweetness made her turn, but could not pierce her frozen resolve.\r\nShe gazed at him across the great distance of her despair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen it is really so?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nHer lips tried to form words, but failed.\r\n\r\nHe looked out of the window, and saw nothing but shadow. The blue of the\r\nmountains was now become a deep purple. Suddenly his hand closed hard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-by, then,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nAt that word she was at his feet, clutching him. \xe2\x80\x9cFor my sake,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nbegged him. \xe2\x80\x9cFor my sake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA tremble passed through his frame. She felt his legs shake as she held\r\nthem, and, looking up, she saw that his eyes were closed with misery.\r\nThen he opened them, and in their steady look she read her answer. He\r\nunclasped her hands from holding him, and raised her to her feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no right to kiss you any more,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. And then, before his\r\ndesire could break him down from this, he was gone, and she was alone.\r\n\r\nShe did not fall, or totter, but stood motionless. And next--it seemed\r\na moment and it seemed eternity--she heard in the distance a shot, and\r\nthen two shots. Out of the window she saw people beginning to run. At\r\nthat she turned and fled to her room, and flung herself face downward\r\nupon the floor.\r\n\r\nTrampas had departed into solitude from the saloon, leaving behind him\r\nhis ULTIMATUM. His loud and public threat was town knowledge already,\r\nwould very likely be county knowledge to-night. Riders would take it\r\nwith them to entertain distant cabins up the river and down the river;\r\nand by dark the stage would go south with the news of it--and the news\r\nof its outcome. For everything would be over by dark. After five years,\r\nhere was the end coming--coming before dark. Trampas had got up this\r\nmorning with no such thought. It seemed very strange to look back upon\r\nthe morning; it lay so distant, so irrevocable. And he thought of how he\r\nhad eaten his breakfast. How would he eat his supper? For supper would\r\ncome afterward. Some people were eating theirs now, with nothing like\r\nthis before them. His heart ached and grew cold to think of them, easy\r\nand comfortable with plates and cups of coffee.\r\n\r\nHe looked at the mountains, and saw the sun above their ridges, and\r\nthe shadow coming from their feet. And there close behind him was the\r\nmorning he could never go back to. He could see it clearly; his thoughts\r\nreached out like arms to touch it once more, and be in it again. The\r\nnight that was coming he could not see, and his eyes and his thoughts\r\nshrank from it. He had given his enemy until sundown. He could not\r\ntrace the path which had led him to this. He remembered their first\r\nmeeting--five years back, in Medicine Bow, and the words which at once\r\nbegan his hate. No, it was before any words; it was the encounter of\r\ntheir eyes. For out of the eyes of every stranger looks either a friend\r\nor an enemy, waiting to be known. But how had five years of hate come to\r\nplay him such a trick, suddenly, to-day? Since last autumn he had meant\r\nsometime to get even with this man who seemed to stand at every turn\r\nof his crookedness, and rob him of his spoils. But how had he come to\r\nchoose such a way of getting even as this, face to face? He knew many\r\nbetter ways; and now his own rash proclamation had trapped him. His\r\nwords were like doors shutting him in to perform his threat to the\r\nletter, with witnesses at hand to see that he did so.\r\n\r\nTrampas looked at the sun and the shadow again. He had till sundown. The\r\nheart inside him was turning it round in this opposite way: it was to\r\nHIMSELF that in his rage he had given this lessening margin of grace.\r\nBut he dared not leave town in all the world's sight after all the world\r\nhad heard him. Even his friends would fall from him after such an act.\r\nCould he--the thought actually came to him--could he strike before the\r\ntime set? But the thought was useless. Even if his friends could harbor\r\nhim after such a deed, his enemies would find him, and his life would be\r\nforfeit to a certainty. His own trap was closing upon him.\r\n\r\nHe came upon the main street, and saw some distance off the Virginian\r\nstanding in talk with the bishop. He slunk between two houses, and\r\ncursed both of them. The sight had been good for him, bringing some\r\nwarmth of rage back to his desperate heart. And he went into a place and\r\ndrank some whiskey.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn your shoes,\xe2\x80\x9d said the barkeeper, \xe2\x80\x9cI'd be afraid to take so much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the nerves of Trampas were almost beyond the reach of intoxication,\r\nand he swallowed some more, and went out again. Presently he fell in\r\nwith some of his brothers in cattle stealing, and walked along with them\r\nfor a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it will not be long now,\xe2\x80\x9d they said to him. And he had never\r\nheard words so desolate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he made out to say; \xe2\x80\x9csoon now.\xe2\x80\x9d Their cheerfulness seemed\r\nunearthly to him, and his heart almost broke beneath it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe'll have one to your success,\xe2\x80\x9d they suggested.\r\n\r\nSo with them he repaired to another place; and the sight of a man\r\nleaning against the bar made him start so that they noticed him. Then he\r\nsaw that the man was a stranger whom he had never laid eyes on till now.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt looked like Shorty,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, and could have bitten his tongue off.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShorty is quiet up in the Tetons,\xe2\x80\x9d said a friend. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don't want to be\r\nthinking about him. Here's how!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen they clapped him on the back and he left them. He thought of his\r\nenemy and his hate, beating his rage like a failing horse, and treading\r\nthe courage of his drink. Across a space he saw Wiggin, walking with\r\nMcLean and Scipio. They were watching the town to see that his friends\r\nmade no foul play.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe're giving you a clear field,\xe2\x80\x9d said Wiggin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis race will not be pulled,\xe2\x80\x9d said McLean.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe with you at the finish,\xe2\x80\x9d said Scipio.\r\n\r\nAnd they passed on. They did not seem like real people to him.\r\n\r\nTrampas looked at the walls and windows of the houses. Were they real?\r\nWas he here, walking in this street? Something had changed. He looked\r\neverywhere, and feeling it everywhere, wondered what this could be. Then\r\nhe knew: it was the sun that had gone entirely behind the mountains, and\r\nhe drew out his pistol.\r\n\r\nThe Virginian, for precaution, did not walk out of the front door of the\r\nhotel. He went through back ways, and paused once. Against his breast\r\nhe felt the wedding ring where he had it suspended by a chain from his\r\nneck. His hand went up to it, and he drew it out and looked at it. He\r\ntook it off the chain, and his arm went back to hurl it from him as far\r\nas he could. But he stopped and kissed it with one sob, and thrust it in\r\nhis pocket. Then he walked out into the open, watching. He saw men here\r\nand there, and they let him pass as before, without speaking. He saw\r\nhis three friends, and they said no word to him. But they turned and\r\nfollowed in his rear at a little distance, because it was known that\r\nShorty had been found shot from behind. The Virginian gained a position\r\nsoon where no one could come at him except from in front; and the sight\r\nof the mountains was almost more than he could endure, because it was\r\nthere that he had been going to-morrow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is quite a while after sunset,\xe2\x80\x9d he heard himself say.\r\n\r\nA wind seemed to blow his sleeve off his arm, and he replied to it, and\r\nsaw Trampas pitch forward. He saw Trampas raise his arm from the ground\r\nand fall again, and lie there this time, still. A little smoke was\r\nrising from the pistol on the ground, and he looked at his own, and saw\r\nthe smoke flowing upward out of it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI expect that's all,\xe2\x80\x9d he said aloud.\r\n\r\nBut as he came nearer Trampas, he covered him with his weapon. He\r\nstopped a moment, seeing the hand on the ground move. Two fingers\r\ntwitched, and then ceased; for it was all. The Virginian stood looking\r\ndown at Trampas.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBoth of mine hit,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, once more aloud. \xe2\x80\x9cHis must have gone mighty\r\nclose to my arm. I told her it would not be me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe had scarcely noticed that he was being surrounded and congratulated.\r\nHis hand was being shaken, and he saw it was Scipio in tears. Scipio's\r\njoy made his heart like lead within him.
